<a id="paper-2605.13691"></a>
### [Probing Quantum Information Scrambling via Local Randomized Measurements](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13691v1)

**Authors:** Yan-Ming Chen, Dan-Bo Zhang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13691v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum measurements` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **3/5** · `Rydberg arrays` **2/5** · `measurement-induced transitions` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13691_figures/2605.13691_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Convergence of AAI (χ2) via random Clifford sampling in the PXP model. Exact analytical evolution (solid black lines) is com- pared with statistical estimations for subsystem sizes (a) LA = 1 and (b) LA = 2. Estimations are obtained by sampling N ∈ {10, 50, 200} random unitaries. For LA = 1, samples are drawn uniformly from the 24-element Clifford group; for LA = 2, samples are generated via 50-depth random Clifford circuits. Colored lines denote mean values, with error bars indicating the standard deviation across 5 independent trials.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13691_figures/2605.13691_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Spatiotemporal resolution of information scrambling, χ2 versus Holevo χ. For each spatial site x and time t, the plotted color intensity represents the maximum value of the respective information metric evaluated over all subsystems of size LA = 2 that include the site x. The top row (a)-(d) displays the standard Holevo information χ, the middle row (e)-(h) displays the information from subentropy χQ, while the bottom row (i)-(l) shows our averaged accessible information, χ2. (a), (e),(i) confinement in MFIM; (b), (f), (j) ballistic coherent transport in TFIM; (c), (g), (k) persistent scar revivals in PXP; and (d), (h), (l) strict localization in MBL.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13691_figures/2605.13691_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Validation of the classical shadow protocol in non-ergodic regimes. The row (a)-(d) compare the exact evolution of the maximal χ2 (solid lines) with estimations from the classical shadow protocol (dotted lines with markers, Nshots = 3000) for various subsystem sizes LA ∈{1, 2, 3}. At each time step, the plotted maximum is evaluated over all   L LA  possible spatial combinations of the LA-site subsystem within the L = 10 chain. Each column corresponds to a distinct dynamical paradigm: (a) the PXP model exhibiting quantum many-body scars; (b) the many-body localized system; (c) the chaotic mixed-field Ising model; and (d) the integrable transverse-field Ising model.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13691_figures/2605.13691_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Dynamical confinement of information in the MBL phase. The solid green curve represents the dynamics within the full HMBL Hamiltonian system (L = 10), while the dashed red curve corre- sponds to an artificially isolated three-site cage (L = 3) sharing the same local disorder. The striking overlap of the high-amplitude os- cillations at early-to-medium times confirms that the effective l-bits are strictly localized, rendering the local dynamics oblivious to the extended thermodynamic bath.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new metric, Averaged Accessible Information, which can be efficiently extracted using randomized local measurements via the classical shadow protocol. It demonstrates that this metric can successfully distinguish between various many-body phenomena such as chaos, ballistic transport, quantum scars, and localization. This offers a pragmatic shift from needing optimal measurements to using simple, 'blind' randomized probes for studying quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a practical bridge between abstract information-theoretic bounds and experimental reality by showing that simple randomized local probes can capture complex, non-local scrambling dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an experimentally tractable method to probe quantum information scrambling using local randomized measurements, overcoming the difficulty of implementing optimal state-dependent measurements.

**Main result.** The authors derive the Averaged Accessible Information (AAI), a metric that depends solely on local purity, and prove it is a robust, strictly concave diagnostic for various many-body dynamical regimes.

**Method.** The framework utilizes the classical shadow protocol with single-qubit randomized Pauli measurements and employs Haar-random measurement theory and complex analysis to derive analytical expressions.

**Model / system.** The framework is validated across diverse many-body paradigms, including the Mixed-field Ising model (chaos), Transverse-field Ising model (ballistic transport), PXP model (quantum scars), and Many-body localization (MBL).

**Key observables.** Averaged Accessible Information (AAI, denoted as chi_2 or Q2), Holevo information, Subentropy, and local purity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local subsystem size (LA), number of random unitaries (N), and disorder strength (W) for MBL.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes Haar-random measurements and utilizes the annealed approximation (averaging the log is approximately the log of the average), which is valid in high-dimensional Hilbert spaces.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the numerical convergence of the Q2 metric with increasing sample size; Figure 2 provides a spatiotemporal comparison of different information metrics (chi, chi_Q, chi_2) across the four studied models.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of scrambling measurement, derives the analytical form of the AAI metric, proves its mathematical properties like concavity, demonstrates its extraction via classical shadows, and validates its utility across various many-body models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In quantum many-body dynamics, locally encoded information typically scrambles across the entire system, becoming inaccessible to local probes. The upper bound of accessible information of local probes can be characterized by the Holevo information via optimal measurement. In this work, we investigate the information dynamics of quantum scrambling utilizing local randomized probes, quantified by the averaged accessible information (AAI). We derive an analytical expression for the AAI under Haar-random measurements and demonstrate that it is a function of purity of local reduced density matrix. Operationally, we employ the classical shadow protocol, using only single-qubit randomized Pauli measurements, to efficiently extract the AAI across extended subsystems. Through numerical simulations across diverse many-body paradigms, we show that the AAI can reveal distinct scrambling behaviors, resolving phenomena that range from dynamical confinement and ballistic transport to persistent scar revivals and many-body localization. This work highlights a pragmatic paradigm shift--from relying on optimal measurements to utilizing randomized local probes--for the characterization of complex quantum information dynamics.

</details>

**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

Combined spectrograms of the motion z1 and z2 with frequencies Ω1/2π ≈27 kHz and Ω2/2π ≈33 kHz reveal interactions at different resonances: a) reciprocal beamsplit- ter at ∆ω/2π ≈6 kHz, b) reciprocal two-mode squeezing at ∆ω/2π ≈60 kHz, c) anti-reciprocal two-mode squeezing at ∆ω/2π ≈6 kHz, d) anti-reciprocal beamsplitter interaction at ∆ω/2π ≈60 kHz. Single-mode squeezing is observed in b) and d) at ∆ω ≈2Ω1,2. The black lines are fits based on the theoretical dependence of the normal mode frequencies.</figcaption>

The lines are a fit to the theory. The error bars are a combination of errors of the distance, deviations of ∆ω from ∆Ω, and the fit errors. b) The reconstructed phase space of complex eigenfrequencies shows continuous tuning of both the real and imaginary parts that traces a circle around the origin, as predicted by theory (gray line).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The researchers use Floquet engineering to control light-induced interactions between trapped nanoparticles, enabling the creation of programmable quantum operations like squeezing and beamsplitters. This work establishes a platform for exploring non-reciprocal dynamics and non-Hermitian many-body physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides an experimental toolbox for engineering non-reciprocal and non-Hermitian interactions, which is highly relevant for studying exceptional points, reservoir engineering, and topological phases in open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving programmable, time-dependent control over light-induced dipolar interactions between nanoparticles to realize fundamental quantum optical operations like beamsplitters and squeezing.

**Main result.** Demonstrated full control over beamsplitter, single-mode, and two-mode squeezing operations, as well as the realization of non-reciprocal dynamics and negative-mass-like oscillator signatures.

**Method.** Floquet engineering via optical detuning of acousto-optic driven tweezers, combined with heterodyne detection and Hilbert transform-based reconstruction of eigenmodes.

**Model / system.** Two silica nanoparticles trapped in separate optical tweezers within a vacuum chamber, interacting via light-induced dipolar forces (optical binding) modulated by optical frequency detuning.

**Key observables.** Power spectral densities (PSD), particle positions and momenta, mechanical sidebands, quadrature variances, and first/second-order correlation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Optical detuning (delta omega), interparticle distance (kd), mechanical frequencies (Omega), coupling rate (g), and damping rate (gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Rotating-wave approximation (RWA) for resonant interactions, high-Q oscillator assumption, and Gaussian statistics for thermal noise.

**Figures summary.** Spectrograms of particle positions and mode eigenfrequencies; plots of coherent energy exchange and squeezing magnitudes; and phase-space plots illustrating single-mode squeezing and bistability.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of programmable interactions, describes the experimental setup with AOD-driven tweezers, derives the time-dependent Floquet-driven Hamiltonian, demonstrates various squeezing and beamsplitter regimes, and concludes with potential applications in non-Hermitian physics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Tweezer arrays of polarizable objects are a promising platform for assembling quantum matter and building next-generation quantum sensors. Light-induced dipolar interactions have emerged as a method to couple their motion, thereby establishing a new paradigm for controlling collective mechanical degrees of freedom. Here, we extend these into the regime of Floquet-driven interactions, combined with the intrinsic nonreciprocity of optical forces. We demonstrate beamsplitter, single-, and two-mode squeezing operations, as well as signatures of a negative-mass-like oscillator arising from the nonreciprocity. Moreover, we show that a programmable combination of these operations enables continuous tuning of complex eigenfrequencies. These results establish a toolbox of quantum operations of nonreciprocal interactions that are essential for investigating non-Hermitian many-body physics and collective quantum optomechanics.

</details>

For comparison a sequence using conventional initialization where the system is read out using the same laser power as it is initialized with is shown in blue. Counts are obtained in 280 ns readout win- dows. Solid lines are sinc2-ﬁts to the data. (b) Depiction of the pulse sequence and pulse duration used for high-speed two-power initialization. (c) Contrast obtained from an opti- mized pulse sequence where the readout pulse is immediately followed by a short (150 ns) high intensity pulse PH = 21 mW allowing for rapid charge state...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper presents a new method to initialize NV centers in diamond using a two-step laser pulse sequence. By using a high-power pulse to clean the charge state and a low-power pulse to polarize the spin, the researchers significantly improve measurement contrast and speed. This technique is directly applicable to existing quantum sensing and magnetometry platforms.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates how to manipulate dissipative-like charge-state dynamics (ionization/re-capture) to actively engineer a more favorable initial quantum state for sensing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Improving the sensitivity of nitrogen-vacancy (NV) center spin measurements by overcoming the trade-off between charge state purity and spin polarization during initialization.

**Main result.** A two-step 'two-power' initialization protocol increases readout contrast by 17%, reduces initialization error by over 50%, and enables a measurement speedup of over 1.5x for long sequences.

**Method.** The authors use a two-step laser pulse sequence: a high-power pulse to purify the charge state via ionization/re-capture, followed by a low-power pulse to achieve high spin polarization.

**Model / system.** The system consists of NV- and NV0+ charge states in diamond, modeled using an effective rate equation system involving an 8-level structure to account for spin, charge, and metastable dynamics.

**Key observables.** Readout contrast, photon collection rate (brightness), spin polarization, and charge state purity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Laser powers (high-power P_H, low-power P_L, and readout P_R), pulse durations, and measurement sequence duration.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a specific set of decay rates and excitation cross-sections; it also assumes the high-power pulse must be switched off faster than the metastable state lifetime.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the level diagram and population distribution under different power regimes; Fig 2 shows fluorescence quenching and power dependence; Fig 4-5 show ODMR contrast improvements and pulse sequence schematics; Fig 8 shows predicted sensitivity and speedup; Fig 9 shows background reduction; Fig 10 shows laser stability analysis.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of initialization trade-offs, presents a theoretical model of the electronic levels and dynamics, describes the experimental two-power protocol, demonstrates the improvement in contrast and speed, and concludes with a sensitivity analysis and scalability discussion.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose and experimentally demonstrate a method to strongly increase the sensitivity of spin measurements on nitrogen-vacancy (NV) centers in diamond, which can be readily implemented in existing quantum sensing experiments. While charge state transitions of this defect are generally considered a parasitic effect to be avoided, we show here that these can be used to significantly increase the NV center's spin contrast, a key quantity for high sensitivity magnetometry and high fidelity state readout. The protocol consists of a two-step procedure, in which the charge state of the defect is first purified by a strong laser pulse, followed by weak illumination to obtain high spin polarization. We observe a relative improvement of the readout contrast by 17 %, and infer a reduction of the initialization error of more than 50 %. The contrast enhancement is accompanied by a beneficial increase of the readout signal. For long sequence durations, typically encountered in high-resolution magnetometry, a measurement speedup by a factor of >1.5 is extracted, and we find that the technique is beneficial for sequences of any duration. Additionally, our findings give detailed insight into the charge and spin polarization dynamics of the NV center, and provide actionable insights for direct optical, spin-to-charge, and electrical readout of solid-state spin centres.

</details>

### [Purification of a monitored qubit: exact path-integral solution](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12783v1)

**Authors:** Matheus M. R. Poltronieri Martins, Henrique Santos Lima  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12783v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<img src="2605.12783_figures/2605.12783_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Collisional implementation of continuous monitoring. A system qubit S interacts during a short time interval dt with one ancillary probe prepared in the state |ϕ⟩through the weak unitary U = ZS(θ)UCNOT(θ). The ancilla is then projectively measured and discarded, so that each collision extracts only partial information about the system. In the scaling limit θ = √ηdt ≪1, the sequence of weak updates converges to a Markovian conditioned evolution, yielding the stochastic master equation used throughout the paper.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12783_figures/2605.12783_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Left: solutions of the extremal condition Ω/η = tanh(Ωt), Eq. (13), plotted as a function of (ηt)−1. The central minimum at Ω= 0 becomes a local maximum and is replaced by two symmetric minima, providing the origin of the bimodal purification distribution.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

**Summary.** This paper provides an exact analytical solution for the purification of a single qubit under continuous monitoring. By using a path-integral approach, the authors demonstrate how a qubit transitions from a mixed state to a bimodal distribution of pure states. This work serves as a robust theoretical benchmark for studying information extraction in monitored quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides an exact analytical benchmark for understanding how continuous measurement extracts information and drives purification, which is fundamental to the study of open quantum systems and measurement-induced dynamics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the purification dynamics of a single qubit under continuous-in-time monitoring, specifically seeking an exact analytical solution for the full probability distribution of the qubit's trajectories.

**Main result.** The authors derive an exact closed-form solution for the probability distribution of the qubit's state and purity using the Onsager-Machlup path-integral formalism, revealing a dynamical crossover from a diffusion-dominated regime to a measurement-dominated regime characterized by bimodality.

**Method.** The study employs a collisional model framework, maps the stochastic master equation to a multiplicative Langevin equation, and uses the Onsager-Machlup path-integral approach and Girsanov's theorem to solve the dynamics.

**Model / system.** A single qubit initially in a mixed state interacting sequentially with a stream of ancillary probe qubits in a collisional model, described by a stochastic master equation in the continuous monitoring limit.

**Key observables.** State coordinate (q), purity (tau), effective purification rate (Omega), and the probability distributions of these quantities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Measurement rate (eta), time (t), and the crossover threshold (eta*t = 1).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is limited to a single qubit, meaning it cannot exhibit many-body measurement-induced phase transitions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the collisional model schematic; Figure 2 displays individual qubit trajectories; Figure 3 illustrates the transition of the action landscape from one to two minima; Figure 4 compares analytical and numerical results for the effective-rate distribution and mean purity.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the collisional model and stochastic master equation, applies a mathematical transformation to solve the Langevin equation via path integrals, validates the analytical results against numerical simulations, and discusses the physical implications of the observed dynamical crossover.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the purification dynamics of a single qubit under continuous in time monitoring. By employing a collisional model framework where the system interacts sequentially with ancillary qubits, we describe the conditioned evolution of the density matrix through a stochastic master equation. We show that for initial mixed states, the dynamics reduce to a multiplicative Langevin equation for a single scalar parameter representing the state's purity. This stochastic process is solved exactly using the Onsager-Machlup path integral formalism, allowing us to derive the full probability distribution for the qubit's trajectories. Our analytical results reveal that purification is characterized by a dynamical crossover from a diffusion dominated regime to a measurement dominated regime, visible in the emergence of a bimodal state distribution. The analytical solutions are in strong agreement with numerical simulations, providing a robust theoretical benchmark for the study of information extraction in monitored quantum systems.

</details>

### [Quantum selective measurement as a quasilinear evolution](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13739v1)

**Authors:** Jakub Rembieliński, Karol Ławniczak

**Summary.** The paper proposes replacing the discontinuous 'jump' of quantum measurement with a continuous, nonlinear, but quasilinear evolution. This new framework maintains the no-signaling principle and recovers the standard results of von Neumann measurements. It provides a more physically intuitive description of how a quantum state evolves into an eigenstate during a measurement process.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics as it provides a continuous-time dynamical alternative to the projection postulate, offering a more transparent description of the measurement process and its impact on entanglement.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to resolve the conceptual ambiguity of the instantaneous wavefunction collapse in von Neumann measurements by replacing it with a continuous, nonlinear, yet quasilinear dynamical evolution.

**Main result.** The proposed quasilinear evolution successfully reproduces all essential properties of von Neumann projections, including state purification, independence from the initial state, and consistent action on entangled states without violating the no-signaling principle.

**Method.** The authors derive a nonlinear generalization of the GKSL master equation and use a Kraus-like time-dependent form to solve for the evolution of the density operator, analyzing the dynamics via the Bloch vector and Riccati equations.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on a general two-level quantum system (qubit) and entangled two-qubit systems, governed by a quasilinear evolution equation with a time-dependent generator G(t) and a time-independent observable Omega.

**Key observables.** The final state (eigenstate of the observable Omega), Bloch vector trajectories, and the purity of the quantum state.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The magnitude and direction of the observable (omega), the driving generator (g(t)), the angle (Theta) between omega and g, and specific potential profiles like the Inverted Morse potential.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The direction of the vector g(t) is assumed to be time-independent, and the magnitude of the measurement influence is zero before and after the measurement interval.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Inverted Morse and constant potential profiles for the measurement strength; Figure 2 compares Bloch vector evolution trajectories; Figure 5 illustrates the dynamics of subsystems A and B in an entangled two-qubit system.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of instantaneous collapse, defines the mathematical framework of quasilinear evolution, constructs a dynamical model for qubits using specific potential profiles, tests the model's robustness and entanglement compatibility, and identifies regions of structural instability.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose replacing the instantaneous state reduction in von Neumann selective measurement with continuous nonlinear evolution. Despite its nonlinearity, this evolution preserves the equivalence of quantum ensembles and hence obeys the no-signaling principle. Its final states coincide with those produced by the von Neumann projection. The defining features of rank-one projective measurement are retained: convergence to the eigenstate of the observable associated with the selected outcome, independence of this final state from the initial state, and consistent action on entangled states.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a method to recover pure multi-qubit states transferred through a noisy spin chain by using an enlarged receiver and ancilla measurements. It demonstrates that while environmental interaction introduces mixedness, the protocol can effectively restore the original state's structure. The method is shown to be robust against perturbations and can even outperform standard homogeneous chains when using engineered high-fidelity chains.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum networks as it provides a practical protocol for error mitigation and state recovery in noisy communication channels using local measurements.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving high-fidelity transfer of multi-qubit k-excitation states along a spin chain while mitigating the degradation caused by environmental decoherence.

**Main result.** The authors develop a measurement-based algorithm using an extended receiver and ancilla measurement to restore the transferred state, achieving probabilistic perfect state transfer as environmental interaction vanishes.

**Method.** The approach uses Kraus operators and a unitary transformation at an extended receiver, combined with an ancilla-based measurement strategy to remove 'garbage' terms from the density matrix.

**Model / system.** A spin-1/2 chain with XX-type dipole-dipole interaction and an external magnetic field, evolving under a Lindblad master equation that preserves the excitation number.

**Key observables.** The lambda-parameter (state fidelity/proportionality), the nu-parameter (contribution of the maximally mixed state), and the success probability p.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimensionless interaction strength (Gamma/D12), dimensionless time (t*D12), and the number of nodes in the chain (N).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The excitation number is conserved during spin evolution, and the state transfer is inherently probabilistic due to the measurement-based removal of error terms.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the communication line setup (Sender, TL, Receiver); Figure 2 depicts the quantum circuit for state transfer and restoration; Figure 3 displays the evolution of the lambda-parameter for various chain lengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of state transfer in noisy chains, defines the physical model and Lindblad dynamics, presents the state-restoring algorithm using Kraus operators, provides a dimensionality analysis for the extended receiver, and concludes with numerical simulations and robustness tests.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider the multi-qubit fixed-excitation state transfer along the spin chain with dipole-dipole interaction subjected to the interaction with environment governed by the Lindblad equation preserving the excitation number during spin-evolution. The state transfer algorithm includes the state restoring via Kraus operators and ancilla measurement. As a result, the transferred state appears in superposition with completely mixed state, the latter disappears with vanishing interaction with environment. In that case we deal with probabilistic perfect state transfer. Example of an arbitrary multi-qubit one-excitation state transfer is present and its robustness with respect to perturbation of the Kraus operators is studied.

</details>

### [Controllable Quantum Memory Capacity in Quantum Reservoir Networks with Tunable partial-SWAPs](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12713v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new way to control how much information a quantum reservoir retains by using a tunable partial-SWAP gate. This mechanism allows researchers to tune the 'memory' of the system, helping to optimize quantum neural networks for time-series prediction tasks on noisy hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper provides a hardware-realizable method to implement controlled dissipation and purification in a quantum system, which is highly relevant to the study of open quantum systems and the management of noise in NISQ-era devices.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing recurrent Quantum Reservoir Computing (QRC) architectures lack a controllable hyperparameter to manage memory dissipation and the 'fading memory' effect.

**Main result.** The authors introduce a tunable partial-SWAP mechanism that acts as a controlled amplitude-damping channel, allowing for precise control over memory capacity and providing a purification effect that aids NISQ-era performance.

**Method.** The researchers implement a two-register recurrent QRN using a tunable partial-SWAP gate and validate it through STMC and NARMA-5 benchmarks using both Aer simulations and IBM QPUs.

**Model / system.** A recurrent Quantum Reservoir Network (QRN) consisting of a memory register and a readout register, implemented on gate-based IBM QPUs and classical simulators.

**Key observables.** Short-term memory capacity (STMC) recall, NARMA-5 Root Mean Square Error (RMSE), and the coefficient of determination (R2) for various time delays.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Partial-SWAP strength (gamma), number of qubits, number of reuploading blocks (n_repeats), and the damping probability (p).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The readout qubit is reset to the |0> state at every time-step, and the memory and readout registers are assumed to be of equal size for scalability.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 details the QRN architecture; Figure 2 shows circuit implementations of the partial-SWAP; Figures 3-5 show ablation studies on RMSE vs. gamma, qubits, and nonlinearity; Figure 6 compares simulator vs. hardware performance; Figure 7 compares QRN to classical ESN.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QRC architectural problem, proposes the partial-SWAP mechanism, provides a mathematical derivation of the induced amplitude-damping channel, presents experimental validation on simulators and IBM hardware, and concludes with ablation studies and comparisons to classical models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the field of quantum reservoir computing (QRC), many different computational models and architectures have been proposed. From these models, we identify feedback based models -- which use a feedback mechanism to re-embed classical measurements from the QRC -- and recurrent models -- which use a multi-register approach with memory and readout qubits -- as the two major competing architectures that have been discussed and validated on hardware. In this paper, we advance upon the recurrent architectures, which employ a two register approach to endow the QRC with a fading memory. While these approaches have been validated on hardware and have demonstrated great real-world performance on noisy-intermediate-scale-quantum (NISQ) quantum processing units (QPUs), the exact mechanism through which the memory capacity arises is not completely understood or fully controllable. With this, we augment the recurrent approaches and present a hardware-realizable mechanism, which we call a tunable partial-SWAP, that allows for the direct control of the rate of memory dissipation from a QRN implemented on a gate-based QPU. The theory behind this mechanism is discussed in terms of a controlled amplitude-damping channel and validation experiments using a randomized short-term memory capacity (STMC) recall benchmark and the NARMA-5 dataset are conducted using simulation and IBM QPUs, respectively.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13563"></a>
### [Probing Floquet topological phases via non-Hermitian skin effect of reflected waves](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13563v1)

**Authors:** Fangqiao Ye, Haiping Hu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13563v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

(a),(c),(e) GH shifts evaluated for an incident energy ϵ0 = π for the trivial phase (J = 0.5π/τ), the Floquet Chern insulator phase (J = 1.5π/τ), and the anomalous Floquet phase (J = 2.5π/τ), respectively. (b),(d),(f) Corresponding GH shifts for the same three phases, but with the incident energy ϵ0 = 0. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the topological properties of Floquet insulators can be probed via the scattering of waves. It shows that the non-Hermitian skin effect in reflected waves and the resulting Goos-Hänchen shift directly reveal the bulk Floquet invariants. This offers a practical real-space method to identify complex topological phases like the anomalous Floquet phase.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel real-space, boundary-based diagnostic for non-equilibrium topology, which is highly relevant for characterizing driven systems in AMO and quantum optics without needing full momentum-space tomography.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** How to diagnose the bulk topological properties of periodically driven (Floquet) systems, particularly the anomalous Floquet phase, using a real-space scattering approach.

**Main result.** The authors establish that the non-Hermitian skin effect (NHSE) of reflected waves and the momentum-integrated Goos-Hänchen (GH) shift can quantitatively recover the bulk Floquet topological invariant.

**Method.** A discrete-time scattering formalism is used to derive the reflection matrix and analyze its spectral properties and phase winding.

**Model / system.** A 2D Floquet Chern insulator modeled as a multi-step driving lattice on a bipartite square lattice, coupled to two semi-infinite, topologically trivial leads.

**Key observables.** Non-Hermitian winding number of the reflection matrix, Goos-Hänchen (GH) shift, and the Floquet topological invariant (W3).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Driving period (tau), driving strength (J), quasienergy gaps (0 and pi), and the wide-band limit for leads.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system operates in the wide-band limit (energy-independent density of states in leads) and assumes a sufficiently long system to suppress bulk transmission.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the lattice model and scattering setup; Fig 2 compares the reflection matrix eigenspectra under PBC and OBC; Fig 3 shows the spatial localization of reflection eigenvectors; Fig 4 displays the momentum-dependent GH shift across different topological phases.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scattering setup, develops the discrete-time scattering formalism, analyzes the non-Hermitian skin effect in the reflection matrix, connects the GH shift to the bulk topological invariant, and verifies the results across trivial, Chern, and anomalous phases.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Periodically driven systems host topological phases without static analogs, such as the anomalous Floquet phase characterized by trivial bulk bands yet robust boundary modes. In this work, we investigate the scattering problem of a Floquet Chern insulator and reveal the non-Hermitian skin effect (NHSE) of reflected waves. Using a discrete-time scattering formalism, we demonstrate how the non-Hermitian winding number of the reflection matrix is linked to the bulk Floquet invariant via boundary resonances. This reflected-wave NHSE relies on which quasienergy gap the incident wave resides in, leading to a gap-dependent Goos-Hänchen (GH) shift. We further show that the momentum-integrated GH shift quantitatively yields the Floquet topological invariant of the corresponding gap. Our work highlights a frequency-dependent NHSE of reflected waves in driven systems and provides a real-space scattering approach to identify non-equilibrium topology.

</details>

### [Violations of the Leggett-Garg inequality in Hybrid Liouvillian Dynamics: The Nonlinear Role of Detector Efficiency](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13494v1)

**Authors:** Sourav Paul, Parveen Kumar, Sourin Das

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

**Summary.** The paper shows that the extreme violations of the Leggett-Garg inequality predicted in non-Hermitian dynamics are not robust but are highly sensitive to detector efficiency. It reveals a nonlinear, logarithmic collapse of these violations as soon as any quantum jumps are detected, placing strict constraints on experimental implementations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics because it demonstrates how continuous monitoring and imperfect post-selection can fundamentally alter temporal correlations and suppress non-classicality.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the robustness of extreme violations of the Leggett-Garg inequality (LGI) under realistic measurement conditions and determining if super-quantum violations are physically achievable or merely singular limits.

**Main result.** Extreme LGI violations (approaching the algebraic bound of 3) are found to be intrinsically fragile, as even an infinitesimal increase in detector efficiency causes a rapid, nonlinear suppression of the violation toward the classical bound.

**Method.** The authors use a quantum trajectory framework with a non-trace-preserving, time-local hybrid Liouvillian master equation and analyze sequential measurement protocols and joint probabilities.

**Model / system.** An open two-level system (qubit) undergoing hybrid Liouvanian dynamics, characterized by a Hamiltonian with coherent tunneling and a dissipation term that includes a detector efficiency parameter q.

**Key observables.** Leggett-Garg parameter (K3), No-Signaling in Time (NSIT) conditions, Arrow of Time (AoT) conditions, and Bloch vector components.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Detector efficiency (q), dissipation rate (gamma), coupling strength (J), and the dimensionless decay rate (gamma/J).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dynamics are assumed to be time-local and Markovian within the hybrid Liouvillian framework.

**Figures summary.** The figures include a heatmap of the maximal K3 parameter and a universal scaling profile showing the hyperbolic-tangent dependence of the violation on the logarithm of detector efficiency.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of LGI violations, derives the hybrid Liouvillian master equation from a microscopic model, analyzes the spectral structure and exceptional points, quantifies the violation of LGI and NSIT conditions, and concludes with experimental implications.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Violations of the Leggett-Garg inequality (LGI) up to its algebraic bound under non-Hermitian dynamics are well established theoretically. Here, we demonstrate that such extreme violations are intrinsically fragile when realistic measurement processes are taken into account. We consider an open two-level system described by a time-local hybrid Liouvillian, with a continuous parameter $q \in [0,1]$, representing detector efficiency, i.e., the fraction of quantum jump trajectories that are retained in the ensemble. This parameter interpolates between trace-preserving Lindblad dynamics ($q=1$) and non-Hermitian ``no-jump" evolution ($q=0$). While $K_3$ approaches its algebraic maximum of 3 in the null-efficiency limit, even an infinitesimal increase in detector efficiency induces a rapid, highly nonlinear suppression toward the classical bound. This logarithmic sensitivity reveals that maximal LGI violations are not robust physical features but rather singular limits of idealized measurement conditions. Our results have direct experimental implications: achieving algebraic LGI violations in systems undergoing continuous time evolution requires near-perfect suppression of detected quantum jumps (i.e., effective post-selection), placing stringent constraints on detector performance. In contrast to discrete protocols based on time-non-divisible dynamics, our framework shows that extreme violations arising within continuous, divisible quantum trajectory evolution constitute a fundamentally fragile regime.

</details>

**Authors:** Martin Achleitner, Alessandro Trenti, Philip Walther, Hannes Hübel  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13359v1>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates the successful distribution of GHz-rate sequential time-bin entangled photons over a 30 km metropolitan fiber network. By using dispersion compensation and time-bin encoding, the researchers achieved 93% visibility, showing that high-speed quantum communication is feasible using existing fiber infrastructure.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum networks as it demonstrates the practical scaling of high-bandwidth entanglement distribution using robust time-bin encoding in real-world fiber infrastructures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of distributing high-rate (GHz) sequential time-bin entanglement over a metropolitan fiber network while overcoming chromatic dispersion and polarization fluctuations.

**Main result.** Demonstrated high-quality entanglement distribution over a 3-D km fiber link in Vienna with a measured 93% quantum visibility, proving the feasibility of GHz-rate QKD using off-the-shelf components.

**Method.** The researchers used a type-II SPDC source pumped by a GHz-rate pulse train, implemented dispersion compensation to manage pulse broadening, and utilized a Mach-Zehnder Interferometer for time-bin analysis.

**Model / system.** An experimental platform consisting of a mode-locked laser, a ppKTP crystal for SPDC at 1554nm, a 28.6 km metropolitan fiber link, and an analysis setup with SNSPDs and a Mach-Zehnder Interferometer.

**Key observables.** Coincidence rate, singles rate, Coincidence-to-Accidental Ratio (CAR), and quantum interference visibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** GHz repetition rates (1-10 GHz), 9.5 dB fiber loss, 93% visibility, 1554 nm wavelength, and 400 ps coincidence window.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The visibility was corrected by subtracting accidental coincidences; the SPDC spectrum was not filtered, which may limit maximum achievable visibility.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the schematic of the entanglement source; Figure 9 displays the quantum interference fringe (visibility vs. phase) and a delay histogram showing the three coincidence peaks.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for entanglement distribution, describes the experimental setup and source, details the network and dispersion management, presents the characterization of rates and visibility, and concludes with the implications for QKD.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Efficient generation and high-quality distribution of entanglement is becoming increasingly more relevant in the field of quantum technologies, with important applications such as multiparty computation as well as quantum key distribution (QKD) on the rise. Quantum communication protocols based on entanglement offer an inherent quantum based randomness for key generation and provide in general higher security compared to prepare and measure implementations. Moreover, the future quantum internet will also be based on the distribution of entanglement for securely connecting quantum computers in a network.   In this work we show the feasibility of using sequential time-bin entangled states for quantum key distribution in metropolitan networks using off-the-shelf components. The time-bin encoding ensures high fidelity distribution robust against random polarisation fluctuations occuring in optical fibers. Modulated laser pulses in the GHz frequency range are used to generate time-bin entangled photon pairs. The entangled photons are then sent over an about 30km long (9.5dB loss) fiber link within the Vienna fiber network, showing high degree of distributed entanglement with a measured 93\% quantum visibility.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13241"></a>
### [No chaos required: traversable wormhole signals survive 98% coupling deletion](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13241v1)

**Authors:** Sagar Dubey  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13241v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13241_figures/2605.13241_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Signal-chaos decoupling at N = 10, β = 8, µ = 0.1 (50 realizations per point). (a) Level spacing ratio ⟨r⟩ver- sus sparsity p for N = 10 (filled circles) and N = 14 (open squares). Dashed lines indicate GUE (⟨r⟩= 0.603) and Pois- son (⟨r⟩= 0.386) reference values. (b) Transmission peak height |C(t∗)| versus p. Error bars denote SEM. The dashed line marks the dense (p = 1.0) mean. The signal varies by less than 1.1% despite a complete loss of quantum chaos.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13241_figures/2605.13241_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 1(b) shows the main result: the transmission peak height |C(t∗)| as a function of sparsity, computed from 50 disorder realizations at each of nine sparsity values from p = 1.0 to p = 0.02. </figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13241_figures/2605.13241_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 2. Transmission peak height versus inter-system cou- pling µ for three sparsity levels: p = 1.0 (chaotic, circles), p = 0.1 (edge of chaos, squares), and p = 0.05 (non-chaotic, triangles). N = 10, β = 8, 50 realizations. Error bars denote SEM. The three curves overlap at each µ value, confirming that the signal is controlled by the coupling, not by the inter- nal dynamics.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13241_figures/2605.13241_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 3. Noise robustness of the transmission signal at N = 8, β = 8, µ = 0.1 (30 realizations). (a) Peak height |C(t∗)| versus dephasing rate γ for four sparsity levels. The curves overlap, confirming that noise sensitivity is independent of sparsity. The dashed line marks the 50% degradation thresh- old. (b) Critical dephasing rate γ∗(at which the signal drops to 50% of its noiseless value) versus sparsity. γ∗is constant at ≈0.055 J.</figcaption>

<img src="2605.13241_figures/2605.13241_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

The mean (horizontal markers inside each vi- olin) is approximately constant across all sparsities, but the spread grows from σ = 0.006 at p = 1.0 to σ = 0.061 at p = 0.02. Dashed line: dense mean.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that the transmission signal in traversable wormhole simulations is independent of the system's internal quantum chaos. This decoupling means the signal primarily tracks inter-system coupling, allowing for highly efficient quantum simulations that discard up to 98% of Hamiltonian terms.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides critical insight into the validity of using quantum circuits to simulate holographic gravity and offers a massive reduction in gate complexity for simulating wormhole-like protocols.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the transmission signal in traversable wormhole protocols is a signature of holographic dynamics or merely a generic feature of inter-system coupling, and determining if this signal depends on internal quantum chaos.

**Main result.** The transmission signal is decoupled from internal chaos, remaining invariant even when 98% of the Hamiltonian couplings are deleted, which implies that the signal probes inter-system coupling fidelity rather than gravity-like dynamics.

**Method.** Numerical simulations using exact diagonalization (ED) for small systems and Krylov-subspace extensions (Lanczos) for larger systems, combined with level spacing analysis and Lindblad master equation evolution.

**Model / system.** A doubled Sachdev-Ye-Kitaev (SYK) model consisting of two sides (Left and Right) in a Thermofield Double (TFD) state, connected by a weak Maldacova-Qi bilinear interaction.

**Key observables.** Transmission signal peak height |C(t*)|, peak time t*, Full Width at Half Maximum (FWHM), level spacing ratio <r> (chaos diagnostic), and entanglement entropy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sparsity parameter p (fraction of surviving couplings), inter-system coupling strength mu, inverse temperature beta, and dephasing rate gamma.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study is restricted to the q=4 SYK model and relies on numerical results without a complete analytical proof.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the transition from GUE to sub-Poisson statistics via sparsity and the corresponding invariance of the transmission peak height; other figures illustrate TFD structural diagnostics and noise sensitivity.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of signal interpretation, describes the sparsification methodology, presents results on signal-chaos decoupling, analyzes the robustness of the TFD state, and concludes with implications for quantum simulation complexity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The traversable wormhole protocol in coupled Sachdev-Ye-Kitaev (SYK) systems produces a transmission signal C(t) widely interpreted as evidence of holographic dynamics. Recent work has questioned this interpretation, showing that similar signals arise in generic thermalizing systems. We address what the signal actually probes by systematically destroying quantum chaos in the SYK model via random coupling deletion, while monitoring the transmission signal across the chaos-to-integrable transition. Using exact diagonalization of the doubled SYK model at N=10 with 50 disorder realizations per sparsity, supplemented by Krylov-subspace extensions to N=20, we find that the ensemble-averaged peak height varies by less than 1.1% across a 50-fold sparsification range, even as the underlying spectrum transitions from Gaussian-unitary-ensemble to sub-Poisson statistics. A 1,200-instance sweep over the inter-system coupling mu confirms that the signal is controlled by mu alone, with no dependence on internal chaos. We further verify that the thermofield double state retains its thermal structure under sparsification despite substantial changes to the state vector, providing a structural explanation for the invariance. These results indicate that the transmission signal diagnoses inter-system coupling fidelity rather than holographic dynamics, and that future quantum-simulation experiments require independent chaos diagnostics to substantiate gravitational claims. As a practical consequence, the invariance implies that 98% of the Hamiltonian's coupling terms can be discarded (with variance rescaling of the survivors), reducing the gate count per Trotter step by approximately 50x at N=10 and bringing larger traversable-wormhole simulations within experimental reach.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13564"></a>

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13564_figures/2605.13564_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 4(b) the data are for the 2D Ising model. Mpemba eﬀect cannot be detected till very late time by which growths</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13564_figures/2605.13564_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 4. (a) Plots of ℓ(t), versus time, for the 3D Ising model, following quenches of initial conﬁgurations from a few diﬀerent Ts</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores how different initial temperatures affect the speed of phase ordering in Ising and XY models. It identifies a significant slowdown in growth for the 3D XY model at zero temperature and demonstrates that the Mpemba effect is robust in 3D systems, making it a potentially observable phenomenon in experiments.

**Why it may be interesting.** The findings regarding non-standard growth exponents and the emergence of the Mpemba effect in higher dimensions provide deep insights into non-equilibrium many-body dynamics and the relaxation of systems toward equilibrium.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates phase ordering dynamics following temperature quenches, specifically focusing on slow growth exponents and the occurrence of the Mpemba effect in O(n) symmetric models.

**Main result.** In the 3D XY model at zero temperature, the growth exponent is found to be significantly lower (~0.15) than the expected 1/2. Additionally, the Mpemba effect is observed in 3D models even without restricting initial magnetization, suggesting high experimental relevance.

**Method.** Monte Carlo simulations using the Metropolis algorithm on 2D and 3D lattices, with error analysis performed via the Jackknife resampling method.

**Model / system.** O(n) symmetric models, specifically the 2D and 3D Ising models (n=1) and the 3D XY model (n=2), undergoing quenches from a paramagnetic phase to a ferromagnetic phase.

**Key observables.** Characteristic length scale l(t), growth exponent alpha, two-point equal-time correlation function, potential energy, magnetization, and susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Initial temperature (Ts), final temperature (Tf), spatial dimension (d=2, 3), and initial magnetization (m).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the Metropolis algorithm for these dynamics and focuses on non-conserved order parameter dynamics.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows snapshots of evolving line defects; Figure 2 displays the characteristic length scale growth, scaling collapse of the correlation function, and the instantaneous growth exponent.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of phase ordering and the Mpemba effect, describes the Monte Carlo methodology and models used, presents results regarding growth exponents and the Mpemba effect across different dimensions and magnetization constraints, and concludes with implications for experimental relevance.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study phase ordering dynamics in the three-dimensional nonconserved XY model, via Monte Carlo simulations, for quenches from paramagnetic phase to certain final temperatures $T_f$ within the ferromagnetic region of the phase diagram. The growth in the system occurs via annihilation of vortex and anti-vortex pairs, cores of which, in the three dimensional system geometry, join from different planes, on which the spins lie, to form line defects. In the long-time limit, the associated characteristic length scale, $\ell(t)$, appears to grow with time $(t)$ approximately as $t^{0.15}$, for $T_f=0$. The exponent is much smaller, like in the zero temperature intermediate time ordering in the three dimensional Ising model, than $1/2$, the expected value, that is realized for quenches to $T_f$ value that is sufficiently larger than zero. We carry out quenches from different starting temperatures, $T_s$, that lie above the critical temperature $T_c$. It is observed that the systems with higher $T_s$ approach the final equilibrium faster. This resembles the puzzling Mpemba effect. We present similar results also from the simulations of the two- and three- dimensional Ising model. In the case of the 2D Ising model, we show that the Mpemba effect is observed only if the starting magnetization is restricted to a value close to zero. In $d=3$, on the other hand, for both the models, the effect appears even if the initial configurations at a given $T_s$ are chosen from the full distribution of magnetization. Thus, our results are of much experimental relevance.

</details>

### [Quantized Transport in Floquet Topological Insulators](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13066v1)

**Authors:** Rekha Kumari, Manas Kulkarni, Abhishek Dhar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13066v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

**Summary.** This paper proves that the total conductance of a Floquet topological insulator, when summed over all photon-assisted sidebands, exhibits robust quantization. This 'sum rule' allows for the identification of topological phases, including anomalous phases, through steady-state transport measurements.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental link between non-equilibrium steady-state transport and topological invariants in driven systems, which is crucial for understanding how topological protection manifests in open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to understand the microscopic origin of Floquet conductance sum rules and establish a connection between steady-state transport signatures and Floquet winding invariants, especially in anomalous phases.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that while individual Floquet sidebands are not quantized, the summed conductance over all sidebands is quantized as $|W_{\epsilon}| e^2/h$ for longitudinal and $W_{\epsilon} e^2/h$ for Hall conductance.

**Method.** The study utilizes the Floquet nonequilibrium Green's-function (NEGF) formalism combined with exact numerical simulations on strip and cylindrical geometries and an analytical approach in the weak-coupling limit.

**Model / system.** A 2D bipartite square lattice subject to a five-step periodic driving protocol that breaks time-reversal symmetry, coupled to static fermionic reservoirs.

**Key observables.** Two-terminal (longitudinal) conductance, Hall (transverse) conductance, Floquet winding invariants, and quasienergy spectrum.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Driving frequency (Omega), chemical potential (mu_F), coupling strength (gamma), system size (nx, ny), and the ratio of hopping amplitude to frequency (J/Omega).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reservoirs are treated in the wide-band limit, the system is at zero temperature, and the reservoirs are ideal Fermi reservoirs in the linear-response limit.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the lattice structure, the five-step driving protocol, and the resulting stroboscopic dynamics; Figure 2 shows the cylindrical geometry for spectral analysis; Figure 5 depicts the spatial variation of bond currents and sideband contributions to conductance.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical model and driving protocol, presents numerical transport results for longitudinal and Hall conductance, provides an analytical derivation of the sum rule in the weak-coupling limit, and discusses the spatial structure of currents and convergence of the sum.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study quantum transport in a periodically driven (Floquet) topological system coupled to static fermionic reservoirs. Using the Floquet nonequilibrium Green's-function (NEGF) formalism we show, from exact numerics for a strip geometry, that the two-terminal (longitudinal) conductance is quantized as $|W_{\varepsilon}|\,e^2/h$, while the Hall (transverse) conductance is quantized as $W_{\varepsilon}\,e^2/h$, where $W_{\varepsilon}$ is the Floquet winding invariant associated with the quasienergy gap at $\varepsilon = 0$ or $\varepsilon = Ω/2$. Quantization is achieved only after summing over the contribution of all Floquet sidebands. We provide an analytic understanding of this Floquet conductance sum rule, by considering the Hall conductance in the weak coupling limit. In that limit, we show that the Floquet Hall conductance gets contributions from the Floquet sidebands, which includes the signs of the velocities of the edge modes. Their sum yields exact quantization, as predicted by the Floquet sum rule. We find that in a wide range of parameter regime, the convergence is fast, making observation of the sum rule and Floquet winding numbers accessible to experiments.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports the first implementation of an on-chip quantum memory using erbium-doped thin-film lithium niobate. It achieves high-fidelity storage of telecom-band time-bin qubits and demonstrates temporal multi-mode capability. This provides a critical building block for scalable, chip-scale quantum repeaters and the quantum internet.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates the control of coherence and state preservation in a solid-state integrated platform, specifically addressing the challenges of decoherence and efficient photon-matter interaction in the telecom band.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a demonstrated on-chip quantum memory within the thin-film lithium niobate (TFLN) platform for telecom-band photons, which is essential for scalable quantum repeaters and networks.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated the first on-chip quantum memory in Er3+-doped TFLN, achieving a storage time of 400 ns with 1.95% efficiency and a time-bin qubit storage fidelity of 96.8%.

**Method.** The study utilizes the Atomic Frequency Comb (AFC) protocol via spectral hole burning in erbium-doped TFLN waveguides, combined with photon-counting measurements and unbalanced Mach-Zehnder interferometry.

**Model / system.** An integrated platform consisting of a 5 mm-long x-cut thin-film lithium niobate (TFLN) waveguide doped with 0.5 mol% Er3+ ions, operated at cryogenic temperatures (1.6 K) and under a 4 T magnetic field.

**Key observables.** Optical lifetime (T1), optical coherence time (T2), ground-state lifetime, absorption coefficient, storage efficiency, and quantum state fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Telecom wavelength (1531.6 nm), storage time of 400 ns, efficiency of 1.95%, and 4-mode temporal multiplexing capability.

**Figures summary.** Fig 2 shows spectroscopic characterization including absorption, T1, T2, and spectral hole decay; Fig 3 displays the AFC structure, photon time histograms, and multi-mode storage; Fig 4 shows photon-counting histograms for interference analysis.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for integrated quantum memory, describes the TFLN platform and Er3+ doping, details the spectroscopic characterization of the ions, presents the implementation of the AFC protocol for storage and multiplexing, and concludes with the demonstration of high-fidelity time-bin qubit storage.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Integrated photonics has emerged as a promising platform for quantum communication and quantum computation. Thin-film lithium niobate (TFLN) has gained significant attention in this field due to its exceptional optical properties, enabling the realization of numerous integrated photonic devices. However, quantum memory, which serves as a universal building block for the quantum internet, has not yet been demonstrated in TFLN. In this study, we realized the first on-chip quantum memory using erbium ions doped TFLN. The developed quantum memory achieves a storage time of 400 ns with an efficiency of 1.95%, significantly outperforming conventional waveguide delay lines. The multimode capability is demonstrated by successfully storing four temporal modes. Furthermore, single-photon-level coherent pulses are encoded into time-bin qubits and stored with a fidelity of 96.8% , surpassing the classical limit achievable by measure-and-prepare strategy. Our results demonstrate the first on-chip quantum memory for telecom-band time-bin qubits in TFLN, providing a key building block toward integrated quantum registers and repeaters for scalable quantum information processing.

</details>

**Summary.** The researchers optimized a barrel-shaped microwave coil design to create a highly uniform magnetic field for controlling large ensembles of NV centers in diamond. By reducing field inhomogeneity, they achieved much higher control fidelity compared to standard planar antennas. This advancement is crucial for improving the sensitivity and performance of diamond-based quantum sensors.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is relevant for quantum sensing and quantum information as it provides a hardware-level solution to improving the coherence and control fidelity of spin ensembles, which is critical for scalable quantum networks and high-sensitivity magnetometry.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving high spatial homogeneity of the microwave magnetic field when controlling large volumes of nitrogen-vacancy (NV) center ensembles in diamond to improve the sensitivity of pulsed magnetometry.

**Main result.** The development and optimization of a barrel-shaped coil system that significantly reduces magnetic field inhomogeneity (down to <0.1% radially) compared to traditional planar antenna systems.

**Method.** Numerical electromagnetic simulations using COMSOL Multiphysics and experimental validation via measuring Rabi oscillations of NV center ensembles.

**Model / system.** Ensembles of negatively charged nitrogen-vacancy (NV) centers in a diamond crystal, controlled by various microwave field-forming geometries including planar antennas, dielectric resonators, and specialized barrel-shaped coils.

**Key observables.** Rabi oscillations, magnetic flux density (B), magnetic field inhomogeneity (sigma_pp), and photoluminescence (PL) intensity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Operating frequency of ~2.87 GHz, target inhomogeneity threshold of <= 0.5%, and NV density of approximately 300 ppb.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quasi-static approximation is used for simulations; the decay in Rabi oscillations is attributed primarily to magnetic field inhomogeneity rather than microwave amplitude fluctuations.

**Figures summary.** The figures include comparisons of inhomogeneity levels across different geometries, schematics of the various field-forming systems, plots of magnetic flux density distributions, and experimental Rabi oscillation data for both planar and barrel-shaped coils.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a comparison of five field-forming systems, follows with numerical optimization of the barrel-shaped coil, presents experimental validation using Rabi oscillations, and concludes with a detailed analysis of the sources of signal decay.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The homogeneity of the microwave magnetic field is essential in controlling a large volume of ensemble spins, for example, in the case of sensitive magnetometry with nitrogen-vacancy (NV) centers in diamond. This is particularly important for pulsed measurement, where the fidelity of control pulses plays a crucial role in its sensitivity. So far, several magnetic field-forming systems have been proposed, but no detailed comparison has been made. Here, we numerically study the homogeneity of five different systems, including a planar antenna, a dielectric resonator, a cylindrical inductor, a barrel-shaped coil, and a nested barrel-shaped coil. The results of the simulation allowed us to optimize the design parameters of the barrel-shaped field-forming system, which led to significantly improved magnetic field uniformity. To measure this effect, we experimentally compared the homogeneity of a field-forming system having a barrel shape with that of a planar field-forming system by measuring Rabi oscillations of an ensemble of NV centers with them. Significant improvements in inhomogeneity were confirmed in the barrel-shaped coil.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.12638"></a>
### [Dissipative Dynamics and Active Stabilization of Linear and Nonlinear Waves in Non-PT-Symmetric Harmonic Traps](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12638v1)

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 5. Spatio-temporal evolution of the wave amplitude |ψ(x, t)| in the presence of the NM protocol in Eq. (26) with γ = −2 and σ0 = −1, t0 = 100 (left panel), σ = 1, t0 = 75 (right panel). All other parameters are fixed as in Fig. 3.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that while static complex potentials can stabilize linear waves, they lead to the decay or collapse of nonlinear waves due to gain-loss imbalances. By implementing a time-periodic modulation of the nonlinearity, the authors show it is possible to actively stabilize these waves into robust non-equilibrium stationary states.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a general strategy for controlling non-equilibrium dynamics in open quantum systems, specifically addressing how to prevent the collapse of nonlinear waves in dissipative environments like BECs or lossy photonic waveguides.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the instability of nonlinear waves in non-PT-symmetric harmonic traps, where static complex potentials fail to prevent the decay or collapse of nonlinear wave packets.

**Main result.** The introduction of a time-dependent modulation of the nonlinearity (nonlinear management) can effectively stabilize metastable nonlinear states into robust non-equilibrium stationary states (NESS).

**Method.** The study uses an analytical mapping between real and complex Nonlinear Schrödinger equations combined with direct numerical simulations of wave packet dynamics.

**Model / system.** A normalized nonlinear Schrödinger (NLS) equation with a complex non-Hermitian potential $V(x) = V_R(x) + iW(x)$ representing gain and loss, applicable to photonics and Bose-Einstein condensates.

**Key observables.** Wavefunction amplitude, density $|\psi(x, t)|^2$, center-of-mass position $x_c(t)$, and peak density amplitude.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nonlinearity strength $\sigma$ (attractive, repulsive, or linear), modulation frequency $\Omega$, and modulation amplitude $\gamma$.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The stabilization relies on the assumption that active time-dependent modulation can compensate for the residual gain-loss imbalance inherent in nonlinear regimes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the engineered potential and damped linear dynamics; Figure 2 compares the onset of instability (collapse/decay) in linear vs. nonlinear cases; Figure 3 and 5 illustrate the spatio-temporal evolution and the formation of robust NESS under the nonlinear management protocol.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by introducing the complex potential engineering via analytical mapping, moves to demonstrating stable damped motion in the linear regime, explores the resulting instability in the nonlinear regime, and concludes by presenting a stabilization strategy via periodic nonlinearity modulation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the dissipative dynamics of linear and nonlinear waves in harmonic traps by means of engineered complex non-Hermitian potentials. By combining an analytical mapping between real and complex Schrödinger equations with direct numerical simulations, we show that while in the linear case the damped motion leads to the formation of a stationary state at the trap center, in the nonlinear case a static potential design alone is insufficient to ensure long-term stability. Instead, the system relaxes toward a long-lived metastable configuration that eventually undergoes decay or collapse. To overcome this limitation, we introduce a time-dependent modulation of the nonlinearity that effectively converts these metastable states into robust non-equilibrium stationary states. This approach establishes a general strategy for controlling nonlinear waves in non-Hermitian systems, with potential applications in photonics and Bose--Einstein condensates.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.12677"></a>
### [Loss-induced nonreciprocal quantum battery](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12677v1)

**Authors:** Muhammad Zaeem Zafar, Muhammad Irfan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12677v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

**Summary.** The paper proposes a method to enhance quantum battery charging by using an auxiliary cavity with controlled loss to create nonreciprocal energy flow. This mechanism suppresses energy flowing back from the battery to the charger, significantly increasing the steady-state energy storage capacity. The approach is potentially implementable using existing cavity QED or circuit QED techniques.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics as it demonstrates how engineered dissipation (loss engineering) can be used as a resource to break reciprocity and enhance energy transfer.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Improving the charging efficiency and performance of quantum batteries by developing a nonreciprocal model that achieves directional energy flow.

**Main result.** By engineering the loss in an auxiliary cavity, the authors induce a nonreciprocal energy flow that suppresses back-transmission, achieving a steady-state energy storage advantage of up to 8 times compared to bipartite systems.

**Method.** The study uses a combination of analytical derivations via quantum Langevin equations and input-output formalism, alongside numerical simulations using the QuTiP software package.

**Model / system.** A three-cavity system consisting of a charger cavity, a battery cavity, and an auxiliary cavity, modeled using a tripartite bosonic Hamiltonian and a Lindblad master equation to account for local decay rates.

**Key observables.** Energy stored in the battery and charger, energy transfer gain (ratio of battery to charger energy), and transmission coefficients.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Decay rates (kappa), coupling strengths (J), driving amplitude (Omega), detuning (Delta), and the phase of the coupling (theta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system follows Markovian dynamics, operates in the weak drive limit, and assumes all stored energy can be extracted (ergotropy equals total energy).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the three-cavity schematic; Figure 2 displays the time evolution of energy in the charger and battery and the evolution of energy transfer gain; Figure 3 shows steady-state gain versus coupling strength.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of quantum battery efficiency, describes the three-cavity model and its Hamiltonian, provides analytical derivations for nonreciprocal transmission, presents numerical results comparing the proposed model to reciprocal and bipartite cases, and concludes with experimental implementation suggestions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonreciprocal quantum batteries offer superior charging performance compared to reciprocal quantum batteries. We consider a charger-battery system comprising two optical cavities that interact independently with a third auxiliary cavity. We show that the nonzero dissipation of the auxiliary cavity induces a nonreciprocal exchange of excitations among the charger-battery system. Therefore, by engineering the loss in the auxiliary cavity, we induce a directional energy flow that enhances the charging efficiency. Using numerical and analytical calculations, we show that the steady-state energy stored in the battery significantly exceeds that in the charger. We compare our results with those of the reciprocal cases and demonstrate that our nonreciprocal quantum battery model exhibits a significant charging advantage. We believe that our proposed scheme represents a step forward in cavity-loss engineering, making it a viable approach for nonreciprocal quantum batteries with existing experimental techniques.

</details>
