### [Low Rank Structure of the Reduced Transition Matrix](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12665v1)

**Authors:** Cathy Li, Bruno Bertini, Katja Klobas, Tianci Zhou  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12665v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** The paper shows that while influence matrices in chaotic quantum circuits exhibit high entanglement, the related reduced transition matrix (RTM) has a low-rank structure with only logarithmic entropy growth. This discovery enables an efficient joint compression method for simulating local expectation values in complex quantum dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a new pathway for the efficient classical simulation of local observables in highly chaotic systems where standard wavefunction-based methods fail due to entanglement growth.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of classically simulating quantum many-body dynamics due to the volume-law entanglement growth in influence matrices, even in chaotic systems.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the reduced transition matrix (RTM) can be efficiently approximated via low-rank approximation because its von Neumann entropy grows at most logarithmically with time, despite the high entanglement of its constituent influence matrices.

**Method.** The study uses the influence-matrix formalism, the folding algorithm, and a joint compression procedure. Analytical proofs involve the entanglement membrane approach and the Schmidt-Mirsky theorem, while numerical validation uses random and dual-unitary circuits.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on non-equilibrium quantum many-body dynamics, specifically using brickwork quantum circuits, including chaotic dual-unitary circuits and Haar-random unitary circuits.

**Key observables.** Local expectation values (one-point functions), von Neumann entropy of the RTM, singular-value spectrum, and the Schatten 1-norm of the truncation error.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Entangling power (p), time (t), dimension of the local Hilbert space (d), and the critical entangling power (pc).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The validity of the scaling of amplitudes away from the critical point is treated as a conjecture supported by the membrane approach; the paper also assumes the availability of influence matrices for the compression procedure.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the schematic of one-point functions and the RTM construction; Figure 2 compares standard MPS compression with the proposed joint compression; Figure 4 illustrates the probability distribution and entropy growth in dual-unitary and random circuits; Supplemental figures visualize domain wall and magnon configurations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of influence matrix entanglement, proposes the RTM low-rank approximation, provides error control proofs, establishes logarithmic entropy bounds for dual-unitary circuits, and validates these findings with numerical simulations of random and non-random circuits.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The influence-matrix formalism provides an alternative route to the classical simulation of quantum dynamics. Because influence matrices retain information only about the effective bath seen by local observables, they are expected to be easier to simulate than the full wavefunction. Recent work, however, has shown that they carry strong temporal correlations even in maximally chaotic systems, making them difficult to represent efficiently. Here we show that the reduced transition matrix, a suitable combination of influence matrices that directly determines local expectation values, can nevertheless be efficiently approximated. We first show that the truncation error is controlled by its singular-value spectrum, which naturally motivates a low-rank approximation. We then prove that, for chaotic dual-unitary circuits, the associated entropy grows at most logarithmically in time. Our conclusions follow from exact results for random dual-unitary circuits and are further supported by numerical results for fixed instances of both dual-unitary and random circuits.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Insights into the Nature of Quantum Emitters in Electron-Irradiated hexagonal Boron Nitride](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12663v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates the origin of green-yellow single-photon emitters in electron-irradiated hBN. By using thermal annealing and plasma etching, the authors prove these emitters are intrinsic defects rather than surface contaminants. This provides a foundation for using ultra-thin hBN layers in quantum photonic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information as it addresses the fundamental reliability and reproducibility of solid-state single-photon sources, which are essential building blocks for quantum photonic networks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining whether green-yellow quantum emitters in electron-irradiated hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) are intrinsic lattice defects or extrinsic organic contaminants.

**Main result.** The study rules out organic contamination as the source of the emission and demonstrates that these emitters are stable, localized within the top hBN layers, and can be created in flakes thinner than 10 nm.

**Method.** The researchers used hyperspectral imaging, thermal annealing up to 500 C, and oxygen plasma etching for depth-resolved analysis, combined with SEM and AFM.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of exfoliated hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) flakes that have undergone electron-beam irradiation via low-acceleration-voltage SEM.

**Key observables.** Zero-Phonon Line (ZPL) position, Phonon Sideband (PSB) features, photoluminescence intensity, fluorescence lifetime, and photon purity (g2(0)).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (4.5 K to room temperature), annealing temperature (up to 500 C), hBN thickness (below 10 nm), and electron beam fluence.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis excludes spectral data below 550 nm due to hardware-induced attenuation, and assumes circular polarization effectively compensates for arbitrary dipole orientations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows ZPL-filtered intensity maps and spectral histograms at different temperatures; Figure 2 displays PL intensity and lifetime maps after various annealing temperatures; Figure 3 presents AFM and PL maps before and after plasma etching to show depth-dependent emitter stability.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the contamination ambiguity, presents a framework using imaging and annealing to rule out organics, uses plasma etching to determine vertical localization, and concludes with implications for scalable quantum photonics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum emitters in hexagonal boron nitride (hBN) have emerged as a promising solid-state platform for quantum technology applications. However, a persistent challenge in the field is the unclear origin of many observed emission lines, particularly in the visible range, which can be difficult to distinguish from signals arising from organic or process-induced contamination during sample preparations and handling. This ambiguity limits both the reproducibility of emitter generation and the reliable identification of truly intrinsic quantum defects. This work provides a step-by-step framework to assess whether quantum emitters in electron-irradiated hBN are associated with organic contaminants introduced during sample preparation. We employ hyperspectral imaging, thermal annealing, and oxygen plasma etching to investigate the origin of the green-yellow emitters in electron-irradiated hBN. The combined results not only rule out organic contamination as the source of emission but also provide insight into the spectral variability, thermal stability, and vertical localization of the emitters generated in electron-irradiated hBN that was created without any pre- or post-processing. In addition, our experiments demonstrate the feasibility of creating stable emitters in hBN with thicknesses below 10 nm. These findings provide practical guidance for the identification and controlled implementation of hBN-based single-photon emitters in quantum photonic devices.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to significantly boost the number of atoms and their storage time in nanophotonic traps using advanced cooling techniques. By applying Lambda-enhanced gray-molasses and EIT cooling, the researchers achieved a six-fold increase in atom loading and a five-fold increase in trap lifetime. This provides a robust pathway for improving the optical depth and coupling strength in nanofiber-based quantum interfaces.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work demonstrates highly efficient sub-Doppler cooling techniques (LambdaGM and EIT) specifically optimized for the extreme constraints of nanophotonic interfaces, which is crucial for developing scalable quantum networks and light-matter interfaces.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently loading neutral atoms into nanophotonic traps is difficult due to extremely small trap volumes, which limits the number of atoms coupled to the waveguide and their storage time.

**Main result.** The implementation of Lambda-enhanced gray-molasse (LambdaGM) cooling leads to a six-fold increase in the number of loaded Cesium atoms and, combined with EIT cooling, extends the trap storage time by up to five-fold.

**Method.** The researchers use a sequential cooling approach starting with Red-detuned Polarization Gradient Cooling (RPGC) followed by Lambda-enhanced gray-molasses (LambdaGM) on the D1 transition, and subsequently apply EIT cooling using fiber-guided light fields.

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of an optical nanofiber-based dipole trap using the evanescent field of a tapered optical fiber to trap Cesium atoms in a two-color (red and blue detuned) configuration.

**Key observables.** Optical depth (OD), atom number (N), atom-photon coupling strength (beta), atomic temperature, trap storage time, and loading/loss rates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trap depth of 24 microK, trap volume of approximately 1e-16 cm^3, EIT cooling power at the picowatt scale, and the collisional blockade regime.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1a shows the experimental setup; Fig 2 shows optimal two-photon detuning and OD; Fig 3 shows fluorescence imaging; Fig 5 shows comparison of atom storage times and the observation of a Fano-type resonance in OD.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the nanophotonic trapping challenge, describes the experimental setup and cooling sequence (MOT, RPGC, LambdaGM, EIT), presents results on loading enhancement and atom numbers, demonstrates storage time extension via EIT, and concludes with the performance of fiber-guided cooling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nanophotonic traps for cold atoms typically have trap volumes that are orders of magnitude smaller than, e.g., free-space optical tweezers. This makes efficient loading of these traps challenging, thereby limiting the total number of atoms coupled to the nanophotonic waveguide. Here, we demonstrate that $Λ$-enhanced gray-molasses ($Λ$GM) can substantially increase the number of trapped atoms in a nanofiber-based cold-atom setup. Specifically, we observe a six-fold increase in the number of loaded atoms compared to conventional red-detuned polarization gradient cooling. Despite the unusually small depth of our optical trap of only 24 $μ$K, we load about 4000 individual Cesium atoms, achieving optical depths exceeding 140 and reaching the collisional blockade regime over a length of approximately 1 mm. After loading, we perform efficient EIT-assisted cooling that is found to increase the trap storage time to 400(9) ms. This is a 5-fold improvement over the passive storage time. Remarkably, EIT-cooling also works with two co-propagating nanofiber-guided light fields and requiries only about a few hundred picowatt of optical power. Our results provide an efficient method to boost both the number of loaded atoms and the storage time of nanophotonic atom traps.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13756"></a>

**Authors:** Jakub Rembieliński, Karol Ławniczak

**Summary.** This paper replaces the instantaneous von Neumann collapse with a continuous quasilinear evolution that describes state reduction as a smooth dynamical process. The proposed model preserves fundamental quantum principles like the no-signaling principle and the Born rule while identifying specific parameter regimes where the model might deviate from standard theory, offering potential experimental tests.

**Why it may be interesting.** It offers a novel way to unify unitary evolution with measurement-induced reduction through a continuous nonlinear dynamical process, which is highly relevant to the foundations of open quantum systems and measurement theory.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the conceptual issues of the von Neumann projection postulate, specifically the instantaneous and discontinuous nature of state collapse.

**Main result.** The authors propose a quasilinear evolution that provides a continuous, deterministic description of state reduction that is consistent with the no-signaling principle and the Born rule.

**Method.** The study uses a nonlinear generalization of the von Neumann equation, employing Bloch-vector representation, SL(2,C) group invariants, and numerical simulations of Riccati-type equations.

**Model / system.** A two-level quantum system (qubit) undergoing evolution driven by a time-dependent potential, with specific applications to the Stern-Gerlach experiment.

**Key observables.** Spin projection (observable Omega), Bloch vector trajectories, and the rate of change of the state vector.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The driving potential magnitude (g0), the orientation angle (Theta), and the Casimir invariants (C1, C2) of the evolution.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The validity of the model relies on the preservation of ensemble equivalence to ensure consistency with Einstein causality.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the parameter space as a tetrahedron, the evolution of Bloch vectors under different potentials (Morse and Stern-Gerlach), and the rate of state change in various regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the quasilinear framework, proves its consistency with no-signaling and the Born rule, analyzes the dynamics of two-level systems using geometric invariants, explores structural instability and experimental testability, and applies the model to the Stern-Gerlach experiment.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this article, we introduce a new form of quantum selective measurement in which the von Neumann projection postulate is replaced by quasilinear evolution, governed by a nonlinear generalization of the von Neumann equation. We demonstrate that this equation preserves the equivalence of quantum ensembles and, consequently, satisfies the no-signalling principle, ensuring consistency with both quantum mechanics and Einstein causality. Our approach eliminates the need for instantaneous, discontinuous state collapse and provides a unified description of the postmeasurement quantum state reduction as a form of quantum state evolution. Notably, it does not require invoking concepts such as the quantum state assigned to a classical apparatus. At the same time, the stochastic character of selective measurement and the Born rule remain unchanged. We present several numerical solutions of the evolution equation for quasilinear selective measurement in two-level quantum systems and compare them with the standard von Neumann projection. The results demonstrate agreement between the two measurement schemes in their fundamental properties. Furthermore, we investigate phenomena associated with the structural instability of the evolution equation and identify very narrow parameter regions in which the outcomes deviate from those predicted by the von Neumann projection. These regions may offer opportunities to test the proposed approach experimentally. Finally, using specific analytical solutions, we discuss the Stern-Gerlach experiment within the framework of quasilinear measurement.

</details>

### [QCIVET: A Quantum--Classical Pipeline Integrity Framework with Contract-Based Subtype Verification and Hash-Chained Audit Traces](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13109v1)

**Authors:** Esra Yeniaras, Muhammad Amin Karimov

**Summary.** The paper introduces QCIVET, a framework designed to verify that the quantum stages of a hybrid computing pipeline behave according to their intended specifications. It bridges the gap between classical supply-chain security and quantum hardware noise by using observable-based contracts to detect malicious or erroneous circuit substitutions. The framework was successfully validated on real IBM quantum hardware, demonstrating its ability to catch 'sneaky' attacks that pass simple verification tests.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Ensuring the integrity and auditability of hybrid quantum-classical pipelines against adversarial threats like specification tampering, hardware-level poisoning, and 'sneaky' circuit substitutions that bypass classical verification.

**Main result.** The QCIVET framework successfully detects 'sneaky-subtype' attacks and hardware drift on real IBM Heron processors, providing a low-latency, hardware-validated runtime audit mechanism.

**Method.** A dual-layer auditing approach using hash-chained audit trails for structural integrity and calibrated observable-deviation tests based on Liskov-Wing behavioral subtyping for semantic integrity.

**Model / system.** Hybrid quantum-classical pipelines involving quantum subroutines (e.g., VQE, quantum kernels) executed on IBM Quantum processors, specifically the ibm_fez (Heron r2) and Eagle r3 architectures.

**Key observables.** Self-adjoint operators (Pauli expectation values) used for observable-deviation contracts and informationally complete observable families.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Error tolerance threshold (epsilon), diamond-norm distance between quantum channels, and classical commit latency (sub-millisecond).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the adversary cannot forge external anchors or SHA-256 hashes, and that the observable family used for verification is sufficiently informationally complete.

**Figures summary.** Figures include a landscape map of quantum software contracts, a calibration window showing noise floor vs. logical deviation, and visualizations of various hash-chain tampering scenarios.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the integrity gap in hybrid pipelines, proposes the QCIVET framework, provides formal proofs for soundness and completeness, demonstrates experimental validation on real IBM hardware across three application domains (VQE, fraud detection, cloud auditing), and compares the framework against existing classical and quantum security literature.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hybrid quantum--classical pipelines increasingly support applications such as drug discovery, fraud detection, and cloud quantum processing unit (QPU) auditing, yet existing integrity-verification methods remain largely classical and fail to capture quantum-stage behaviour. We propose QCIVET, a contract-based integrity-verification framework that models a hybrid pipeline as a sequence of stages with explicit specifications and audits it at both syntactic and semantic levels. Syntactic integrity is enforced through a hash-chained audit trail with optional external anchoring, while semantic integrity at quantum stages is verified using a calibrated observable-deviation test grounded in the behavioural-subtyping discipline of Liskov and Wing. We prove soundness under the diamond-norm distance between quantum channels, conditional completeness for informationally complete observable families, and compositionality under inheritance chains. We further identify a class of Z-only-sneaky overrides that evade weak single-Pauli contracts but are exposed by multi-Pauli contracts. The framework is evaluated under calibration-derived noise models from IBM Quantum Eagle r3 and Heron r2 processors, and the subtype-separation protocol is validated end-to-end on a real ibm_fez (Heron r2) processor. QCIVET is instantiated on three representative applications: variational quantum eigensolver (VQE) for drug discovery, quantum-assisted fraud detection, and customer-side auditing of cloud QPU services. The reference implementation, including a real-time verification engine with sub-millisecond per-stage commit latency, is released as open source.

</details>

### [A Quantum Multi-Programming Framework to Maximize Quantum Resources for the LUCJ Ansatz](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12614v1)

**Authors:** Milana Bazayeva, Abigail McClain Gomez, Kenneth M. Merz

**Summary.** The paper introduces a quantum multi-programming framework designed to run multiple quantum chemistry circuits simultaneously on a single QPU to increase throughput. By using a robust post-processing protocol (SQD/ext-SQD), the authors demonstrate that the accuracy of molecular energy estimations can be maintained despite the increased hardware cross-talk associated with parallel execution.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** How to maximize quantum hardware throughput on cloud platforms via multi-programming without allowing hardware cross-talk to degrade the accuracy of quantum chemistry simulations.

**Main result.** The proposed multi-programming framework effectively mitigates cross-talk-induced noise, achieving energy deviations from the classical reference as low as 0.001 kcal/mol at the final SQD iteration.

**Method.** Development of a novel parallel experiment class for the LUCJ ansatz using Qiskit, combined with Sample-based Quantum Diagonalization (SQD) and its extended version (ext-SQD) for error mitigation.

**Model / system.** Simulated ethanol molecules in two configurations: equilibrium (EtOHEq) and a stretched O-H bond (EtOH1.2), executed on the IBM Quantum ibm_boston QPU.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energy deviation relative to the Heat-bath Configuration Interaction (HCI) reference.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Qubit layouts (serial vs. parallel), number of ancilla qubits (2 to 5), and SQD sample sizes (1K, 3K, and 5K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Manual qubit partitioning was used; circuits are kept at a single repetition; the HCI method is assumed to be a reliable ground-truth reference.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the multi-programming workflow; Figure 2 illustrates different qubit layouts with buffers; Figure 3 displays the distribution of energy deviations across layouts and modes; Table 1 and 2 summarize energy errors at different SQD stages and sample sizes.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to resource management challenges, description of the multi-programming framework and LUCJ ansatz, experimental setup on IBM hardware, results comparing serial vs. parallel execution, and an analysis of sampling convergence and ancilla scaling.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In the context of quantum computing, efficient resource management is crucial for optimizing throughput on cloud-based platforms and maximizing hardware utilization. In the present work, we propose an approach to tackle quantum chemistry problems via quantum multi-programming of the Local Unitary Cluster Jastrow (LUCJ) ansätze. The ground-state energy of the molecular system is obtained via Sample-based quantum diagonalization (SQD), further refined by its extended version (ext-SQD). Building upon the Qiskit Experiments package, which already supports parallel execution functionality for general tasks, we developed a novel parallel experiment class tailored for quantum chemistry problems. Cross-talk is a known issue in the multi-programming frameworks and can corrupt the ground-energy estimation of the simulated systems. To assess its impact within our approach, we simulated two conformations of the ethanol molecule: one at the equilibrium state (EtOH$_{Eq}$), and one with the O-H bond stretched to 1.2 ${Å}$ (EtOH$_{1.2}$). We defined three different layouts that we executed in a randomized fashion, alternating serial and parallel execution within 10 independent replicates. The single modality of each circuit was kept as a baseline to evaluate the effect of cross-talk induced by quantum multi-programming. The energies obtained at the first-, last- and ext-SQD iteration were compared to the classical Heat-bath Configuration Interaction (HCI) reference. Our findings highlight the viability of a quantum multi-programming workflow for quantum chemistry as the robust post-processing protocol effectively mitigates possible cross-talk induced noise. At the final step of the configuration recovery process, the energy difference relative to the HCI reference is negligible, within 0.001 kcal/mol.

</details>

### [Observation of an aperiodic polariton monotile](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13206v1)

**Authors:** Sergey Alyatkin, Yaroslav V. Kartashov, Kirill Sitnik, Philipp Grigoryev, Pavlos G. Lagoudakis

**Summary.** This paper reports the first experimental realization of an aperiodic polariton lattice using the recently discovered 'einstein' monotile. By sculpting these tiles in a GaAs microcavity, the authors demonstrate that the unique geometry leads to distinct spectral features and a complex phase-locking pattern unlike any periodic or conventional quasicrystalline system.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it demonstrates how a programmable, driven-dissipative artificial material can use aperiodicity to engineer complex, non-trivial synchronization and spectral responses.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to realize and investigate the physical properties of a newly discovered class of aperiodic quasicrystals composed of a single 'einstein' (hat) monotile, specifically focusing on how this unique geometry affects the propagation of coherent excitations and phase synchronization.

**Main result.** The researchers successfully imprinted 'einstein' monotile structures in an inorganic microcavity, observing six-fold symmetric Bragg peaks, Dirac-like spectral features, and a complex phase-locking pattern that is distinct from both periodic and Penrose quasicrystalline tilings.

**Method.** The study uses optical sculpting via a Spatial Light Modulator (SLM) to create the tiling, combined with energy-resolved momentum-space tomography and homodyne interferometry for phase reconstruction.

**Model / system.** The system is an inorganic GaAs-based microcavity hosting nonresonantly excited exciton-polariton condensates, modeled by the 2D Gross-Pitaevskii equation coupled to a reservoir rate equation.

**Key observables.** Bragg peaks, Dirac-like spectral fingerprints, real-space polariton density, and relative phases of the condensates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice constant (D) ranging from 22.6 to 27.2 micrometers, number of tiles (M = 1, 4, 13), and pump power relative to threshold (P approx 1.15 P_thr).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the driven-dissipative Gross-Pitaevskii framework for describing the polariton dynamics in the microcavity.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 compares different tiling geometries and shows real/reciprocal space PL for various tile numbers; Fig 2 presents 3D energy tomography and energy-resolved slices; Fig 3 compares phase locking patterns across periodic, Penrose, and 'einstein' lattices.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of the 'einstein' monotile, describes the experimental setup and optical sculpting technique, presents the spectral and spatial results of the aperiodic tiling, compares the phase synchronization of the monotile to other lattices, and concludes with numerical validation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A plethora of unconventional localization phenomena and fractal features of linear spectrum observed in quasiperiodic structures have been accompanied by a long-standing quest for the geometrical elements and structures that permit tilings of the plane, but only in a non-periodic manner. Until 2024, it was believed that such quasiperiodic structures, or quasicrystals, could only be composed of at least two different tiles. Surprisingly, a newly discovered class of quasicrystals requires only one elementary monotile. However, its physical realization and study of propagating coherent excitations in this novel setting remained elusive. Here we optically sculpt aperiodic quasicrystals composed of "einstein" monotiles in an inorganic microcavity and observe nontrivial relative phases of the exciton-polariton condensates nonresonantly excited at the vertices of each monotile. Utilizing energy-resolved tomography in momentum-space, we reveal the formation of distinct Bragg peaks with six-fold symmetry and Dirac-like spectral fingerprints, intrinsic to the underlying graphene-like structure, while interferometric phase reconstruction shows a nontrivial synchronization pattern distinct from both periodic triangular lattices and Penrose quasicrystals. Our work demonstrates that monotiles can be converted into a programmable driven-dissipative artificial material, where long-range coherence coexists with enforced geometric aperiodicity, producing synchronization and spectral responses distinct from both periodic and conventional quasicrystalline tilings.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Quantifying information flow along a stochastic trajectory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13509v1)

**Authors:** Yongjae Oh, Euijoon Kwon, Yongjoo Baek

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13509_figures/2605.13509_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) Histogram of time-integrated SIF JX versus stochastic area AXY of the two-particle model, collected for 106 trajectory segments with observation period τ = 1. The parameters used for the simulation are k = 2, K = 2, γ = 1, T = 2, and the discrete time step is fixed at ∆t = 0.001. The color bar indicates the number of trajectories inside each bin. (b) Covariance between the SIF and the stochas- tic area measured for 105 trajectories, as τ is varied. We also show the sample trajectories achieving the (c) minimum [JX(τ) = −4.472] and the (d) maximum [JX(τ) = 4.096] val- ues of JX(τ). The colors indicate the time evolution from the initial (blue) to the final state (red).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13509_figures/2605.13509_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. (a) Schematic of NESIF. ∆t = 0.01. (b, c) Test results of NESIF using the N-bead model with k = K = γ = T = 1. (b) Comparison between the exact scaled variance DJx1 (solid line) and the values inferred using the NESIF (symbols), as the dataset size M is varied. (c) The coefficient of determination R2 between Jx1(τ = 1) and ˆ Jx1(τ = 1). In (b) and (c), the error bars denote standard deviation of 10 independent trainings, and the lines are to guide the eye.</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a scalable deep-learning method called NESIF to quantify directed information flow along individual stochastic trajectories. It overcomes the computational bottlenecks of traditional measures, allowing for the detection of hidden cooperative structures in complex systems like oscillators and biological cells. The method also establishes a formal link between information flow and stochastic thermodynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The development of scalable, data-driven tools to quantify non-equilibrium information flow and entropy production is highly relevant for studying the dynamics of open quantum systems and many-body fluctuations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Quantifying directed information exchange at the trajectory level (Stochastic Information Flow) and overcoming the computational 'curse of dimensionality' when estimating these flows from high-dimensional time-series data.

**Main result.** The authors propose NESIF, a scalable deep-learning method that accurately estimates SIF, and demonstrate its ability to identify cooperative structures in Kuramoto oscillators and differentiate between cancerous and non-cancerous cell dynamics.

**Method.** A neural estimation approach (NESIF) using artificial neural networks and alpha-divergence (alpha-MINE) to estimate Stochastic Mutual Information from time-series data.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes several models: a two-particle Langevin system of overdamped Brownian particles, an N-bead harmonic chain, noisy Kuramoto oscillators, and empirical trajectories of interacting motile human epithelial cells.

**Key observables.** Stochastic Information Flow (SIF), Stochastic Mutual Information (SMI), scaled variance of SIF, entropy production rates, and stochastic area.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), coupling stiffness (K), observation period (tau), alpha-divergence parameter (alpha), and number of particles/beads (N).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The current NESIF method assumes the system is in a steady state and that noise in bipartite Langevin dynamics is independent.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the anticorrelation between SIF and stochastic area with trajectory visualizations; Figure 2 validates the NESIF estimator using an N-bead model; Figure 3 and 6 analyze SIF statistics and synchronization in Kuramoto oscillators; Figure 4 compares information exchange in cancerous vs. non-cancerous cells.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SIF concept, proposes the NESIF deep-learning estimator, validates it with analytical models (two-particle and N-bead), applies it to synchronization (Kuramoto), and finally demonstrates its utility on biological cell data.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stochastic information flow (SIF) quantifies information flow at the trajectory level, overcoming the limitations of conventional symmetric, ensemble-averaged measures. However, computational difficulties have hindered the empirical application of the SIF. In this work, we propose a scalable deep-learning method for estimating the SIF from general time-series data. Its applications to an exactly solvable two-particle model, Kuramoto oscillators, and empirical trajectories of interacting motile cells demonstrate the utility of SIF as a data-driven indicator of cooperative structures.

</details>

### [Ghost State of Light](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13584v1)

**Authors:** R. M. de Boer, C. Toebes, Jan Klars, S. R. K. Rodriguez  
**Type:** both · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13584v1>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how a nonlinear system with memory can be used to trap light in a 'ghost state' near a bifurcation point. By using the thermal relaxation of olive oil, the authors show that trajectories can be steered into phase-space bottlenecks, creating extremely long-lived intensity plateaus.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a way to experimentally access and control long-lived transient states in non-equilibrium systems using memory-induced steering, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and dissipative phase transitions.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of experimentally observing and accessing 'ghost states'—long-lived, non-stationary bottlenecks in phase space that occur near a saddle-node bifurcation.

**Main result.** The researchers identified that a nonlinear response with memory can steer relaxation pathways into the bottleneck, enabling the observation of ghost states with lifetimes exceeding the cavity photon lifetime by over ten orders of magnitude.

**Method.** The study combines experimental measurements in a microcavity with numerical simulations using the xSPDE toolbox and an overdamped Langevin equation approach.

**Model / system.** A plano-concave Fabry-Perot microcavity filled with olive oil, which provides a thermo-optical nonlinearity with a time-delayed (memory) response, driven by a continuous-wave laser.

**Key observables.** Cavity transmission/intensity, plateau duration (ghost lifetime), and phase-space velocity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Memory time (tau), laser-cavity detuning (Delta), dissipation rate (Gamma), and nonlinearity strength (U).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The simulations use a simplified memory time shorter than the experimental value to manage computational costs, and the 1D effective potential approximation assumes dynamics are primarily along the nullcline.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows experimental and simulated transmission plateaus; Fig 2 displays scaling laws for plateau duration; Fig 3 illustrates relaxation trajectories in the complex plane; Fig 6 provides a parameter space map of the bifurcation.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to ghost states and the bottleneck problem, description of the experimental microcavity setup, presentation of the stochastic model and simulations, analysis of scaling laws and memory effects, and discussion of the implications for non-stationary states.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report the observation of a long-lived non-stationary state of light in a single-mode optical cavity. The observed state is a ghost of a saddle-node bifurcation which creates a bottleneck in phase space. While such ghosts are known to exist, accessing them is challenging because it requires a mechanism that steers the relaxation pathway away from the true attractor and into the bottleneck where the ghost emerges. Here we identify such a mechanism, namely a nonlinear response with memory. Our experimental system leverages this mechanism, enabling us to observe ghost states with lifetimes exceeding the cavity photon lifetime by more than ten orders of magnitude, even in the presence of strong fluctuations. The ghost manifests as a plateau in the relaxation dynamics of the cavity transmission, reminiscent of prethermalization. We show how the ghost lifetime depends on the memory time and the distance to the bifurcation, and we observe signatures of scaling in the distribution of ghost lifetimes at fixed driving conditions. Our work establishes minimal conditions for realizing parametrically long-lived non-stationary states.

</details>

### [Universal Design and Physical Applications of Non-Uniform Cellular Automata on Translationally Invariant Lattices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13379v1)

**Authors:** Xiang-You Huang, Jie-Yu Zhang, Peng Ye  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13379v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1. Schematic overview of the NUCA algorithm. Di- verse physical applications of our algorithm are demonstrated. Through a linear NUCA, we generate quantum states associated with subsystem symmetries in Sec. **Summary.** The paper introduces a universal algorithm for non-uniform cellular automata that works on both Euclidean and hyperbolic lattices. By using a lattice-deforming procedure, the authors successfully model complex physical phenomena such as subsystem symmetries and directed percolation in hyperbolic spaces.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and quantum information as it provides a new mathematical framework to study topological order and phase transitions in non-Euclidean geometries, which could impact the development of hyperbolic quantum error-correcting codes.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Standard uniform cellular automata (CA) are incompatible with hyperbolic lattices due to their non-Abelian translation symmetry and exponential growth, making it difficult to model physical phenomena like subsystem symmetries and directed percolation on these geometries.

**Main result.** The authors develop a universal higher-order non-uniform cellular automata (NUCA) algorithm that applies to both Euclidean and hyperbolic lattices, enabling the generation of subsystem symmetry-protected topological (SSPT) states and the simulation of directed percolation.

**Method.** A lattice-deforming procedure embeds hyperbolic lattices into Euclidean square lattices using a splitting method and coordinate-scaling functions to capture geometric data through non-uniform update rules.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes various models including linear NUCA, non-uniform Clifford quantum cellular automata (CQCA), and probabilistic NUCA applied to regular Euclidean and hyperbolic lattices (specifically {5,4} and {6,6} lattices).

**Key observables.** Subsystem symmetries (regular and irregular), subsystem symmetry-protected topological (SSPT) states, multi-point strange correlators, and directed percolation thresholds.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice geometry (Schläfli symbols {p,q}), curvature, update rule order, and the probability parameter in probabilistic NUCA.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The algorithm assumes the ability to embed hyperbolic lattices into a Euclidean square lattice via a splitting method, which introduces geometric distortion that must be compensated for by the non-uniform rules.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of the NUCA algorithm and its applications; Figures 2-6 illustrate lattice geometries and the lattice-deforming procedure; Figures 14-22 show the construction of cluster states, symmetry patterns, and phase diagrams for directed percolation.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by introducing the NUCA algorithm and the lattice-deforming procedure, then moves to applications in quantum topology (SSPT and CQCA), followed by applications in non-equilibrium statistical physics (directed percolation), and concludes with discussions on complex {6,6} lattices.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Lattice geometry profoundly shapes physical phenomena such as subsystem symmetry and directed percolation (DP). Among various lattice geometries, hyperbolic lattices are characterized by constant negative curvature and non-Abelian translation symmetry, offering a rich platform for investigating this geometry-physics interplay. However, the exponentially growing lattice size and nontrivial translation symmetry make approaches developed for Euclidean lattices incompatible, a limitation particularly evident in uniform cellular automata (CA). To resolve this, we develop a higher-order non-uniform cellular automata (NUCA) algorithm applicable to both translationally invariant regular Euclidean and hyperbolic lattices. In the algorithm, the non-uniform update rules incorporate nontrivial geometric data through a lattice-deforming procedure. We demonstrate the broad applicability of our algorithm to hyperbolic lattices through several applications on the hyperbolic $\{5,4\}$ lattice. By applying a linear NUCA, we generate subsystem symmetry-protected topological (SSPT) states and spontaneous subsystem symmetry-breaking states associated with regular or irregular subsystem symmetries unattainable on Euclidean lattices. We design the multi-point strange correlators to detect nontrivial SSPT states and derive a sufficient condition for non-Abelian translationally invariant NUCA-generated models. Furthermore, by generalizing the NUCA to non-uniform Clifford quantum cellular automata (CQCA), we generate subsystem symmetries of the hyperbolic cluster state, extending the established correspondence between translationally invariant CQCA and subsystem symmetries. Moreover, we simulate the DP process via a probabilistic NUCA that inherits the treelike structure of the lattice, and numerically estimate percolation thresholds and the phase diagram.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13556"></a>
### [Emergence of information interference in stochastic systems with non-diagonal noise and switching environments](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13556v1)

**Authors:** Andrea Marchetti, Daniel Maria Busiello, Giorgio Nicoletti  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13556v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

When τ ≫τjumps, the environment is much faster than the DOFs and the MI converges to a value that only depends on the noise and drift matrices in the various environmental states. As the environment becomes slower, the MI increases due to the additional dependencies induced by the shared temperature switch and converges to the value...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13556_figures/2605.13556_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. (a) Two identical particles (r1 = r2 = r = 1) immersed in a shared fluid, which induces a hydrodynamic coupling regulated by the dimensionless parameter γ. The particles interact non-reciprocally through the external force, with β quantifying the interaction asymmetry. (b) As γ in- creases, so does the MI between the particles Iint, with a non- monotonic behavior as a function of β. (c) The presence of the fluid induces a non-diagonal noise matrix and leads to the emergence of static information interference Ξint. Such inter- ference can be both constructive, signalling that the hydro- dynamic coupling is enhancing information, or destructive, when it masks information. We require...</figcaption>
</figure>

(b) The noise anisotropy induces a static information interference in the MI between X and Y , whose sign is determined by both ∆µ and the imbalance in the energy barriers α. **Summary.** This paper explores how anisotropic noise and changing environments create 'information interference,' preventing the simple summation of individual physical contributions to a system's mutual information. By studying hydrodynamic and chemical systems, the authors demonstrate that these interplays can either enhance or mask statistical dependencies. This establishes a new way to quantify how complex environments shape information in stochastic processes.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to open quantum systems and many-body dynamics as it provides a general information-theoretic perspective on how correlated noise and environmental fluctuations fundamentally alter the statistical dependencies and information flow in non-equilibrium systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how non-diagonal noise (anisotropy) and stochastically switching environments lead to 'information interference,' where total mutual information cannot be decomposed into simple additive contributions from deterministic interactions, noise, and environmental changes.

**Main result.** The authors identify two sources of interference: a static term from the interplay between deterministic coupling and noise anisotropy, and a dynamic term from the interplay between internal processes and environmental switches. They also show that hydrodynamic interactions can either mask or enhance information depending on the degree of non-reciprocity.

**Method.** The study uses an information-theoretic framework based on Mutual Information (MI) and Kullback-Leibler divergence applied to multivariate Ornstein-Uhlenbeck processes, utilizing matrix decomposition and timescale separation (fast/slow-jump limits).

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to linearized stochastic systems, specifically including hydrodynamically coupled Brownian particles (using the Rotne-Prager-Yamakawa tensor) and fuel-driven chemical reaction networks modeled via the Linear Noise Approximation.

**Key observables.** Mutual Information (MI), Information Interference (static and dynamic terms), and entropy of the jump process.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Deterministic coupling strength (g), noise anisotropy (a), non-reciprocity (beta), environmental switching rates (tau_jumps), and chemical potential (delta_mu).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to linearized stochastic systems with Gaussian steady-state solutions and assumes the noise covariance matrix is positive definite.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows MI estimates vs. the ratio of internal to environmental timescales; Figure 2 illustrates the setup of particles with hydrodynamic coupling and non-reciprocal forces; Figure 3 depicts a chemical reaction cycle with driving forces.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of information interference, develops a mathematical framework for decomposing MI in the presence of non-diagonal noise and switching environments, and then applies this framework to three distinct physical cases: temperature switching, hydrodynamic interactions, and chemical reaction networks.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stochastic forces in natural systems are rarely isotropic. From hydrodynamically coupled colloids to chemical reaction networks, noise contributions are inherently correlated. Together with internal interactions and changing environments, they shape the dependencies between the degrees of freedom of real-world systems, as quantified by their mutual information. In this work, we focus on linearized stochastic systems with both non-diagonal noise matrices and stochastically switching environments. We study how their presence leads to the emergence of information interference, so that the total mutual information cannot be decomposed as the sum of the contributions from deterministic interactions, noise anisotropy, and environmental switching alone. We identify two distinct sources of information interference: a static term, arising from the simultaneous presence of deterministic coupling and noise anisotropy; and a dynamic term, emerging from the interplay between internal processes and environmental switches. We then apply this framework to different physical systems. In the presence of switching temperatures, the mutual information disentangles exactly into internal and environmental contributions. When the noise anisotropy arises instead from hydrodynamic interactions, we find that the presence of a shared fluid can either mask or enhance the information stemming from a non-conservative force depending on its degree of non-reciprocity. Finally, in a fuel-driven chemical reaction network, we show that information interference is controlled by the non-equilibrium driving. These results establish a general information-theoretic perspective on how anisotropic noise and environmental variability shape statistical dependencies in stochastic systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12955"></a>
### [Microscopic Origins of Collapse Models: Decoherence from Graviton Bremsstrahlung](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12955v1)

**Authors:** Moslem Zarei  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12955v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12955_figures/2605.12955_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Graviton Bremsstrahlung process from a fermion in a spatial superposition state.</figcaption>

<img src="2605.12955_figures/2605.12955_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Contour plot of the gravitationally induced decoherence time τdec = Γ−1 as a function of the total mass M and the number of constituents N. The decoherence rate scales as Γ ∝N 2, reflecting the coherent enhancement of graviton emission in composite systems, while finite-size effects introduce an exponential suppression through the ratio of the object size to the graviton coherence length. The dashed vertical line indicates the electron mass, highlighting the regime where single particle quantum coherence is preserved. In contrast, increasing N at fixed or growing mass leads to a rapid reduction of coherence time, illustrating the transition from microscopic quantum behavior to...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that the emission of gravitons (Bremsstrahlung) during particle motion provides a microscopic origin for wave function collapse. It demonstrates that while individual gravitational interactions are weak, the collective effect in large masses leads to rapid decoherence and the emergence of classical behavior.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental link between quantum field theory and the emergence of classicality, offering a microscopic derivation for the decoherence-induced suppression of superpositions that is central to the study of open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the wave function collapse described by phenomenological models (like CSL or Diósi-Penrose) can be derived from a microscopic quantum field-theoretic framework, specifically through graviton-induced decoherence.

**Main result.** The paper derives a decoherence rate from graviton Bremsstrahlung that scales quadratically with the number of constituents and mass, providing a mechanism for the emergence of classicality in macroscopic systems.

**Method.** The author utilizes the Quantum Boltzmann Equation (QBE) and the S-matrix to analyze the interaction between a fermion and a graviton bath, applying the Born-Markov and weak-field approximations.

**Model / system.** A spin-1/2 fermionic spinor field in a spatial superposition interacting with soft graviton degrees of freedom (tensor field) via a graviton Bremsstrahlung process.

**Key observables.** The decay of the off-diagonal coherence element (rho_12) of the density matrix and the decoherence rate (Gamma).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spatial separation (delta x), particle mass (m_f), gravitational coupling (kappa), number of constituents (N), and the graviton spectral density.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Weak-field limit of General Relativity, Born-Markov approximation, non-relativistic regime for the fermion, and an isotropic, unpolarized graviton bath.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the graviton Bremsstrahlung process for a fermion in superposition; Figure 2 is a contour plot showing decoherence time as a function of total mass and number of constituents.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the field operators and interaction Hamiltonian, progresses to constructing the QBE collision term, derives the decoherence rate and scaling laws, and concludes by discussing implications for collapse models and experimental tests.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Some collapse models proposed that gravitational effects cause the instability of mass distribution superpositions, leading to wave function collapse. In this paper, we utilize the quantum Boltzmann equation (QBE) to analyze the behavior of a fermion in a spatial superposition under graviton emission. We introduce a quantitative measure that links the stability of the superposition to the spatial separation, particle mass, and gravitational coupling. By examining the collision term in the QBE, we derive the decoherence rate and show how it depends on these parameters. Our results provide a detailed framework for understanding gravity induced decoherence, bridging the gap between quantum field theory and collapse models. We also discuss the implications of these findings for experimental tests of gravitationally induced wave function collapse and the broader class of collapse models known as dissipative continuous spontaneous localization (CSL) model.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13466"></a>

**Summary.** The paper investigates unexpected signal narrowing and nonlinear effects in cesium vapor under strong spin-exchange conditions. It demonstrates that these phenomena, including bistability and memory, stem from the spontaneous polarization of atoms. This discovery suggests a way to use atomic vapors as amplifiers for high-precision quantum sensing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It explores spontaneous symmetry breaking and nonlinear collective dynamics in an open quantum system, providing a geometric mechanism for signal amplification and ultra-narrow resonances relevant to quantum sensing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the origin of anomalous anisotropy, ultra-narrow resonance widths, and nonlinear phenomena like bistability and hysteresis in cesium vapor under strong spin-exchange conditions.

**Main result.** The study identifies that ultra-narrow alignment resonances arise from the projection of spontaneous transverse orientation onto the detection axis, effectively acting as an amplifier for alignment signals.

**Method.** Experimental 2D magnetic field mapping and polarization rotation measurements in Cs vapor, complemented by a theoretical model incorporating spontaneous polarization and nonlinear dynamics.

**Model / system.** Cesium (Cs) vapor in a nitrogen buffer gas at high pressure, operating in the Spin-Exchange Relaxation-Free (SERF) regime under linearly polarized optical pumping.

**Key observables.** Polarization rotation signal (Sb), transmission signal (St), alignment (quadrupole moment), and orientation (dipole moment).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin-exchange rate (Rse), Larmor frequency, temperature (77-103 C), and magnetic field components (Bx, By, Bz).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theoretical model is a minimal version using only the lowest relevant tensor moments and treats spontaneous polarization as a coherent process without additional dissipation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental setup; Figure 2 displays polarization rotation signals at different temperatures; Figure 3 shows transmission signals; Figure 6 illustrates the formation of alignment components.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the anomalous anisotropy, describes the experimental setup and high-density regime, presents the observed nonlinearities (bistability/hystricsis), proposes a theoretical model based on spontaneous polarization, and concludes with the geometric interpretation of the signals.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present the results of an experimental study of the anomalous anisotropy of alignment signals in cesium vapors under strong spin exchange conditions in zero magnetic fields under linearly polarized optical pumping. We show that the anisotropy of the Hanle resonances in the plane perpendicular to the pump beam increases sharply with increasing concentration. In one direction, the resonance widths are determined by classical spin exchange, while in the other, by the SERF (Spin-Exchange Relaxation Free) effect. With further concentration increases, additional nonlinear effects arise, such as an increase of the normalized signal amplitude, effective magnetic field, bistability, hysteresis, and memory. To explain these observations, as well as the results presented in our previous studies, we construct a demonstration theoretical model incorporating spontaneous polarization effects arising under strong spin exchange. The model qualitatively shows that the experimentally observed ultra-narrow alignment resonances may originate predominantly from quadrupole anisotropy associated with spontaneous transverse orientation projected onto the detection axis.The unique properties of these resonances, such as their ultra-small width and magnetic field-controlled bistability with a long-term memory effect, make them promising for use in quantum sensing and information.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that the charging speed of a quantum battery can be optimized by tuning the system's parameters to approach an exceptional point in its Liouvillian spectrum. By maximizing the spectral gap, the researchers achieve faster relaxation to the charged steady state. This approach provides a way to enhance energy storage efficiency using single-atom platforms like trapped ions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental link between non-Hermitian physics (exceptional points) and quantum thermodynamics, offering a specific mechanism (spectral engineering) to accelerate dissipative processes without needing many-body effects.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the steady-state charging performance of quantum batteries by controlling the relaxation dynamics and the Liouvillian spectral gap in open quantum systems.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrate that the asymptotic charging power can be significantly enhanced by engineering the Liouvillian spectrum to approach an exceptional point, which maximizes the spectral gap.

**Method.** Liouvillian spectral analysis using a Lindblad master equation, involving dimensionality reduction of the Liouvillian operator and investigation of eigenvalue coalescence.

**Model / system.** A three-level quantum battery model realized in a single trapped 40Ca+ ion, where energy is transferred from a thermal photon reservoir to a long-lived metastable storage state.

**Key observables.** Steady-state energy, relaxation time, average charging power, Liouvillian spectral gap, and l1 coherence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Thermal photon occupation (Nth), coherent coupling strength (Rabi frequency Omega), decay rates, and detuning.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dynamics are analyzed in the limit where the metastable state decay is much slower than other processes, allowing for a reduced-dimensional slow-sector description; use of Born-Markov and rotating-wave approximations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows the separation of the Liouvillian spectrum into slow and fast branches; Figure 6 compares steady-state properties like energy and coherence against charging power; Figure 7 demonstrates the robustness of the optimal charging regime across different convergence thresholds; Figure 8 shows the dependence of the spectral gap on detuning.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the quantum battery problem, defines the three-level ion model and Lindblad dynamics, performs a spectral decomposition of the Liouvillian to isolate slow-sector dynamics, analyzes the role of exceptional points in maximizing the spectral gap, and concludes with the robustness and implications for energy storage.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum batteries, which use quantum systems to store and deliver energy,   are promising for next-generation energy storage. However, optimizing charging   strategies and understanding the interplay between dissipation and quantum   coherence remain open challenges. Here, we investigate steady-state charging   in an open quantum battery and demonstrate that the charging timescale depends   on the spectral gap of the Liouvillian operator governing dissipative dynamics.   As a minimal example, we examine a three-level quantum battery realized in   a single trapped ${}^{40}\mathrm{Ca}^{+}$ ion, where energy from an   engineered thermal photon reservoir is coherently transferred to a long-lived   metastable storage state. We find that long-term dynamics are confined to   a low-dimensional manifold of slow Liouvillian modes, with their   spectral structure determining the relaxation rate to the charged   steady state. By adjusting experimentally accessible parameters,   such as reservoir occupation and coherent coupling strength, the non-Hermitian   Liouvillian spectrum can approach an exceptional point. This increases   the spectral gap and accelerates the approach to steady state. As a result,   this mechanism significantly enhances asymptotic charging power without   relying on many-body collectivity or steady coherence. Our findings offer   fundamental insights into open quantum thermodynamics and provide a path   to efficient energy storage and fast-charging solutions in emerging quantum   technologies.

</details>

### [Observation of end-to-end pumping in a quasiperiodic Fibonacci-type photonic chain](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13116v1)

**Authors:** Arnob Kumar Ghosh, Ang Chen, Ashraf El Hassan, Patric Holmvall, Mohamed Bourennane, Annica M. Black-Schaffer  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13116v1>

**Summary.** The researchers developed a simplified method to pump light from one end of a quasiperiodic photonic chain to the other by only adjusting two waveguides. This approach is highly robust against structural defects and avoids the need for complex, system-wide tuning. This discovery provides a viable platform for reliable state transfer in topological photonic networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is relevant for quantum optics and many-body dynamics as it demonstrates a simplified, robust method for state transfer in quasiperiodic systems, which could serve as a blueprint for connecting non-neighboring elements in quantum networks or photonic circuits.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for an efficient and robust end-to-end pumping protocol in topological systems that does not require continuous, fine-tuned control of all bond strengths along the entire chain.

**Main result.** Demonstrated that a localized pumping state can be transferred between opposite ends of a quasiperiodic Fibonacci-type photonic chain by modulating only two outer bonds, a process that remains robust against structural defects.

**Method.** The study uses a tight-binding formalism for numerical simulations and an experimental platform consisting of an array of coupled optical waveguides fabricated via femtosecond laser direct writing.

**Model / system.** A 1D quasiperiodic Fibonacci-type photonic chain implemented in an array of 11 coupled optical waveguides, where light propagation is described by a tight-binding Hamiltonian with coupling strengths determined by waveguide spacing.

**Key observables.** Light intensity distribution at the output facet, eigenvalue spectra as a function of the phason angle, and the excitation gap between the pumping state and adjacent eigenstates.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of waveguides (N=11), characteristic distances (dA=10 um, dB=7 um), phason angle (phi), and coupling strength (J=63.5 mm^-1).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The paraxial approximation is used to map Maxwell's equations to the Schrodinger equation, and waveguide diameters are assumed to be uniform to neglect onsite terms.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the waveguide array schematic and tight-binding representation; Fig 2 displays eigenvalue spectra and spatial distribution of the pumping state; Fig 4 shows the experimental setup; Fig 5 compares theory and experiment for light transfer; Fig 6 demonstrates robustness against structural defects.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of efficient pumping, presents the numerical tight-binding model and the proposed simplified protocol, details the experimental waveguide fabrication and implementation, demonstrates successful end-to-end transfer, and concludes with tests of robustness against defects and future directions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological pumps offer a promising route to operate as connecting buses, supplying efficient and robust connectivity between non-neighboring elements in a network. Here, we investigate a finite quasiperiodic Fibonacci-type photonic chain and demonstrate its ability for end-to-end pumping, with only small and simple changes to the system. First, we use a tight-binding formalism to numerically show that a localized pumping state can be transferred between opposite ends of the system, with only a small structural change to the chain. Then, we experimentally implement this topological pump in an array of coupled optical waveguides, where light propagation is effectively described by the tight-binding model under the paraxial approximation, enabling direct correspondence between theory and experiment. We numerically simulate and experimentally demonstrate pumping by injecting light into a single waveguide at one end of the setup, which activates a localized pumping state. As the light propagates along the wave guide array, it is also pumped to the other end. We further show that pumping remains robust against structural deformation, such as controlled defects in the waveguide array. Our results establish that quasiperiodic Fibonacci-type photonic lattices are a robust and experimentally viable platform for disorder-resilient state transfer.

</details>

### [Grassmann tensor networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12907v1)

**Authors:** Jian-Gang Kong, Jia-Ji Zhu, Z. Y. Xie  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12907v1>
