**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive introduction and a new unified framework for Grassmann tensor networks. It demonstrates how to adapt standard tensor network algorithms to handle fermionic statistics locally, offering a promising route for simulating complex 2D fermionic systems and lattice gauge theories.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a powerful new toolkit for simulating fermionic many-body dynamics and 2D systems by bypassing the non-local string operators of Jordan-Wigner transformations and the sign problem of Monte Carlo.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the need for efficient, non-perturbative numerical methods to study strongly correlated fermionic systems without the sign problem inherent in Monte Carlo simulations.

**Main result.** The authors establish a unified, self-contained framework for Grassmann tensor networks (GTNs) and demonstrate their validity through the 'Grassmannization' of standard algorithms like DMRG, TEBD, and CTMRG.

**Method.** The method utilizes Grassmann algebra within a coherent-state path-integral representation, allowing for local handling of fermion exchange statistics and sign factors.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to various models including the Hubbard and t-J models, the Anderson impurity model, the honeycomb lattice t-V1-V2 model, and (1+1)D Gross-Neveu-Wilson models.

**Key observables.** Physical observables, partition functions, wave functions, particle density, and ground-state energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bond dimension (D), environment dimension (chi), chemical potential (mu), and interaction strengths (t, J, V1, V2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The validity of the contraction depends on matching parity of shared indices; the ordering of Grassmann variables is assumed to match the index ordering of the tensors.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the SVD decomposition of a Grassmann tensor; Figure 9 and 10 show convergence of energy and particle density in the t-V1-V2 model compared to DMRG data.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a pedagogical progression: starting with the basics of Grassmann algebra and operations, moving to tensor network representations and 'Grassmannization' of algorithms, and concluding with numerical validation on various physical models.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Developing non-perturbative methods to reveal exotic properties of strongly correlated fermionic systems remains one of the most essential tasks of theoretical physics. Tensor network methods with Grassmann algebra offer powerful numerical tools for fermionic many-body systems in the coherent-state path-integral representation. Despite their vast potential for both condensed-matter and particle-physics communities, Grassmann tensor network methods are somewhat underexploited in practical simulations. In this work, we provide a detailed, self-contained introduction to Grassmann tensor network methods, from the basics of the Grassmann tensor operations to the Grassmannization of typical tensor network algorithms. Furthermore, the resulting Grassmann tensor network methods are validated in several interesting models in both particle physics and condensed matter physics.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13606"></a>
### [Lieb-Schultz-Mattis theorem from gauge constraints](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13606v1)

**Authors:** Bhandaru Phani Parasar  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13606v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13606_figures/2605.13606_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) - The one-dimensional chain on which the model is defined. Sites are shown in cyan, while the links are depicted in magenta. As per the convention used, the link jτ connects the sites jσ and j + 1σ. The hopping terms hα j are defined on the links and the gauge transformations Aα j′ are defined on the sites. (b) - In the Gauss law subspace, for any given site, the states on the site and the two links touching it must be all different. For example, the configuration shown on the left is valid, but the one on the right is not allowed as the states on the two links are the same.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper shows that the kinematic constraints of a gauge theory can act as a source for a Lieb-Schultz-Mattis theorem, preventing a trivial gapped ground state. By studying a Z2 x Z2 gauge theory, the authors identify a critical point described by free Dirac fermions with a central charge of c=1. This demonstrates that gauge-induced symmetries can fundamentally dictate the phase diagram of a quantum system.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a novel mechanism for generating topological constraints (LSM theorems) from gauge-induced symmetries, which is highly relevant to the study of constrained dynamics and many-body physics.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the kinematic constraints of a gauge theory (Gauss law) can generate a Lieb-Schultz-Mattis (LSM) type theorem.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that the Gauss law in a Z2 x Z2 gauge theory induces a U(1) symmetry that anticommutes with lattice reflection, thereby ruling out a trivial gapped ground state and forcing either gaplessness or spontaneous symmetry breaking.

**Method.** The study employs algebraic mapping of the gauge-constrained Hilbert space, Jordan-Wigner transformation to a fermionic representation, Toeplitz determinant analysis for correlations, and DMRG for numerical verification of the central charge.

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional periodic chain of spin-1 operators subject to a Z2 x Z2 gauge theory with a local Gauss law constraint.

**Key observables.** Ground state energy, bipartite entanglement entropy, central charge (c=1), and two-point correlation functions of gauge-invariant quantities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hopping amplitudes (t_alpha), potential strengths (K_alpha), and the thermodynamic limit (L -> infinity).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes periodic boundary conditions and relies on the validity of the Fisher-Hartwig conjecture for asymptotic correlation decay.

**Figures summary.** A plot of entanglement entropy versus subsystem size used to extract the central charge.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by constructing the gauge theory and Gauss law subspace, moves to deriving the effective Hamiltonian and its symmetries, proves the LSM theorem via variational methods, analyzes the critical point using fermion mapping and Toeplitz determinants, and concludes with numerical verification via DMRG.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We construct a $\mathbb{Z}_2 \times \mathbb{Z}_2$ gauge theory coupled to matter on a one-dimensional chain, aiming to study the ground-state physics in the Gauss law subspace. We show that the theory in the Gauss law subspace has a U$(1)$ symmetry whose generator commutes with lattice translations, but anticommutes with the lattice reflection operator. This leads to a Lieb-Schultz-Mattis (LSM) theorem that always rules out a trivial gapped ground state in the Gauss law subspace, if the hamiltonian is invariant under translations and reflection. Any point in the parameter space must realize a either a spontaneously symmetry broken (SSB) ground state, or a gapless ground state. Imposing the Gauss law is pivotal for the existence of the U$(1)$ symmetry, and hence of the LSM theorem. We thus demonstrate a novel mechanism to obtain an LSM-type theorem, wherein the symmetry responsible for the theorem originates from the kinematic constraints of a gauge theory. We identify a point in the parameter space at which the system is gapless. At the gapless point, the excitations admit a description in terms of free Dirac fermions with a constraint on the total fermion number. The asymptotic behavior of the two-point correlation function of the simplest local gauge-invariant quantity at the gapless point is found to be $ \propto \cos{(πr)}\,r^{-2/9}$, where $r$ is the lattice separation between the two points. This model is also a natural platform to study phase diagram topological defects residing in families of SSB phases.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13561"></a>
### [Metastable Hyperuniformity at Discontinuous Absorbing Transitions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13561v1)

**Authors:** Yusheng Lei, Ran Ni  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13561v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

(E) Structure factor of the metastable active states near the stability limit in Facilitated Manna model with system size L =...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13561_figures/2605.13561_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

Structure factor near metastable active states. Inset: Steady state ac- tive particle fraction fa as a function of particle density ρ.</figcaption>

Inset: Steady state active particle fraction fa as a function of particle density ρ.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper identifies a new type of 'metastable hyperuniformity' that appears near the edge of stability in discontinuous absorbing phase transitions. It demonstrates that this structural signature is a universal feature arising from the interplay of nonlinear activation and demographic noise, rather than specific microscopic rules.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether structural order, specifically hyperuniformity, exists near discontinuous absorbing phase transitions and seeks to identify the underlying physical mechanism.

**Main result.** The authors discover a universal, metastable hyperuniform regime near the stability limit of discontinuous absorbing transitions, characterized by an anomalous scaling of the structure factor S(k) ~ k^1.2 in both 2D and 3D.

**Method.** The research employs numerical simulations of a facilitated Manna model and develops a minimal conserved Reggeon field theory using the Doi-Peliti formalism and mean-field analysis.

**Model / system.** The system is a facilitated Manna model on a d-dimensional lattice involving active and passive particles with a conserved total density, where activity is driven by a density-dependent facilitation mechanism.

**Key observables.** Structure factor S(k), active particle fraction (f_a), and the density field (rho).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The stability limit of the metastable active phase, the scaling exponent (1.2), and the dimensionality (2D and 3D).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The field theory derivation relies on an adiabatic approximation where the active density is treated as a fast variable slaved to the slow conserved field, and certain higher-order diffusive noise terms are neglected.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1(A) shows the model schematic; 1(B) shows the discontinuous jump in active fraction; 1(C) shows waiting time scaling; 1(D) shows the time evolution of active particles; 1(E/F) shows the structure factor scaling.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of structural order in absorbing transitions, presents numerical evidence from a facilitated Manna model, develops a corresponding Reggeon field theory to extract the physical mechanism, and concludes with a noise hierarchy analysis.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonequilibrium hyperuniformity can arise either as a steady-state property of driven active fluids or as a critical signature at continuous absorbing transition points in two and three dimensions. Whether analogous structural order exists near discontinuous absorbing transitions, and what mechanism generates it, remains unclear. Here, we show that discontinuous absorbing transitions generically host a metastable hyperuniform regime near the stability limit. Using a facilitated Manna model without center-of-mass conservation, we find anomalous scaling $S(k\to0)\sim k^{1.2}$, which appears only near the metastable regime and disappears both deep in the active phase and in the absorbing phase. This scaling is robust in both two and three dimensions, in contrast to critical hyperuniformity at continuous absorbing transitions. We further formulate a minimal conserved Reggeon field theory that reproduces the same metastable hyperuniform regime and anomalous scaling, demonstrating that the phenomenon does not rely on microscopic update rules but arises from the interplay of nonlinear activation, multiplicative demographic noise, and conserved diffusive fluctuations. These results identify metastable hyperuniformity as a generic pseudo-critical structural signature of discontinuous absorbing transitions coupled to a conserved density.

</details>

### [Optimal Quantum Illumination with Nonlocal Non-Gaussian Operations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13747v1)

**Summary.** The paper proposes a nonlocal non-Gaussian photon addition protocol to enhance quantum illumination. It demonstrates that this method provides a significant advantage in target detection sensitivity and is more robust to photon loss and more resource-efficient than existing local non-Gaussian methods.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates how nonlocal non-Gaussian operations can engineer robust entanglement for sensing tasks in the presence of decoherence and loss.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Enhancing the performance of Quantum Illumination (QI) for detecting weakly reflecting targets in noisy environments by improving upon standard Two-Mode Squeezed State (TMSS) and existing local non-Gaussian protocols.

**Main result.** The proposed Nonlocal Non-Gaussian Photon Addition (NLPA) protocol outperforms local non-Gaussian operations (PS, PA, PC) and the TMSS baseline, offering a significant SNR enhancement and superior robustness to photon loss while maintaining high success probabilities.

**Method.** The authors use continuous-variable quantum information theory, evaluating error probabilities via the Helstrom limit and Quantum Chernoff Bound, and benchmarking protocols using the error exponent ratio (gain parameter) and von Neumann entropy.

**Model / system.** A bipartite quantum illumination system consisting of a signal mode sent to a weakly reflecting target (low transmissivity kappa) and a retained idler mode, interacting with a thermal background environment.

**Key observables.** Error probability (Pe), Signal-to-Noise Ratio (SNR), von Neumann entropy (Ev), success probability (Psucc), and the gain parameter (G).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target reflectivity (kappa), thermal photon number (n_th), squeezing parameter (r), beam splitter transmissivity (T), and photon loss (eta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The target reflectivity is assumed to be very low (kappa << 1), the environment is modeled as a thermal state, and the large-K limit is approximated using the Quantum Chernoff Bound.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 & 2 show protocol schematics; Fig 3 shows error probability vs. number of copies; Fig 4 shows gain ratio vs. transmissivity; Fig 6 shows error probability under different regimes; Fig 7 shows gain vs. transmissivity in lossless cases; Fig 8 & 9 show performance and gain under photon loss.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QI problem, describes the NLPA and local non-Gaussian protocols, quantifies entanglement and success probability, compares detection performance (error probability and gain) in lossless and lossy regimes, and evaluates different receiver strategies.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Enhancing quantum illumination with highly entangled probes remains an active area of research. In this context, non-Gaussian operations provide an effective route for engineering probe states that can surpass the standard two-mode squeezed state (TMSS). In this work, we investigate a specific nonlocal non-Gaussian operation protocol and show that the engineered state using this protocol outperforms previously considered local non-Gaussian scenarios, engineered based on photon catalysis, addition, and subtraction under realistic conditions, including photon loss. Furthermore, by employing a $50{:}50$ beam splitter with photon-number difference detection, we demonstrate a significant enhancement in the signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) for target detection relative to the TMSS. Thus, our protocol exhibits improved performance, highlighting a resource-efficient and experimentally feasible probe for enhanced quantum illumination.

</details>

### [Quantum dynamics of two $XX$ interacting PT-symmetric non-Hermitian qubits: enhancement of quantum annealing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13008v1)

**Authors:** Yana Komissarova, Mikhail V. Fistul, Ilya M. Eremin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13008v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper explores how PT-symmetric non-Hermiticity can be used to enhance quantum adiabatic annealing. By introducing a small amount of gain and loss into a two-qubit system, the researchers show that the probability of reaching the target ground state can be significantly increased compared to the standard Hermitian case.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel way to use non-Hermitian physics (gain and loss) as a resource to improve the performance of quantum annealing protocols, which is highly relevant for open quantum systems and quantum control.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether introducing PT-symmetric non-Hermitian terms into the Hamiltonian can enhance the efficiency of Quantum Adiabatic Annealing (QAA) by mitigating the effects of small energy gaps.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that adding even small PT-symmetric non-Hermitian terms can significantly enhance the probability of reaching the ground state during annealing, effectively mitigating Landau-Zener-Stuckelberg tunneling failures.

**Method.** Theoretical analysis of stationary and time-dependent Hamiltonians using a 4x4 matrix approach, reduction to an effective 2x2 model, and numerical solution of the time-dependent Schrodinger equation.

**Model / system.** A minimal model consisting of two interacting XX-coupled PT-symmetric non-Hermitian qubits subject to a time-dependent annealing Hamiltonian with a staggered imaginary longitudinal magnetic field.

**Key observables.** Probability of reaching the ground state (P_gr), population probabilities (P_up, P_down), and the energy spectrum (real and imaginary parts of eigenvalues).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gain/loss parameter (gamma), annealing speed (k), qubit bias (epsilon), interaction strength (g), and transition amplitude (Delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study uses a minimal two-qubit model, assumes all individual qubits are identical, and assumes the non-Hermitian part of the Hamiltonian remains constant during the annealing process.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the two-qubit system; Figure 2 displays the real part of energy levels with color gradients for the imaginary part; Figure 5 shows energy differences in different PT phases; Figure 6 illustrates the failure of standard Hermitian QAA.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of QAA efficiency, defines the non-Hermitian two-qubit model, analyzes the energy spectrum and phase transitions (PT-unbroken vs. broken), investigates time-independent and time-dependent dynamics, and concludes with the derivation of the enhanced success probability.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum information platforms enable analog quantum simulations, such as quantum annealing, offering a promising route to solving complex combinatorial optimization problems. Here, we propose a quantum information architecture based on networks of interacting parity-time (PT)-symmetric non-Hermitian qubits. While the dynamics of individual PT-symmetric qubits have been experimentally demonstrated across multiple platforms including NV centers, superconducting circuits, and trapped-ion systems yet coherent dynamics in interacting systems remain largely unexplored. To address this issue we theoretically investigate stationary and time-dependent Hamiltonians relevant to quantum annealing using a minimal model of two interacting XX-coupled PT-symmetric non-Hermitian qubits. We analyze both symmetry-preserving and symmetry-broken regimes and demonstrate that adding even tiny PT-symmetric non-Hermitian terms in the qubits Hamiltonian allows to greatly enhance the probability of reaching the ground state after annealing.

</details>

### [Berry-Phase-Induced Chirality in Thermodynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13685v1)

**Authors:** Zhaoyu Fei, Yu-Han Ma  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13685v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13685_figures/2605.13685_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>

<img src="2605.13685_figures/2605.13685_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13685_figures/2605.13685_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13685_figures/2605.13685_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the Berry phase induces a chiral asymmetry in the work performed on an open quantum system. While quantum interference fringes disappear in the presence of strong decoherence, a robust, 'fringe-free' chiral work signal remains. This finding clarifies how quantum geometry influences the geometric formulation of thermodynamics in dissipative environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental link between quantum geometry and irreversible thermodynamics, showing that the thermal bath can actually act as a mechanism to encode geometric information into measurable, robust thermodynamic quantities.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the thermodynamic role of the Berry phase in open quantum systems and determining if geometric signatures persist or are erased by decoherence.

**Main result.** The authors discover a Berry-phase-induced chiral work difference that survives decoherence, transitioning from an oscillatory 'interferometric' signal in the unitary regime to a robust, fringe-free signal in the dissipative regime.

**Method.** The study employs a Dissipative Adiabatic Perturbation Expansion (DAPE) framework to expand off-diagonal coherences in terms of an adiabaticity parameter.

**Model / system.** An open quantum system (specifically a two-level system) driven by time-dependent control parameters and weakly coupled to a thermal bath, following a Born-Markov master equation.

**Key observables.** Average work (W), chiral work difference (delta W), and thermodynamic length (L).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Adiabaticity parameter (epsilon), driving frequency (Omega), relaxation/dephasing rates (gamma), and the Berry phase (Phi).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is in the slow-driving (adiabatic) regime, the Lamb shift is neglected, and population differences are treated as approximately constant to leading order.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of geometric phases in thermodynamics, develops the DAPE framework, decomposes work into metric and Berry-phase contributions, demonstrates the discovery of chiral work, and validates the theory using a two-level system and NMR-like parameters.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Geometric phases are foundational to isolated quantum systems, yet their thermodynamic role in open systems remains unrevealed Developing a dissipative adiabatic perturbation expansion, we discover a Berry-phase-induced chiral work difference that survives decoherence. This chirality evolves from an interferometric thermodynamic Aharonov-Bohm effect in the unitary regime to a fringe-free signal in the dissipative regime. We illustrate this framework in a two-level system and assess its experimental feasibility. Our findings clarify the role of quantum geometry in the geometric formulation of thermodynamics.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.12588"></a>
### [Coherent control of spinmons](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12588v1)

**Authors:** Johanne Bratland Tjernshaugen, Florinda Viñas Boström, Jeroen Danon, Jacob Linder, Karsten Flensberg, Antonio L. R. Manesco  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12588v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new qubit architecture called a 'spinmon' that uses the entanglement between a transmon and an Andreev spin to bypass the typical noise-protection tradeoff. It demonstrates that this qubit can be controlled via electrostatic and magnetic means while maintaining high robustness against common decoherence channels. This provides a potential pathway for more stable superconducting quantum computing hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a novel way to engineer protected subspaces in a hybrid system, which is highly relevant for researchers working on open quantum systems and the design of noise-resilient quantum architectures.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental tradeoff in superconducting qubits where protecting against one noise source (like relaxation) often increases sensitivity to others (like dephasing).

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that a 'spinmon' qubit, which encodes information in entangled states of a transmon and an Andreev quasiparticle spin, allows for coherent control via gate voltage and flux while remaining robust against charge and flux noise.

**Method.** The study uses a combination of analytical approaches, such as a harmonic oscillator approximation for potential wells and Fermi's Golden Rule for relaxation rates, alongside numerical Hamiltonian diagonalization.

**Model / system.** The system is a 'spinmon' consisting of a capacitively shunted Andreev spin qubit (ASQ) in parallel with a gate-tunable, even-parity mesoscopic Josephson junction, incorporating spin-orbit coupling and a Zeeman field.

**Key observables.** Qubit splitting (omega_q), Rabi driving/rotation rates, relaxation rates (Gamma), and coherence times against flux and charge noise.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Energy scales: E0 = E_SO = 500 MHz, Ec = B = 40 MHz; Temperature: 10 mK; Regime: heavy transmon limit (Ec << E0).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The potential wells are approximated as harmonic oscillators in the heavy transmon limit; noise is modeled using 1/f spectral density for flux and charge.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the spinmon setup and the potential landscape; Figure 2 illustrates the dependence of spin-orbit modulation and qubit control parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the spinmon concept and the noise tradeoff problem, describes the physical Hamiltonian and system components, details the analytical and numerical methods for control and decoherence, and concludes with a discussion on noise robustness and experimental implementation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The protection of superconducting qubits from certain noise sources often comes at the cost of increased sensitivity to other decoherence channels. Here, we explore a route to avoid this tradeoff by encoding quantum information in quantum states of a transmon entangled with the spin of a trapped Andreev quasiparticle. We term such devices spinmons. We lift the spinmon Kramers degeneracy by introducing a Zeeman field and develop two routes for full qubit control via electrostatic gates and an AC flux drive, providing multiple directions for experimental implementations. Finally, we compute coherence times and verify the qubit robustness against flux and charge noise sources.

</details>

### [Optimal Bounds, Barriers, and Extensions for Non-Hermitian Bivariate Quantum Signal Processing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12656v1)

**Authors:** Joshua M. Courtney  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12656v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. </div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a definitive analysis of Multivariate Quantum Signal Processing for non-Hermitian systems, establishing tight bounds on query complexity and identifying fundamental barriers to simulation. It also introduces efficient algorithms for angle-finding and demonstrates how to handle time-dependent non-Hermitian Hamiltonians.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides fundamental limits and algorithmic improvements for simulating dissipative and non-Hermitian dynamics, which are central to open quantum systems, quantum optics, and Lindbladian evolution.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Resolving open problems in Multivariate Quantum Signal Processing (M-QSP) for non-Hermitian Hamiltonian simulation, specifically regarding query complexity, optimization landscapes, and fundamental simulation barriers.

**Main result.** The paper proves the tightness of anti-Hermitian query complexity, identifies the existence of spurious local minima in the optimization landscape, and establishes a three-tier hierarchy of simulation barriers.

**Method.** The study employs polynomial approximation theory, Chebyshev coefficient bounds, and L-BFGS-B optimization, alongside numerical surveys of random Hamiltonian ensembles (GUE and Wishart).

**Model / system.** The model focuses on the simulation of non-Hermitian effective Hamiltonians H_eff = H_R + iH_I, where H_R and H_I are Hermitian, using a bivariate quantum signal processing framework and a walk-operator oracle.

**Key observables.** Query complexity (d_I, d_R), spectral abscissa (omega), normalization factor (lambda), and the advantage factor (e^{2(beta_I - omega)T}).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Simulation time (T), error tolerance (epsilon), Hermitian norm (alpha_R), anti-Hermitian norm (beta_I), and the spectral-abscissa ratio (omega/beta_I).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a walk-operator oracle model for the primary complexity bounds and considers both commuting and non-commuting regimes of H_R and H_I.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the mean spectral-abscissa ratio across different dimensions, the exponential scaling of the advantage factor, and the convergence rates of the optimizer.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by addressing complexity and tightness of bounds, moves to analyzing the optimization landscape and angle-finding efficiency, extends the framework to time-dependent Hamiltonians, and concludes with a hierarchy of fundamental simulation barriers.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multivariate quantum signal processing (M-QSP) has recently been shown to be applicable for non-Hermitian Hamiltonian simulation, opening several problems regarding the optimization landscape, angle-finding, and constant-factor analysis. We resolve several of these problems here. We find the anti-Hermitian query complexity $d_I = Θ(\betaI T + \log(1/\varepsilon)/\log\log(1/\varepsilon))$ to be tight, established via Chebyshev coefficient bounds, modified Bessel function asymptotics, and Lambert~$W$ inversion. Fast-forwarding to $d_I = \mathcal{O}(\sqrt{\betaI T})$ is impossible in the bivariate polynomial model, though a linear state-dependent improvement to $d_I = \mathcal{O} β_{\mathrm{eff}} T + \log(1/\varepsilon)/\log\log(1/\varepsilon))$ is achievable. The optimization landscape of M-QSP admits spurious local minima, but a warm-start basin guarantee ensures the two-stage algorithm converges. CRC-exploiting block peeling reduces angle-finding from $\mathcal{O}(d^3)$ to $\mathcal{O}(d^2)$ classical operations, and optimized error allocation yields a leading constant of approximately~$2$ relative to the information-theoretic lower bound. A constant-ratio condition extends to non-identical signal operators, enabling time-dependent non-Hermitian simulation with query complexity $\mathcal{O}(\int_0^T(\alphaR(s) + \betaI(s))\,ds + \log(1/\varepsilon)/\log\log(1/\varepsilon))$. Block-encoding overhead $e^{-2\betaI T}$ holds across all function classes within the walk-operator oracle model, and dilational methods (Schrödingerization) achieve the walk-operator barrier. A precisely characterized direct-access construction achieves the intrinsic barrier $e^{-2ωT}$ (with $ω< \betaI$ for non-commuting Hamiltonians) on a restricted domain, though extension to the full bitorus remains open.

</details>

### [Random Access Code protocols: Quantum advantage related to intraparticle entanglement-based contextuality](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13350v1)

**Authors:** Nilaj Saha, Sumit Mukherjee, Dipankar Home  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13350v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13350_figures/2605.13350_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: Mach-Zehnder arrangement for the scheme: Spin-polarized neutrons passing through beamsplitters (BS1 &amp; BS2+PS2) are prepared in the intraparticle entangled state of Eq. <figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13350_figures/2605.13350_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the increase in success probability in quantum Random Access Code protocols is quantitatively tied to the violation of single-particle noncontextuality inequalities. By using intraparticle entanglement between path and spin, the authors show that the quantum advantage scales directly with the strength of the underlying quantum resource. This establishes a measurable, fundamental connection between communication complexity and quantum contextuality.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental link between information-theoretic tasks (RAC) and quantum foundations (contextuality), showing how single-particle resources can be used to quantify quantum advantage, which is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information processing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to establish a quantitative correspondence between the quantum advantage in Random Access Code (RAC) protocols and the magnitude of intraparticle entanglement-based contextuality violations.

**Main result.** The authors prove that the enhancement in success probability for n-to-1 RAC protocols is directly commensurate with the violation of single-particle noncontextuality Bell-type inequalities, providing a universal relation between success probability and the Bell-type parameter.

**Method.** The study uses an algebraic isomorphism between path and spin Hilbert spaces to map RAC protocols onto Bell-CHSH-type inequalities, employing a concatenation scheme to generalize results from 2-to-1 and 3-to-1 subunits to n-to-1 protocols.

**Model / system.** The physical model involves single-particle systems utilizing intraparticle entanglement between co-measurable degrees of freedom, such as path and spin/polarization, implementable via Mach-Zehnder interferometry or photonic setups.

**Key observables.** Success probability (P), Bell-type correlation variables (C_n), and detector counts in interferometric setups.

**Important parameters / regimes.** n-bit encoding (n-to-1), quantum advantage (delta P), and the magnitude of noncontextuality violation (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the validity of noncontextuality for single-particle path-spin measurements and the mathematical equivalence between intraparticle and interparticle entanglement descriptions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows schematic diagrams of 2-to-1 and 3-to-1 RAC subunits and examples of 4-to-1 and 5-to-1 protocol concatenations.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of RAC enhancement, establishes the classical bounds using noncontextuality, develops the quantum framework using intraparticle entanglement, provides a mathematical mapping via Hilbert space isomorphism, generalizes the result to n-bit protocols via concatenation, and discusses experimental feasibility.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quantum enhancement of success probability in the Random Access Code (RAC) protocols remains unexplored from two important perspectives. First, the use of entanglement between two co-measurable degrees of freedom of a single particle (intraparticle entanglement) in achieving such quantum enhancement has not been investigated. Second, no explicit quantitative correspondence has been established between the predicted/observed quantum advantage and the underlying quantum resource responsible for it. In this work, we address both these aspects simultaneously by harnessing a single-particle resource. For this purpose, the RAC protocol is formulated in terms of intraparticle entanglement between, for instance, spin/polarization and path degrees of freedom of a single particle. Within this framework, a relevant Bell-type inequality, derived from the assumption of noncontextuality for single particle path-spin measurements, is used. Based on these ingredients, the formulated analysis reveals that the magnitude of quantum-mechanical violation of such Bell-type inequality, signifying a form of quantum contextuality, is quantitatively commensurate with the quantum enhancement of success probability in any intraparticle entanglement-assisted $n$-bit RAC protocol. In particular, the maximal success probability of a quantum $n \mapsto 1$ RAC protocol corresponds to the maximal quantum violation of the relevant Bell-type inequality. This correspondence is empirically testable using a readily implementable single-particle interferometric setup requiring coherence preservation only for a single particle.

</details>

### [Combining moment matrices, symmetric extension, and Lovász theta: $Φ_{\text{E8}}$ is entangled](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13832v1)

**Authors:** Jȩdrzej Stempin, Gerard Anglès Munné, Santiago Llorens, Felix Huber  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13832v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13832_figures/2605.13832_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. The putative separable state ΦE8 allows for a sym- metric three-copy extension Φ(3) E8 , resulting in a highly con- strained moment matrix that is nonlinear in expectations. Via semidefinite programming (SDP) duality we show that no such moment matrix exists, proving entanglement in ΦE8.</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper solves a long-standing open problem by proving that the 14-qubit state Phi_E8 is entangled. By combining symmetric extensions with moment matrices and graph theory, the authors provide an explicit, mathematically rigorous entanglement witness. This method offers a scalable framework for detecting entanglement in complex multipartite systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to solve an open problem by providing an explicit entanglement witness to prove that the 14-qubit state Phi_E8 is entangled.

**Main result.** The authors successfully prove the entanglement of the Phi_E8 state by providing a rational infeasibility certificate for a semidefinite program, effectively constructing an operational entanglement witness.

**Method.** The approach combines the symmetric extension hierarchy with the non-commutative optimization hierarchy (moment matrices) and utilizes symmetry reduction via the Terwilliger algebra to make the semidefinite program (SDP) computationally tractable.

**Model / system.** The system is a 14-qubit multipartite state, Phi_E8, which is constructed from projectors onto symmetric and antisymmetric subspaces of qubit pairs and is related to the existence of AME(7,2) states.

**Key observables.** The entanglement witness W_Phi_E8, derived from the Lovasz theta number of the Pauli anti-commutativity graph.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The weight of Pauli strings, the anti-commutativity graph G_4+, and the weight threshold delta = 4.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The proof relies on a relaxation of the SDP via group averaging, though the authors note that infeasibility of the relaxed version implies infeasibility of the original.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a visual summary of the proof sketch, showing the transition from a putative separable state to a constrained three-copy extension.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of Phi_E8 entanglement, describes the construction of the symmetric extension and moment matrix, details the symmetry reduction and SDP formulation, presents the numerical infeasibility certificate, and concludes with the construction of the explicit entanglement witness.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We solve an open problem in entanglement theory posed by Yu et al., {\it Nature Communications 12, 1012 (2021)}. The problem is to show, via an entanglement witness, that the $14$-qubit state $Φ_{\text{E8}}$ is entangled. Inspired by a method from quantum codes, we combine symmetric extension with moment matrices to prove that $Φ_{\text{E8}}$ is entangled. The proof has the form of a rational infeasibility certificate for a semidefinite program, yielding an explicit entanglement witness. Our approach unifies and extends several earlier methods that involve the Lovász theta number of the Pauli anti-commutativity graph, promising scalability and flexibility in further applications.

</details>

### [Nagaoka supermetal in the particle-doped triangular Hubbard model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13837v1)

**Authors:** Rui Cao, Xiangyue Zhang, Hui Tan, Jian-Shu Xu, Yuan-Yao He, Jianmin Yuan, Yongqiang Li

**Summary.** The paper identifies a new quantum state called the Nagaoka supermetal in the particle-doped triangular Hubbard model. This state arises from an interaction-induced higher-order Van Hove singularity that causes anomalous transport properties like sublinear resistivity. The findings provide a new perspective on non-Fermi liquid behavior in geometrically frustrated systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The discovery of an intrinsic, interaction-driven non-Fermi liquid state with power-law divergences is highly relevant to many-body dynamics and is directly accessible in ultracold atom experiments.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the nature of the metallic state emerging upon particle doping in a frustrated Mott insulator and identifying the origin of its non-Fermi liquid behavior.

**Main result.** Discovery of the 'Nagaoka supermetal' state, characterized by sublinear temperature-dependent resistivity and power-law divergences in charge compressibility, driven by an interaction-induced higher-order Van Hove singularity.

**Method.** Dynamical Cluster Approximation (DCA) using a continuous-time hybridization expansion solver, supplemented by second-order perturbation theory and effective low-energy Hamiltonian derivation.

**Model / system.** The two-dimensional triangular-lattice Hubbard model in the strongly correlated regime (U/t = 12), specifically focusing on the particle-doped regime.

**Key observables.** DC resistivity, charge compressibility, zero-frequency spectral weight, density of states, and nearest-neighbor spin correlation functions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interaction strength (U/t = 12), doping level (delta), temperature (T/t), and kinetic renormalization factor (Z).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglect of vertex corrections in resistivity calculations and the use of the bubble approximation for transport properties; cluster size is limited to Nc <= 6 due to the fermion sign problem.

**Figures summary.** A phase map showing the transition from Mott insulator to various regimes (FL, SM, PG, NS) and a finite-temperature phase diagram demonstrating the robustness of the NS state.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of doped Mott insulators, describes the Hubbard model and DCA methodology, presents the discovery of the Nagaoka supermetal and its transport signatures, derives the effective Hamiltonian and the higher-order Van Hove singularity mechanism, and concludes with implications for experimental observations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

While the interplay of correlations and geometric frustration in doped Mott insulators provides a fertile ground for exotic quantum phases, the nature of the metallic state emerging upon particle doping remains poorly understood. In this work, we investigate the triangular-lattice Hubbard model with particle doping and provide compelling evidence for an intrinsic, interaction-driven quantum state, which we term the Nagaoka supermetal. This state is characterized by a sublinear temperature dependence in the DC resistivity, along with singular behaviors in the charge compressibility and zero-frequency spectral weight. To understand the origin of these singular properties, we derive an effective low-energy model and demonstrate that a higher-order Van Hove singularity emerges from the reconstructed dispersion. This singularity gives rise to a power-law divergence in the density of states, capturing the anomalous properties observed in the supermetallic regime. Our findings offer a new perspective on non-Fermi liquid states in geometrically frustrated systems and are directly accessible in current ultracold atom experiments.

</details>

### [Site-selective preparation of two-dimensional dipolar quantum gases in an optical beat-note lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13482v1)

**Summary.** The researchers developed an all-optical method to precisely select single or double layers of Dysprosium atoms within a superlattice. By using parametric heating to remove unwanted layers, they achieved a stable platform for high-resolution microscopy of 2D dipolar gases. This technique is particularly useful for studying systems with strong long-range interactions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work provides a robust, all-optical tool for preparing 2D layers in highly magnetic systems, which is essential for studying long-range dipolar interactions and Casimir-Polder forces in a controlled, single-atom-resolved manner.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of performing high-resolution microscopy on 2D dipolar quantum gases due to the challenge of selecting individual atomic layers, especially for lanthanide atoms where standard magnetic-gradient techniques are ineffective.

**Main result.** The demonstration of an all-optical method for the deterministic spatial selection of single- and bilayer samples of cold Dysprosium atoms using site-selective parametric heating in a beat-note superlattice.

**Method.** The researchers use a beat-note superlattice (BNSL) created by retroreflecting two collinear laser beams and apply site-selective parametric heating via amplitude modulation to remove atoms from unwanted planes.

**Model / system.** A 2D system of bosonic 164-Dy (Dysprosium) atoms in an optical beat-note superlattice (BNSL) with a tight-binding regime and site-dependent local band gaps.

**Key observables.** Atom number after heating, local excitation frequencies (local band gap), site-resolved contrast, and in-situ fluorescence imaging of the atomic cloud.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice spacing of ~536.8 nm, axial confinement frequency of 25 kHz, initial temperature of 123 nK, and a wavelength ratio n approx 25 for the BNSL.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes potential depth equality (V1 approx V2) and utilizes the harmonic oscillator approximation for the tight-binding regime.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the BNSL setup and atom number loading; Fig 2 shows absorption images and loss spectra after heating; Fig 3 shows the spatial variation of the local band gap; Fig 5 shows fluorescence imaging and lattice contrast measurements.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of layer selection, describes the BNSL experimental setup, details the mechanism of site-selective parametric heating, demonstrates the preparation of single and bilayer planes, and concludes with characterization of the resulting 2D gases.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-resolution microscopy of two-dimensional dipolar quantum gases requires selecting individual atomic layers, a task complicated for strongly magnetic lanthanide atoms by the limited applicability of standard magnetic-gradient techniques. We present an all-optical method for the deterministic spatial selection of single- and bilayer samples of cold dipolar atoms using spatially selective parametric heating within a beat-note superlattice. By utilizing a high-resolution microscope objective as a common retroreflector for both optical frequency components, the lattice planes are passively stabilized. This renders their positions exceptionally robust against experimental drifts and structure-borne vibrations, even eliminating the need for active laser stabilization over millimeter-scale separations from the reflecting surface. We validate this approach by demonstrating the robust isolation of one or two atomic layers in precise coincidence with the focal plane of our objective. This enables future single-atom-resolved studies of long-range interacting systems.

</details>

### [OAM-Induced Lattice Rotation Reveals a Fractional Optimum in Fault-Tolerant GKP Quantum Sensing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13271v1)

**Summary.** This paper shows that using fractional orbital angular momentum to rotate GKP lattices can significantly suppress logical errors in quantum sensing. By optimizing the lattice geometry to match the noise channel, the researchers achieved a 23.9-fold reduction in error rates without sacrificing sensing precision.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a geometric design principle for noise-adaptive quantum sensors, demonstrating how structural coupling between encoding (OAM) and error correction (GKP) can be exploited to enhance fault-tolerant metrology.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the geometry of GKP (Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill) error-correcting codes to simultaneously maximize sensing sensitivity and maintain fault tolerance in the presence of asymmetric noise (photon loss and dephasing).

**Main result.** The discovery of a fractional optimal OAM charge of l=1.5 (theta=67.5 degrees) that reduces the logical error rate by 23.9x compared to a square lattice while maintaining nearly identical Quantum Fisher Information.

**Method.** An end-to-end differentiable programming framework using Strawberry Fields and TensorFlow to jointly optimize lattice rotation, aspect ratio, and energy envelope via gradient descent.

**Model / system.** Continuous-variable bosonic modes using GKP states in a phase-space lattice, subject to a photon-loss channel and an anisotropic dephasing channel.

**Key observables.** Quantum Fisher Information (QFI), Logical Error Rate (P_err), and Metrological Capacity (C).

**Important parameters / regimes.** OAM topological charge (l), lattice rotation angle (theta), lattice aspect ratio (r), photon loss transmissivity (eta), and dephasing rate (gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the fault-tolerant regime where error probabilities are small; the model uses a Fock-space truncation (D=30).

**Figures summary.** Training convergence for QFI and error rates, visualizations of Wigner functions showing interference fringes and noise impact, and performance comparisons of error reduction across different OAM charges.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to GKP sensing optimization; description of the OAM-induced lattice rotation mechanism; presentation of the differentiable optimization framework; results showing the fractional optimum and error reduction; analytical derivation of the optimal angle and its monotonicity; discussion of experimental implementation routes and robustness.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Photon loss and dephasing rapidly degrade the sensitivity of quantum sensors, yet systematic methods for designing error-correcting codes whose geometry is simultaneously adapted to the sensing task and the noise channel do not exist. Here we establish that orbital-angular-momentum (OAM) encoding and Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill (GKP) lattice geometry are structurally coupled: an OAM mode of topological charge $\ell$ induces a phase-space rotation $θ_\ell=\ellπ/\ell_{\max}$, corresponding to a family of twisted GKP stabilizer lattices. Using an end-to-end differentiable Strawberry Fields--TensorFlow circuit, we jointly optimise $\ell$, the lattice aspect ratio $r$, and the finite-energy envelope $ε$ to maximise quantum Fisher information subject to $P_{\rm err}\leq10^{-3}$. The optimum occurs at the fractional charge $\ell=1.5$ ($θ=67.5^\circ$), implementable with a half-integer spiral phase plate, which reduces $P_{\rm err}$ by $23.9\times$ relative to the square-lattice baseline while leaving $\mathcal{F}_Q$ unchanged to within $0.2\%$. This surpasses the best integer value ($\ell=2$, $15.7\times$) and arises from an exact $180^\circ$ periodicity of the $P_{\rm err}(θ)$ landscape, confirmed analytically and numerically. We derive a transcendental balance equation for the optimal angle $θ^*(η,γ,r)$ and prove that it decreases with both $γ$ and $η$. A Shannon-inspired metrological capacity $\mathcal{C}=\mathcal{F}_Q\cdot(-\ln P_{\rm err})$, maximised at $\ell=1.5$ with a $41\%$ gain over the square lattice, quantifies the joint sensitivity--fault-tolerance resource. These results establish a geometric design principle for noise-adaptive quantum sensors and a fully open-source differentiable template extensible to other bosonic code families.

</details>

### [Decoherence of spatial superpositions along stationary worldlines](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13677v1)

**Authors:** Clemens Jakubec, Aaron Bartleson, Peter W. Milonni, Kanu Sinha  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13677v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Summary of commonly studied decoherence mechanisms in flat spacetime as well as curved spacetime. (a): Unruh- DeWitt field detectors with a classical center of mass consisting of either a two-level system (with frequency ωi) or a harmonic oscillator have been studied in a plethora of situations. Along a hyperbolic worldline such a detector will see an effective temperature TDU. The thermalization and decoherence of such detectors has been studied in [10–18]. (b): Our model (see Fig. 2) where a particle with an internal oscillator degree of freedom in a spatial superposition of ˆ Xi(τ) is coupled to a field via its polarizability combines the decoherence mechanisms of (a) and (c)....</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13677_figures/2605.13677_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Schematic representation of a particle with center- of-mass mass M moving along a worldline Xµ 0 (τ) through Minkowski spacetime. The particle is prepared in a spatial superposition, with ˆ Xµ(τ) as its quantized center-of-mass po- sition. The particle has an internal oscillator degree of free- dom with position ˆq(τ), mass m, frequency ωq and couples to a scalar field ˆϕ( ˆ Xµ(τ)).</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13677_figures/2605.13677_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. (a) An inertial observer, Alice, in Minkowski vacuum, observes the decoherence of a particle undergoing uniform ac- celeration, with Bob as a co-moving observer. From Alice’s perspective the particle interacts with the vacuum field, scat- tering quantum fluctuations and experiencing a momentum recoil in the process. This recoil is associated with ‘which- path’ measurement, which leads to decoherence. (b) In Bob’s perspective, the field is thermal and the decoherence results from scattering of thermal photons.</figcaption>

</div>

**Summary.** This paper analyzes how a particle's spatial superposition decays due to relativistic effects while moving along non-inertial paths. It distinguishes between decoherence caused by the thermal-like Unruh effect and that caused by gravitational time dilation. The findings show that both effects contribute to the loss of quantum coherence and the emergence of new dispersive forces.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental link between relativistic effects (Unruh effect and time dilation) and open quantum system dynamics, specifically how spacetime geometry induces decoherence and forces in a quantum particle.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the mechanisms of decoherence and the emergence of dispersive potentials for the center-of-mass motion of a particle moving along stationary, non-inertial worldlines in the Minkowski vacuum.

**Main result.** The authors identify two distinct decoherence components: one arising from the Davies-Unruh effect (field spectrum modification) and another from differential time dilation across the particle's spatial wavefunction.

**Method.** The study employs a quantum Brownian motion master equation derived under the Born-Markov approximation, utilizing spectral decomposition of field operators and a separation of time scales between internal and external dynamics.

**Model / system.** A polarizable particle with an internal oscillator (internal degree of freedom) and a quantized center-of-mass motion (external degree of freedom) interacting with a 3+1 dimensional scalar field.

**Key observables.** Decoherence rates (Davies-Unruh and time-dilation components), momentum diffusion, dissipation, and dispersion potentials.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Local acceleration, redshift factor, Davies-Unruh temperature, and the ultra-relativistic limit for circular motion.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Stationary worldlines, separation of time scales between internal and external dynamics, Born-Markov approximation, and the particle's center-of-mass wavefunction is sufficiently localized.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares decoherence mechanisms in different spacetimes; Figure 2 provides a schematic of the particle model; Figure 3 illustrates the two physical perspectives of decoherence (inertial vs. co-moving).

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the particle model and interaction, proceeds to derive an effective red-shifted polarizability, constructs a quantum Brownian motion master equation, and concludes by evaluating specific cases of hyperbolic and circular motion.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We analyze the decoherence of a particle's spatial superposition moving along a stationary worldline through the Minkowski vacuum. The particle is modeled via an internal degree of freedom that couples to a scalar field, and an external degree of freedom, i.e., its quantized center-of-mass motion around the stationary worldline. Assuming a separation of time scales between the particle's internal and external dynamics, we first obtain an effective red-shifted polarizability of the particle, characterizing the trajectory-dependent linear response of the internal oscillator to the field. We then derive a quantum Brownian motion master equation for the particle's center of mass, under the Born-Markov approximation, which describes its decoherence in the position basis, as well as, Hamiltonian modifications corresponding to a dispersive potential. The resulting decoherence has two components: (1) arising from a modified field spectrum observed by the particle; and (2) due to a differential time-dilation over the particle's extended spatial wavefunction. For stationary trajectories, both contributions take an effectively thermal form. We evaluate the decoherence rates for two specific cases of hyperbolic and uniform circular motion.

</details>

### [Deep Learning as Neural Low-Degree Filtering: A Spectral Theory of Hierarchical Feature Learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13612v1)

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new theoretical framework called Neural LoFi to explain how deep neural networks learn hierarchical features. It treats deep learning as a sequence of spectral filtering steps, providing a mathematically tractable way to predict when specific features will emerge from data based on sample complexity.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of spectral theory, phase transition analogies (BBP/EA transitions), and renormalization-like coarse-graining to describe feature evolution may resonate with researchers studying the dynamics of many-body systems and renormalization group flows.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism of hierarchical feature learning in deep neural networks and predicting the sample complexity required for specific features to emerge.

**Main result.** The authors introduce Neural LoFi, a spectral surrogate that predicts feature emergence as a phase transition and demonstrates that depth allows networks to learn complex functions by decomposing them into low-degree transitions.

**Method.** The paper uses a spectral filtering framework (Neural LoFi) involving label-weighted moment operators and compares its performance and feature alignment to gradient descent and random feature baselines.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes a hierarchical polynomial toy model (Hermite feature space), fully connected networks, and convolutional architectures applied to datasets like CIFAR-10 and CelebA.

**Key observables.** Population correlation, empirical noise floor, residual effective dimension, feature overlap/alignment, and eigenvalue spectrum of the signed covariance operator.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Layer depth (L), network width (p), number of retained features (k), training set size (n), and the relevance-complexity trade-off.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes a layer-wise vanishing initialization (small-initialization limit), centered labels, and focuses on the early stages of gradient descent.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare Neural LoFi to Gradient Descent performance, show MSE drops and feature overlap increases at predicted sample complexity thresholds, and visualize learned convolutional filters like edge detectors.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Neural LoFi framework, derives its mathematical properties and convergence in the infinite-width limit, provides a theoretical criterion for feature emergence, and validates the theory through experiments on toy models and real-world CNN/FCN architectures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Understanding how deep neural networks learn useful internal representations from data remains a central open problem in the theory of deep learning. We introduce Neural Low-Degree Filtering (Neural LoFi), a stylized limit of gradient-based training in which hierarchical feature learning becomes an explicit iterative spectral procedure. In this limit, the dynamics at each layer decouple: given the current representation, the next layer selects directions with maximal accessible low-degree correlation to the label. This yields a tractable surrogate mechanism for deep learning, together with a natural kernel-space interpretation. Neural LoFi provides a mathematically explicit framework for studying multi-layer feature learning beyond the lazy regime. It predicts how representations are selected layer by layer, explains how emergence of concepts arises with given sample complexity,and gives a concrete mechanism by which depth progressively constructs new features from old ones through low-degree compositionality. We complement the theory with mechanistic experiments on fully connected and convolutional architectures, showing that Neural LoFi improves over lazy random-feature baselines, recovers meaningful structured filters, and predicts representations aligned with early gradient-descent feature discovery with real datasets.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.12972"></a>
### [Matrix-noise Jacobians in stochastic-calculus inference and optimal paths](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12972v1)

**Authors:** Surachate Limkumnerd  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12972v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12972_figures/2605.12972_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Representative likelihood curves for Model A. The full determinant likelihood and the reduced-determinant con- trol are evaluated on the same trajectory. The omitted off- diagonal determinant contribution shifts the maximum of the likelihood in θ.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12972_figures/2605.12972_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Robustness of the paired inference shift in Model A under changes of the generating prescription. The plotted quantity is the paired estimator shift ˆθdiag −ˆθfull for θ0 = 0.25, 0.50, 0.75. The shift vanishes at bc = 0 and remains visible for nonzero bc, showing that the effect is not tied to the Stratonovich value θ0 = 1/2. Error bars denote SEM over independent seeds.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12972_figures/2605.12972_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Most likely transition paths for Model B. The full action contains the state-dependent matrix-Jacobian term Jσ = 4bcx, while the reduced-action control omits it. The full optimal path bows into negative x, as predicted by Eq. (12).</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper reveals that in multidimensional systems with matrix-valued noise, a unique Jacobian term arises that is ignored in simpler scalar models. This term can significantly bias the estimation of stochastic calculus conventions and physically alter the predicted most-likely paths in a system.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for open quantum systems and many-body dynamics, as it shows that neglecting off-diagonal noise correlations can lead to incorrect physical conclusions about the underlying stochastic dynamics and transition paths.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how matrix-valued multiplicative noise affects stochastic-calculus inference and the calculation of optimal paths in multidimensional systems.

**Main result.** The paper identifies a specific matrix-noise Jacobian term, J_sigma, that is absent in scalar-noise models and demonstrates that it causes measurable shifts in inferred stochastic prescriptions and deforms optimized transition paths.

**Method.** The author uses a finite-step path-likelihood framework, short-time expansions, and a variational Hamiltonian approach to derive and test the impact of the matrix-Jacobian term.

**Model / system.** The study uses multidimensional stochastic differential equations with multiplicative noise, specifically testing two models: Model A (a 2D system for inference validation) and Model B (a variational setting for path deformation).

**Key observables.** The matrix-noise Jacobian (J_sigma), the paired estimator shift (theta_diag - theta_full), and the deformation of the Onsager-Machlup optimal path.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The stochastic discretization parameter (theta), the noise-amplitude coefficients (b and c), and the time-step (delta t).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes smooth coefficients, non-singular noise amplitude matrices, and the small time-step limit (delta t -> 0).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 (not fully described) and Figure 3 show the most likely transition paths for Model B, visually demonstrating a 'bowing' effect caused by the matrix-Jacobian term.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of matrix-noise ambiguity, formulates a path-likelihood framework, identifies the J_sigma term via short-time expansion, and then validates the findings through numerical inference tests (Model A) and variational path optimization (Model B).

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multiplicative noise makes stochastic dynamics depend on how the white-noise limit is interpreted. In multidimensional systems with matrix-valued noise amplitudes $σ(x)$, this dependence includes a local Jacobian contribution that is absent from the scalar examples most often used to build intuition. We formulate a finite-step path-likelihood framework for $θ$-discretized diffusions and show that its short-time expansion isolates the scalar $J_σ=\partial_jσ_{ik}\partial_iσ_{jk}-(\partial_iσ_{ik})(\partial_lσ_{lk})$. For a specified noise-amplitude representation $σ$, this quantity vanishes in one-dimensional, scalar-isotropic, and strictly diagonal cases, but can survive when state-dependent noise directions mix different components. We then test its consequences using paired comparisons that hold the drift, diffusion matrix, interpolation point, and Gaussian increment term fixed. In Model A, removing only the off-diagonal determinant contribution produces a shift in the fitted stochastic prescription that vanishes when $J_σ=0$. In Model B, removing the corresponding state-dependent action term changes a stable optimized transition path. These results show that a genuinely matrix-noise part of the short-time path measure can survive the scalar cancellations familiar from simpler settings and produce measurable changes in fitted stochastic prescriptions and Onsager--Machlup paths.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12675"></a>
### [The Physical and Contextual Limits of Quantum Speedup](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12675v1)

**Authors:** Karl Svozil  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12675v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

**Summary.** This paper refutes the idea that quantum computers work by performing many classical computations in parallel. Instead, it argues that speedup is a result of using interference to detect global algebraic structures, a process that is highly sensitive to any information leakage into the environment.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insights into how decoherence and environmental coupling (leakage) specifically quench the interference mechanisms required for quantum advantage, which is central to open quantum systems and quantum computing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to clarify the fundamental physical and contextual limits of quantum speedup, specifically debunking the misconception that quantum computing relies on 'branchwise parallelism' of classical computations.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that quantum speedup arises from engineered interference patterns and algebraic embeddings rather than parallel classical branches, and that this speedup is destroyed if information leaks into an inaccessible environment (witness register).

**Method.** The paper uses a theoretical framework involving unitary dynamics, information theory (Holevo bound, no-cloning/no-deletion theorems), and a modified Bernstein-Vazirani algorithm to model interference loss.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on n-qubit systems governed by closed unitary dynamics, specifically analyzing a modified Bernstein-Vazirani algorithm involving a query register, an answer register, and a witness register.

**Key observables.** Measurement probability distribution Pr(z) in the computational basis and fringe visibility/interference patterns.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The number of qubits (n), the size of the information leakage to the witness register (k), and the hidden string (a).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The witness register is assumed to be inaccessible (modeled by tracing it out), and the dynamics are assumed to be closed and unitary.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a conceptual clarification of quantum speedup, moves to a review of physical constraints (no-cloning, contextuality, unitarity), introduces a mathematical model of information leakage via a modified Bernstein-Vazirani algorithm, and concludes with a discussion on complexity and universality.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum computation is frequently mischaracterized as the simultaneous execution of exponentially many classical computations. This article offers a conceptual clarification of why this "branchwise parallelism" picture is misleading, demonstrating that the components of a quantum superposition cannot be treated as independently readable classical branches. Quantum speedups arise instead from reversible embeddings of algebraic structure made accessible through engineered interference patterns. We review this mechanism through several constraints: unitary garbage erasure is impossible, copying and deletion are context-dependent, and contextuality obstructs a single global classical history. We also distinguish circuit or unitary universality from Turing universality: dense generation of unitaries is not the same as symbolic computation over unbounded inputs with recursion, uniformity, and self-reference. In closed unitary dynamics there is no nontrivial absorbing halting state of the classical many-to-one kind; operational termination requires clocks, flags, measurements, open-system records, or external control. Exponential Hilbert-space dimension supplies a geometry for interference and high-dimensional embedding, not unlimited classical readout.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12885"></a>
### [Competing Effect of Biquadratic and Heisenberg Coupling on Magnetic Tunnel Junction Molecular Spintronics Devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12885v1)

**Authors:** Andoniaina Mariah Randriambololona, Hayden Brown, Eva Mutunga, Andrew Grizzle, Christopher DAngelo, Pawan Tyagi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12885v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**
