**Summary.** This paper explores how Heisenberg and biquadratic exchange couplings compete to determine the magnetic state of molecular spintronic devices. It finds that while Heisenberg coupling dictates the primary magnetic alignment, biquadratic coupling helps stabilize the system against thermal fluctuations. These findings help explain experimental observations of non-standard magnetic phase orientations.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the competing effects of Heisenberg exchange coupling (HC) and biquadratic exchange coupling (BQC) on the magnetic stability and spin orientation in magnetic tunnel junction molecular spintronics devices (MTJMSD).

**Main result.** Heisenberg coupling is the dominant force determining parallel or antiparallel magnetization, while BQC plays a secondary role by assisting in magnetic stabilization and reducing susceptibility to thermal fluctuations.

**Method.** Monte Carlo simulations using the Metropolis algorithm and a 3D Heisenberg/Ising model to study temporal evolution and equilibrium states.

**Model / system.** A Magnetic Tunnel Junction Molecular Spintronics Device (MTJMSD) consisting of a ferromagnet-insulator-ferromagnet (FM-I-FM) trilayer with paramagnetic molecules at the exposed edges, modeled with an expanded Hamiltonian including HC and BQC terms.

**Key observables.** Device magnetization, spin orientation angles, temporal evolution of magnetization, and 2-D autocorrelation of molecular and electrode spins.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Heisenberg coupling strength (J), biquadratic coupling strength (b), thermal energy (kT), and device geometry (pillar vs. extended cross junction).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the BQC is a localized phenomenon at the junction and uses a simplified 3D model for the electrodes.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of the MTJMSD and molecular interactions; autocorrelation graphs; temporal evolution of magnetization; and contour plots of spin angles as a function of coupling and temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the MTJMSD problem, describes the Hamiltonian and Monte Carlo methodology, presents results for varying BQC under different HC regimes, analyzes thermal effects, and concludes with implications for device stability.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Heisenberg exchange coupling (HC) and biquadratic exchange coupling (BQC) are known to occur in magnetic tunnel junctions (MTJ) and nanoscale spintronics structures. MTJ-based molecular spintronics devices (MTJMSD) provide a platform to study these interactions and the correlated magnetic behavior they generate. Molecular spin channels formed along the exposed MTJ edge have been shown to produce strong exchange interactions that include HC and BQC, which can drive perpendicular alignment of spin vectors in adjacent ferromagnetic electrodes. Despite their importance, the competing roles of HC and BQC in MTJMSDs remain unclear. Monte Carlo simulations using a three-dimensional Heisenberg model were performed to systematically vary BQC strength under three conditions: no molecular HC, strong parallel HC, and strong antiparallel HC. The resulting magnetic and physical properties of the MTJMSDs were analyzed. Increasing BQC strength produced minimal changes in overall magnetization when strong HC was present, indicating that HC dominates device magnetization. Temporal evolution studies showed that devices with only BQC failed to reach magnetic stability, while devices with both HC and BQC achieved stable magnetic states due to the stabilizing influence of HC. These results show that BQC plays a secondary role in magnetization dynamics and cannot overcome the stronger stabilizing effect of HC. The presence of BQC offers a plausible explanation for experimentally observed magnetic phase orientations beyond simple parallel and antiparallel states in MTJMSDs.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a classical counterpart to feedback-based quantum optimization algorithms and compares their performance. It reveals that while quantum algorithms can find better solutions through nonlocal dynamics, classical algorithms are faster and more scalable for large-scale optimization problems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides deep insights into the trade-off between the global exploration capabilities of quantum dynamics and the efficient local optimization of classical dynamics, which is central to understanding the limits of quantum advantage in optimization.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the possibility of quantum advantage in combinatorial optimization by comparing feedback-based quantum algorithms (FALQON, iFALQON) with their newly introduced classical counterparts (CACAO, HOT-CACAO).

**Main result.** Quantum algorithms can achieve higher solution quality due to global/nonlocal dynamics, while classical algorithms exhibit significantly faster convergence and better scalability for large-scale HUBO problems.

**Method.** The authors use the quantum-classical correspondence of spin systems and Lyapunov control theory to derive classical counterparts and higher-order counterdiabatic terms, performing benchmarks using QuTiP and Runge-Kutta simulations.

**Model / system.** The study utilizes spin systems described by the Schrödinger equation (quantum) and canonical equations of motion (classical), applied to combinatorial optimization problems like 2-SAT and 3-SAT (QUBO and HUBO).

**Key observables.** Energy density (EP(t)/M), convergence rate, control strengths (beta_X, beta_Y, beta), and solution quality.

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size (N), operation time (t), clause density (alpha), and driving inhomogeneity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the quantum-classical correspondence for spin systems and uses specific initial states like uniform superposition or random spherical sampling.

**Figures summary.** Figures compare energy density and convergence rates across algorithms for small-scale 2-SAT and large-scale 3-SAT, demonstrating the scalability of HOT-CACAO+ and the stability/instability of different driving schemes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the classical counterparts of quantum feedback algorithms, develops a higher-order theory for classical optimization, performs comparative benchmarks on small and large scales, and analyzes the underlying mechanism of performance differences.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Feedback-based quantum optimization is a quantum approach to combinatorial optimization. In this paper, we introduce the classical counterpart of feedback-based quantum optimization by using the quantum-classical correspondence of spin systems to discuss the possibility of quantum advantage. It also enables us to develop higher-order theory of a previously proposed classical approach to feedback-based quantum optimization. First, we compare the feedback-based algorithm for quantum optimization (FALQON) and its variant with their classical counterparts. Then, we perform benchmark tests of various quantum and classical algorithms with small-scale instances, and of classical algorithms with large-scale instances. Main findings are that (i) quantum algorithms can be advantageous to classical algorithms in terms of the quality of solutions, while classical algorithms tend to show faster convergence than quantum ones, and (ii) one of the classical algorithms discussed in this paper shows significant scalability for higher-order unconstrained binary optimization problems. These findings highlight the importance of quantumness and the usefulness of classical approaches.

</details>

### [Quantum resolution of the Schwarzschild singularity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13508v1)

**Authors:** Vishnulal Cheriyodathillathu, Tanmay Patil, Saurya Das, Soumen Basak  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13508v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that the Schwarzschild singularity can be regularized without a full theory of quantum gravity by considering the effective geometry seen by quantum probes. By using Bohmian mechanics, the authors demonstrate that the singularity is replaced by an asymptotic boundary with finite curvature. This suggests that quantum dynamics on a classical background can fundamentally alter the causal structure of black holes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether the Schwarzschild singularity at r=0 can be resolved using a semiclassical approach where quantum effects are modeled via Bohmian trajectories.

**Main result.** The authors show that the effective geometry experienced by a quantum probe has finite curvature invariants and is geodesically complete, effectively rendering the classical singularity physically inaccessible.

**Method.** The study uses the Madelung-Bohm decomposition of the Klein-Gordon wavefunction to map non-geodesic trajectories onto geodesic motion in a conformally related effective metric.

**Model / system.** A massive scalar field (Klein-Gordon field) propagating in a fixed, classical Schwarzschild spacetime background.

**Key observables.** Kretschmann scalar (curvature invariant), conformal proper time, and affine parameter.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The limit r approaching 0, the conformal factor scaling exponent n, and the coupling constants xi and eta.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The background Schwarzschild geometry is assumed to be fixed and classical, neglecting the back-reaction of the quantum probe on the spacetime.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the semiclassical framework and Bohmian trajectories, moves to the derivation of the effective conformal metric, performs an asymptotic analysis of curvature invariants near the origin, and concludes with an evaluation of geodesic completeness.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We revisit the Schwarzschild singularity in a semiclassical setting where the background geometry is classical and quantum effects enter through Bohmian (quantal) trajectories associated with a Klein Gordon wave packet. Using the Madelung-Bohm decomposition of the Klein Gordon wavefunction, we show that the quantum-modified motion is equivalent to geodesic motion in an effective metric conformally related to Schwarzschild, with a conformal factor fixed by the wavefunction amplitude. Solving the wavefunction equation near $r\to 0$ determines this factor and yields finite curvature invariants, in suitable coordinates the interior extends smoothly and the effective spacetime is geodesically complete. This suggests that quantum dynamics on a fixed classical background can regularize the Schwarzschild singularity without a full theory of quantum gravity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12828"></a>
### [The Quad-$C_5$ Graph: Maximum Contextuality Gap on Eight Vertices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12828v1)

**Authors:** Ugur Tamer, Özgür E. Müstecaplıoğlu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12828v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12828_figures/2605.12828_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) The optimal witness Quad-C5(∆= 0.4678, 10 edges). Dark (hub) nodes have degree 3; light (leaf) nodes have degree 2. The four hub nodes {2, 3, 6, 7} form a K2,2 core; leaves attach via two pendant paths 3−0−5−2 (left col- umn) and 6−1−4−7 (right column). (b) The Wagner graph W (∆= 0.4142, 12 edges, 3-regular); dashed lines are the four “long diagonals” of the C8 cycle. Quad-C5surpasses W by 13% in contextuality gap with two fewer edges.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The researchers discovered a new 8-vertex graph, named Quad-C5, that provides the largest known quantum contextuality gap for this size. This graph is more efficient than previous benchmarks and shows enhanced robustness to depolarizing noise in higher dimensions. The discovery reveals a deep algebraic connection between this new graph and the well-known KCBS pentagon via the golden ratio.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is interesting for quantum information theorists as it identifies a more efficient and robust resource for certifying quantum contextuality, which is a fundamental requirement for certain quantum computing protocols and device-independent tasks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to identify the graph on eight vertices that maximizes the quantum contextuality gap within the Cabello-Severini-Winter framework.

**Main result.** The discovery of the Quad-C5 graph, which achieves a maximum contextuality gap of 0.46784, surpassing the previously known Wagner graph while using fewer edges and providing superior noise robustness at d=4.

**Method.** An exhaustive semidefinite programming (SDP) search over all 11,117 connected non-isomorphic graphs on eight vertices, supplemented by high-precision numerical refinement and the PSLQ integer-relation algorithm.

**Model / system.** The study uses the Cabello-Severini-Winter (CSW) framework for projective measurements, modeling contextuality via exclusion graphs where edges represent orthogonal projectors in qutrit (d=3) and four-dimensional (d=4) Hilbert spaces.

**Key observables.** The contextuality gap (Delta), the Lovasz theta function (quantum bound), the independence number (classical bound), and the critical visibility (v*) under depolarizing noise.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of vertices (n=8), dimension (d=3, 4), edge count (m), and the visibility parameter (v) for the depolarizing channel.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the validity of the CSW framework for projective measurements and uses a standard depolarizing noise model.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a structural comparison between the newly discovered Quad-C5 graph and the Wagner graph.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with an exhaustive search and identification of the optimal graph, followed by an algebraic characterization of its properties, a comparison of its efficiency and noise robustness against known witnesses, and a spectral analysis linking it to the KCBS pentagon.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We perform an exhaustive semidefinite-programming search over all 11{,}117 connected non-isomorphic simple graphs on eight vertices to maximize the quantum contextuality gap $Δ(G)=\vartheta(G)-α(G)$, where $\vartheta(G)$ is the Lovász theta function and $α(G)$ is the independence number of the exclusion graph $G$ within the Cabello--Severini--Winter framework for projective measurements. A previously uncharacterized graph on $n=8$ vertices and $m=10$ edges, which we name the Quad-$C_5$ graph (graph6 code: \texttt{GCQb`o}), achieves $Δ=0.46784$, surpassing the Wagner graph $W$ ($Δ\approx0.414$, $m=12$) with two fewer edges. We determine numerically, via the PSLQ integer-relation algorithm at 50-digit precision, that Quad-$C_5$ is a \emph{qutrit} contextuality witness with $η_3=1+\sqrt{5}$ (minimal polynomial $x^2-2x-4=0$), while numerical evidence indicates the Wagner graph requires a four-dimensional (two-qubit) Hilbert space. The graph contains four mutually overlapping induced five-cycles, and its adjacency spectrum is dominated by golden-ratio eigenvalues, tracing the contextuality advantage algebraically to the KCBS pentagon. Under depolarizing noise, Quad-$C_5$ at $d=3$ shares the critical visibility $v^*=1/(3\sqrt{5}-5)\approx0.585$ of the KCBS witness -- an analytically provable coincidence arising from a uniform shift of the graph parameters -- while at $d=4$ it strictly surpasses the Wagner graph in noise robustness.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13154"></a>
### [Three ways to find comfort with the Bell proof and the results of the Bell experiments](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13154v1)

**Authors:** Richard D Gill, Inge S. Helland, Bart Jongejan  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13154v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

Distance (left to right) is so large that a signal travelling from one side to the other at the speed of light takes longer than the time interval between input and output on each side. One “go = yes” trial has binary inputs and outputs; modelled as random variables A,B,X,Y. (Figure 7 from J.S. Bell (1980), “Bertlmanns socks and the nature of reality”)</figcaption>

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 8: Observations by Alice and Bob as explained by a shared hidden parameter. A 4-loop has a perimeter of 4. The curve between the points A (B) and Λ has length λA (λB) and represents the column of green ﬂuid in Alice’s (Bob’s) apparatus. Alice and Bob make the same observation if Λ is in a red sector, different observations if Λ is in a blue sector. The two pairs (8a,8c) and (8b,8d), respectively, illustrate that even if Alice knows her own green ﬂuid level and Bob’s setting, she cannot predict with certainty the green ﬂuid level of Bob. </div>

**Summary.** This paper explores different philosophical and physical ways to interpret the violation of Bell inequalities. Rather than proposing a single new theory, the authors present three different ways to 'find comfort' by abandoning either locality or realism while maintaining scientific integrity.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a deep foundational analysis of the implications of non-locality and the validity of the 'realism' assumption, which is central to understanding the limits of quantum information processing and the nature of quantum correlations.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the fundamental dilemma posed by Bell's theorem: how to reconstruct a coherent worldview following the experimental violation of the CHSH inequality without resorting to unscientific theories like superdeterminism or retrocausality.

**Main result.** The authors present three distinct theoretical frameworks for interpreting Bell violations: one accepting irreducible randomness, one based on the limitations of accessible variables, and one using a geometric hidden-variable construction.

**Method.** The authors use a dialectic approach, employing Pearl-style causal graphs (DAGs) to establish a shared mathematical foundation before presenting three divergent individual interpretations.

**Model / system.** The paper analyzes a Bell-type experiment involving three parties (Alice, Bob, and Carol) and uses models such as the 'Loop of Four' (a 1D geometric construction) and Belavkin-style eventum mechanics.

**Key observables.** The CHSH parameter (S), expectation values of correlated outcomes, and joint detection probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The Tsirelson bound, detector efficiency, and the dimensionality of space.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The authors explicitly reject superdeterminism (violation of statistical independence) and retrocausality, while exploring the abandonment of either locality or realism (counterfactual definiteness).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the spatio-temporal setup of a Bell trial; Figure 2 presents a Directed Acyclic Graph (DAG) of the causal model; Figure 8 illustrates a 1D 4-loop model for hidden variables.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a shared mathematical exposition of Bell's theorem using causal graphs, followed by a summary of loophole-free experiments, and concludes with three individual sections where each author presents their specific physical interpretation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Bell's theorem states that no description of a Bell experiment can be simultaneously local, realistic in the sense of counterfactual definiteness, and free of conspiracy between settings and hidden state. The recent generation of experiments has confirmed the predicted violation of the CHSH inequality, so one of the assumptions must be abandoned. Which one, and how one reconstructs a coherent worldview after doing so, is a question on which many authors disagree. This paper is written by three such authors. All three reject both counterfactual definiteness and conspiratorial violation of statistical independence of setting choices and state. After a joint exposition of the classical half of Bell's theorem in the language of Pearl-style causal graphs, a joint summary of the loophole-free experiments, and a joint survey of the recent literature, each author states where they have presently arrived. Gill accepts irreducible and non-local quantum randomness and finds the choice between locality and realism a false dichotomy. In his later works, Bell derives counterfactual definiteness from classical local causality, and that is what has to go. The metaphysical concepts "realism", "locality", "causality" need to be reconsidered. Helland reconstructs the Hilbert-space formalism from a theory of accessible variables, and from this theory he concludes that every observer must be limited in a specific sense. Jongejan proposes a geometric hidden-variable construction in which the degree of violation of the CHSH inequality depends on the number of dimensions of space, Tsirelson's bound corresponding to three dimensions. The authors conclude with a discussion.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12615"></a>
### [Quantum state isomorphism problems for groups](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12615v1)

**Authors:** Alexandru Gheorghiu, Dale Jacobs, Saeed Mehraban, Arsalan Motamedi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12615v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **1/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12615_figures/2605.12615_page2.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 2</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12615_figures/2605.12615_page3.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 3</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12615_figures/2605.12615_page4.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12615_figures/2605.12615_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12615_figures/2605.12615_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 6</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive complexity classification for determining if two quantum states are equivalent under group actions. It reveals that while some cases like the Pauli group are efficiently solvable, others are as hard as the Graph Isomorphism problem or even QSZK-complete, ruling out efficient quantum algorithms for certain mixed-state problems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and quantum information as it establishes the fundamental computational limits of identifying symmetries and equivalence classes in both discrete-variable and continuous-variable quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates the computational complexity of Quantum State Isomorphism Problems (QSIP) under the action of various groups, determining if two quantum states are related by a group element.

**Main result.** The authors prove that pure-state QSIP is BQP-hard for all nontrivial groups, BQP-complete for the Pauli group, and GI-hard for the Clifford group, while mixed-state QSIP is QSZK-complete.

**Method.** The study employs complexity theory reductions (from Graph Isomorphism, Hidden Subgroup Problem, and Quantum State Distinguishability), classical shadows, and stabilizer-rank analysis.

**Model / system.** The models include n-qubit systems (pure and mixed states), the Pauli and Clifford groups, and infinite-dimensional bosonic systems using the stellar representation and linear optical unitaries.

**Key observables.** Fidelity, trace distance, and inner products between quantum states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Precision parameters (alpha, beta) for state closeness, photon number (r) for bosonic states, and the trace condition (mu) for group representations.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes efficient representability of groups, finite-dimensional or succinctly represented bosonic states, and an unbounded prover in the complexity protocols.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the QSIP framework, analyzes pure-state complexity for various group families (Pauli, Clifford, Abelian), extends the analysis to mixed states, investigates bosonic/linear optical systems, and concludes with implications for the State Hidden Subgroup Problem.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the computational complexity of quantum state isomorphism problems under group actions: given two quantum circuits that prepare pure or mixed states, decide whether the two states are related by a group action. This can be seen as a quantum state version of the Hidden Shift Problem, in much the same way that the State Hidden Subgroup Problem is a quantum version of the ordinary Hidden Subgroup Problem.   We prove several results for this computational problem:   - For the pure-state version, we show that the problem is BQP-hard for all nontrivial groups, and contained in QCMA $\cap$ QCSZK. We further obtain refined results for specific groups of interest: for abelian groups we show that the problem reduces to the state hidden subgroup problem over the generalized dihedral group; for the Clifford group, the problem is at least as hard as Graph Isomorphism under polynomial-time reductions; for the Pauli group it is BQP-complete.   - For the mixed-state version, for nontrivial, finite and efficiently representable groups, the problem is QSZK-complete.   - We also study a variant of this problem over an infinite group, in particular, the bosonic linear optical unitaries. We show that in the setting where the classical description of the quantum state is given in a suitable wave function representation known as the stellar representation, the problem is at least as hard as Graph Isomorphism, and is contained in NP $\cap$ SZK.   Prior to our work, state isomorphism problems had only been studied for the symmetric group [LG17]. As a consequence of our results, we resolve an open question posed in [HEC25] about the existence of a quantum algorithm for the abelian state hidden subgroup problem on mixed states. We show that this problem is QSZK-hard in the worst case, thereby ruling out an efficient quantum algorithm unless QSZK = BQP.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13774"></a>
### [Affiliated operators for classical and quantum control](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13774v1)

**Authors:** Dimitrios Giannakis, Gage Hoefer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13774v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13774_figures/2605.13774_page4.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 4</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13774_figures/2605.13774_page5.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13774_figures/2605.13774_page6.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 6</figcaption>

</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new mathematical framework for controlling infinite-dimensional systems by using the theory of affiliated operators in von Neumann algebras. It successfully handles the mathematical difficulties of unbounded Hamiltonians, allowing for a well-defined Lie algebraic approach to controllability in both quantum and classical contexts.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous mathematical foundation for controlling infinite-dimensional systems, which is crucial for advanced quantum control tasks in cavity QED and continuous-variable quantum information where unbounded Hamiltonians are standard.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Addressing the mathematical challenges of controllability and time-optimal control for bilinear systems in infinite-dimensional Hilbert spaces where Hamiltonians are unbounded.

**Main result.** The authors establish a framework using affiliated operators in finite von Neumann algebras to recover a well-defined dynamical Lie algebra and prove the existence of time-optimal controls.

**Method.** Utilizing von Neumann algebra theory, specifically the properties of $\tau$-measurable and affiliated operators, alongside the Koopman operator formalism for classical systems.

**Model / system.** The framework applies to infinite-dimensional quantum systems governed by the Schrödinger equation and classical dynamical systems modeled via the Koopman operator formalism.

**Key observables.** Reachable sets of vector states and the dynamical Lie algebra structure.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The norm bounds of control terms, the type of von Neumann algebra (finite vs. semifinite), and the spectral properties of the drift operator.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The drift and control terms are assumed to be affiliated with a von Neumann algebra of finite type; the framework specifically addresses the difficulties of unbounded operators.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the operator-algebraic framework for affiliated operators, develops control theory for quantum systems (existence of optimal controls and reachable sets), extends the approach to classical systems via Koopman theory, and provides specific examples like the Jaynes-Cummings model and differential operators on Lie groups.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Using techniques from the theory of von Neumann algebras, we propose a framework for addressing questions of controllability of bilinear systems on infinite dimensional Hilbert spaces. In the setup, we assume only that the drift and control terms arising in a bilinear control system are affiliated with a von Neumann algebra of finite type acting on the same Hilbert space. When the control terms satisfy basic norm bound conditions, we prove existence of time-optimal controls. In the more general setting where all operators may be unbounded, we show how the dynamical Lie algebra for the system is still well-defined and may be used to check approximate controllability of the system in question. We discuss how this approach can be applied to classical dynamical systems through the Koopman operator formalism, and investigate potential candidates for the von Neumann algebra which may guide the choice of controls. We illustrate how an affiliation relation naturally arises in both classical and quantum control systems with a few examples.

</details>

### [Comparative assessment of germanium-based spin-qubit modalities: donor, acceptor, gate-defined hole, and gate-defined electron platforms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13680v1)

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive comparison of four different ways to implement spin qubits using germanium. It concludes that while gate-defined hole qubits are currently the best candidate for scalable quantum computing, the different Ge-based technologies can work together in a specialized ecosystem for memory and hybrid processing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a high-level strategic analysis of semiconductor-based quantum architectures, which is relevant for understanding the physical limits of scalable hardware and the engineering of spin-photon/spin-phonon interfaces.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified comparative framework to evaluate the trade-offs between different germanium-based spin-qubit modalities (donor, acceptor, gate-defined hole, and gate-defined electron) regarding coherence, controllability, and scalability.

**Main result.** The study identifies gate-defined hole-spin qubits as the most mature and promising path for scalable processors, while proposing a layered ecosystem where donors and acceptors serve specialized roles in memory and hybrid architectures.

**Method.** A comparative technical assessment using a literature-based review, scaling arguments, and a new theoretical framework for estimating phononic-crystal-modified T1 relaxation.

**Model / system.** High-purity germanium (Ge) platforms including donor-bound electrons, acceptor-bound holes, and gate-defined electron and hole quantum dots in Ge/SiGe heterostructures.

**Key observables.** Spin-lattice relaxation rate (T1), coherence time (T2), g-tensor anisotropy, valley splitting, and gate fidelity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Spin-orbit coupling strength, effective masses (longitudinal/transverse), isotopic purity (5N to 13N), and impurity-induced strain scales.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The comparison assumes that the developmental trajectory of each modality can be extrapolated from current experimental milestones and materials physics.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 visualizes the relationship between average linear strain and residual impurity density in germanium.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with a review of Ge materials physics, differentiates the four qubit modalities based on band structure (L-point vs. Gamma-point), introduces a framework for relaxation estimation, and concludes with a strategic roadmap for a layered quantum ecosystem.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-purity germanium (Ge) has re-emerged as a versatile semiconductor platform for spin-based quantum information processing because it combines mature materials processing, access to spin-free isotopes, high mobilities, small effective masses, and strong but engineerable spin--orbit coupling. However, ``Ge qubits'' are not a single technology. Donor spin qubits, acceptor spin qubits, gate-defined hole spin qubits, and gate-defined electron spin qubits exploit different parts of the Ge band structure and therefore make distinct trade-offs among coherence, controllability, fabrication complexity, and scalability. Here we compare these four Ge-based spin-qubit modalities on a common physical and architectural footing. We review the shared Ge materials physics, including isotopic purification, the multivalley \(L\)-point conduction band, the spin-\(3/2\) valence band, heavy-hole/light-hole mixing, strain, interfaces, disorder, and phonons. We also introduce a common framework for estimating phononic-crystal-modified \(T_1\) using a calibrated reference relaxation rate, a geometry-dependent strain-density-of-states suppression factor, and parasitic relaxation channels. The comparison shows that gate-defined Ge hole-spin qubits currently offer the strongest combination of all-electrical control, demonstrated multiqubit operation, and scalability. Donor, acceptor, and gate-defined electron qubits remain important complementary directions for memory, hybrid, and exploratory architectures. Overall, Ge supports a diverse qubit ecosystem, with gate-defined hole-spin qubits presently providing the clearest path toward scalable Ge-based quantum processors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13559"></a>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13559_figures/2605.13559_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. (a) DFT and DFT+DMFT spectral function in the extended energy window. Red arrows indicate interband transitions with energy separation of 1.3 eV, corresponding to the prominent peak observed in the NM DFT optical conductivity (Fig. 1(d) and (e)). (b) Interband optical conductivity from AM DFT+DMFT calculation (dashed) with ordered moment M ∼0.1µB/Ru compared with PM DFT+DMFT (thin red) and experimental result (thick black) [20].</figcaption>

**Summary.** This paper uses DFT+DMFT to show that dynamical electron correlations are essential for understanding the magnetic properties of RuO2. It demonstrates that the material's magnetic ground state is highly tunable, with minimal strain being enough to induce altermagnetism. This provides a theoretical resolution to conflicting experimental observations of the material's magnetism.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Resolving the conflicting experimental reports regarding whether the magnetic ground state of RuO2 is paramagnetic or altermagnetic and understanding the role of dynamical electron correlations in its tunability.

**Main result.** The study reveals that RuO2 is highly susceptible to external perturbations, where even a 0.5% compressive strain can drive the system into an altermagnetic phase due to dynamical correlation effects.

**Method.** The researchers used Density Functional Theory combined with Dynamical Mean-Field Theory (DFT+DMFT) with a continuous-time quantum Monte Carlo (CTQMC) impurity solver.

**Model / system.** The physical system is RuO2, a 4d transition-metal oxide with a rutile structure, characterized as a Hund's metal near the paramagnetic-altermagnetic and itinerant-localized phase boundaries.

**Key observables.** Spectral functions, optical conductivity, effective mass, quasiparticle weight (Z), ordered magnetic moment (M), and local spin susceptibility.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coulomb interaction U = 3.5 eV, Hund's coupling J = 0.6 eV, and compressive strain along the c-axis.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes a density-density form of the Coulomb interaction using Slater parametrization and focuses on the local approximation inherent in DMFT.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the crystal structure, ARPES intensity, and spectral functions; Figure 4 compares DFT and DFT+DMFT spectral functions and optical conductivity; Figure 5 discusses orbital-specific effects.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the experimental conflict in RuO2, presents the DFT+DMFT methodology, compares results with experimental ARPES and optical data, investigates the effect of strain on magnetic stability, and concludes with the role of correlations in phase tunability.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

RuO$_2$ has been regarded as a prototypical candidate for metallic altermagnet, offering a potential platform for high-speed and high-efficiency spintronics. However, the magnetic ground state of RuO$_2$ remains a topic of active debate due to conflicting experimental reports. In this work, we investigate the effect of electron correlations in RuO$_2$ using density functional theory combined with dynamical mean-field theory (DFT+DMFT). In contrast to previous DFT-based studies, DFT+DMFT captures essential dynamical correlation effects, yielding spectral functions and optical conductivities in excellent quantitative agreement with experiments, and further reveals that RuO$_2$ resides in the close vicinity of both the paramagnetic-altermagnetic phase boundary and the itinerant-localized crossover, rendering the magnetic ground state highly susceptible to external perturbations. Indeed, even a minimal compressive strain of $\sim$0.5% is sufficient to drive the system into an altermagnetic phase. These findings elucidate the origin of the conflicting experimental observations and reveal that dynamical correlation effects are the key driving force behind the highly tunable magnetic ground state of RuO$_2$.

</details>

### [Fermion lattices can be simulated by same-size qubit lattices with $\mathcal{O}(1)$ interaction overhead](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12600v1)

**Summary.** This paper presents a new framework for simulating higher-dimensional fermion lattices on qubit arrays by dynamically switching between different 1D Jordan-Wigner encodings. It achieves near-optimal scaling for interaction counts and significantly reduces circuit depth for various hardware architectures. This allows for the efficient simulation of complex models like the Fermi-Hubbard model on square, Lieb, and Kagome lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a scalable way to map complex, higher-dimensional fermionic Hamiltonians onto near-term and fault-tolerant quantum hardware without the prohibitive qubit overhead of previous methods.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Simulating 2D and higher-dimensional fermionic lattices on qubit hardware is inefficient due to the 1D connectivity constraints of standard Jordan-Wigner mappings, which introduce significant non-local interaction overhead.

**Main result.** The authors develop a method to simulate all geometrically local interactions on an N-site 2D fermion lattice with O(1) interaction overhead and no space overhead, reducing circuit depth to O(sqrt(N)) on standard lattices and O(1) on surface-code architectures.

**Method.** The method uses 'dynamic fermionic encoding' by switching between complementary 'boustrophedon' (snake-like) Jordan-Wigner encodings to align the encoding path with different lattice dimensions.

**Model / system.** The framework targets 2D and d-dimensional fermionic lattices, specifically implementing Fermi-Hubbard models (including NN and NNN hopping) on various geometries like square, Lieb, and Kagome lattices, as well as the Fermionic Fast Fourier Transform (FFFT).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Scaling of gate counts (CNOTs) and circuit depth relative to the number of sites N; interaction range delta; dimensionality d.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method assumes geometrically local interactions; the efficiency of the encoding switch depends on the underlying hardware connectivity (e.g., superconducting vs. neutral atom).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 introduces F2Q mapping and encoding switching; Figure 2 shows the mechanism for 2D JW reordering; Figures 3-4 illustrate 2D/3D boustrophedon patterns and fermion routing; Figure 5 and Figure 10 compare resource scaling (gate count and depth) against standard FSWAP networks; Figures 16-18 show implementations for Lieb and Kagome lattices.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the motivation of F2Q mapping overhead, introduces the dynamic encoding framework and boustrophedon patterns, extends the construction to higher dimensions and complex routing, and concludes with specific applications to Hubbard models and lattice embeddings.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Local interactions among electrons underlie many complex properties of correlated materials. While the Jordan-Wigner transformation can preserve this locality along one spatial dimension, interactions along the remaining dimensions typically incur substantial overhead. We show how to simulate all geometrically local interactions on an $N$-site two-dimensional fermion lattice with no asymptotic overhead in the number of interactions and no space overhead. The primary overhead of our method is circuit depth, which on a qubit lattice matches that of fermionic swap networks, scaling as $\mathcal{O}(\sqrt{N})$, but reduces to $\mathcal{O}(\log N)$ on reconfigurable qubit arrays and to $\mathcal{O}(1)$ in lattice-surgery-based surface-code architectures. This is enabled by dynamically reorienting the Jordan-Wigner transformation to switch the lattice dimension along which locality is preserved. Furthermore, we study fermion routing, as required for the simulation of non-local interactions. When using qubit lattices, we reach resource scaling that asymptotically matches that of qubit routing, whilst on fully connected qubit devices, a depth scaling arbitrarily close to $\mathcal{O}(\log N)$ is reached. This allows the fermionic fast Fourier transform to be implemented on qubit lattices with asymptotically optimal resource scaling under these locality constraints. Notably, all of our constructions naturally extend to $d$-dimensional lattices. Beyond scaling improvements, we show explicit examples of our method, including Fermi-Hubbard-model simulations of the square-, Lieb- and kagome lattice and the fermionic fast Fourier transform.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13363"></a>
### [Invertible Symmetry and Spontaneous Duality Breaking in the Transverse-Field Ising Model](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13363v1)

**Authors:** José Dupont, Jasper van Wezel  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13363v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13363_figures/2605.13363_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Schematic phase diagram of the transverse- field Ising model with anomaly. The states indicated are adiabatically connected to the ground states in the thermo- dynamic limit throughout the corresponding phases and be- come exact ground states as either λ or 1/λ vanishes. Unlike the model without anomaly, the dual Hamiltonian has two ground states in the thermodynamic limit for λ &gt; 1. These are related by the global symmetry ˆ Xˆρ, which corresponds to a local symmetry ˆσz 1 acting on the anomalous boundary degree of freedom.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper shows that the duality in the 1D transverse-field Ising model can be made exact by using specific open boundary conditions, at the cost of introducing a boundary anomaly. It proposes that the physical distinction between dual models arises from their different sensitivities to local environments, a phenomenon termed spontaneous duality breaking.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The authors investigate whether the Kramers-Wannier duality in the 1D transverse-field Ising model is exact or approximate, and how boundary conditions affect the invertibility of the duality operator and the nature of symmetry breaking.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that by using open boundary conditions, the duality can be made exact and implemented by an invertible operator, though this necessitates a boundary anomaly. They also introduce the concept of 'spontaneous duality breaking,' where environmental perturbations distinguish between dual descriptions.

**Method.** The study employs algebraic transformations, unitary mapping of dual operators, and Conformal Field Theory (CFT) analysis including fusion rules and topological defect operators.

**Model / system.** A modified 1D Transverse-Field Ising Model (TFIM) with open boundary conditions, where the Hamiltonian is adjusted to ensure exact self-duality.

**Key observables.** Ground state degeneracy, spectrum of the Hamiltonian, global spin-flip operator, and dual operators.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Transverse field strength (lambda), critical point (lambda = 1), and the thermodynamic limit (N to infinity).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The argument for spontaneous duality breaking assumes the system is embedded in an environment that provides local fluctuations.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the flaws in standard periodic boundary condition duality, constructs an exact dual model using open boundary conditions, analyzes the resulting boundary anomaly and symmetry transformations, and concludes by resolving the paradox of symmetry breaking via the concept of spontaneous duality breaking.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The self-duality of the transverse-field Ising model is an archetype for dualities that, alongside symmetry and topology, are used as an organizing principle throughout modern physics. This duality, however, is not exact. The original and dual models have different symmetries and numbers of ground states, and the duality is implemented by a non-invertible operator giving rise to a non-invertible symmetry at the quantum critical point. Here, we show that by adjusting the model to accommodate open rather than periodic boundary conditions, it allows for an exact duality implemented by a unique invertible operator. In the model with exact duality, the symmetry at the quantum critical point is also exact, and hence invertible. Moreover, we find that the exact duality necessitates the presence of an anomalous edge degree of freedom, thus realizing a duality rather than topology based bulk-boundary correspondence. Finally, the exactness of the duality implies that the spontaneous breakdown of a global symmetry in terms of the original model can equivalently be described as spontaneously breaking a local symmetry in the dual system. We show that this seeming contradiction of Elitzur's theorem can be explained by the original and dual models obtaining different sensitivities to spatially local perturbations in any physical implementation of the Hamiltonian. Although the dual partners are mathematically equivalent, their physical implementations therefore are not. In analogy to the spontaneous breakdown of symmetries, we term this emergent distinction due to arbitrarily small environmental influences spontaneous duality breaking.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Nonlinear dynamic elastic moduli from equilibrium stress fluctuations](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13703v1)

**Authors:** F. E. Garbuzov, Y. M. Beltukov  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13703v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Low-resolution page preview, page 5</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">

</div>

**Summary.** This paper provides new fluctuation formulas that allow researchers to calculate nonlinear dynamic elastic moduli using only equilibrium molecular dynamics data. This avoids the need for expensive nonequilibrium simulations of finite-strain deformation. The resulting framework unifies linear viscoelasticity and nonlinear dynamic response within a single statistical mechanics formalism.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of established fluctuation formulas to compute nonlinear time-dependent (dynamic) elastic moduli from equilibrium molecular dynamics simulations.

**Main result.** The derivation of transient-time correlation function expressions for both linear and nonlinear dynamic moduli, which recover known quasi-static and linear dynamic results in appropriate limits.

**Method.** A theoretical derivation using the DOLLS/SLLOD equations of motion, a power series expansion of the nonequilibrium average stress as a functional of strain history, and a pulled-back variable transformation.

**Model / system.** A classical system of N interacting particles with a Hamiltonian consisting of kinetic and potential energy, modeled using DOLLS/SLLOD dynamics for irrotational motion.

**Key observables.** Linear dynamic viscoelastic moduli tensor (C_dyn), nonlinear dynamic moduli tensor (N_dyn), Second Piola-Kirchhoff stress tensor (S), and Cauchy stress tensor (sigma).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Small but finite time-dependent strains (anharmonic regime), Born-kinetic terms, and the limit of zero-frequency (quasi-static) response.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The motion is irrotational (L = L^T), the potential is translationally and rotationally invariant, the initial state is a canonical ensemble, and the system follows classical Cauchy elasticity.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the scientific problem and the microscopic equations of motion, proceeds to a mathematical derivation using pulled-back variables and power series expansions of stress, demonstrates the recovery of known limits, and concludes with discussions on symmetry and limitations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fluctuation formulas for elastic and viscoelastic moduli allow their computation from equilibrium molecular dynamics simulations, avoiding explicit nonequilibrium deformation protocols. While such expressions are well established for the quasi-static moduli, and also the linear dynamic moduli, no fluctuation formula exists for the nonlinear time-dependent moduli that govern anharmonic viscoelastic response under finite time-dependent strains. In this work we derive transient-time correlation function expressions for both the linear and the nonlinear dynamic moduli, starting from the DOLLS/SLLOD equations of motion for irrotational motion. The resulting formulas involve equilibrium time correlations of the stress tensor and Born-kinetic terms, and they recover the known quasi-static and linear dynamic results in the appropriate limits.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12984"></a>
### [Numerical security analysis for practical quantum key distribution](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12984v1)

**Authors:** Álvaro Navarrete, Guillermo Currás-Lorenzo, Margarida Pereira, Marcos Curty  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12984v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a numerical framework to certify the security of practical QKD systems against complex, real-world imperfections like device flaws and correlated signals. By using semidefinite programming, the authors achieve tighter security bounds than existing analytical methods. This allows for more robust and reliable implementation of quantum key distribution in practical fiber-optic networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous way to certify the security of real-world quantum communication hardware, which is essential for the deployment of scalable quantum networks and the protection of quantum information against coherent attacks.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Bridging the gap between idealized security proofs and practical QKD implementations by addressing device imperfections and non-IID signal correlations.

**Main result.** A versatile numerical finite-key security framework that provides tight, probabilistic upper bounds on phase error rates against general coherent attacks, even in the presence of pulse correlations and detector mismatches.

**Method.** The authors utilize Semidefinite Programming (SDP) to derive operator inequalities and a Virtual Estimation Protocol (VEP) to bound errors using observable statistics.

**Model / system.** Practical QKD setups, specifically BB84-type prepare-and-measure protocols and decoy-state BB84, including models for state-preparation flaws, detector efficiency mismatch, and correlated photon pulses.

**Key observables.** Phase-error rate (e_ph), secret-key rate (l_key), number of sifted-key bits (N_key), and error statistics from test rounds.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Correlation length (L), security parameters (epsilon), detection efficiency (eta_det), dark-count probability (p_d), and number of protocol rounds (N).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes a fidelity constraint between prepared and reference states and assumes Bob's measurements satisfy the Basis-Independent Detection Efficiency (BIDE) condition.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows optimized secret-key rates for single-photon BB84 under various imperfections; Figure 2 shows performance for decoy-state BB84 with complex correlations; Figure 3 compares the proposed numerical proof against state-of-the-art analytical proofs.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of the theory-practice gap, presents a numerical framework using SDP and VEP, provides mathematical proofs for error bounding and security equivalence, and demonstrates the framework's power through simulations of BB84 and decoy-state protocols.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum key distribution (QKD) promises information-theoretic security based on quantum mechanics and idealized device models. Practical implementations, however, deviate from these models due to unavoidable device imperfections, and existing security proofs fall short of capturing the complexity of real-world systems. Here we introduce a versatile numerical finite-key security framework valid against general coherent attacks and applicable to a broad class of practical QKD setups. It accommodates most relevant imperfections at both transmitter and receiver, including non-independent-and-identically-distributed (non-IID) signals arising in high-speed QKD systems due to the limited bandwidth of optical modulators, while requiring only partial characterization of the apparatuses. We demonstrate the power of our framework by proving the security of a realistic decoy-state QKD implementation with laser sources, providing a practical route towards rigorous security certification of real-world QKD setups.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.12860"></a>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12860_figures/2605.12860_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Experimental 1/k (blocks) and theoretical 1/k1,min values (lines) vs. Ux are shown. The</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how electrokinetically-driven Janus particles accumulate at the walls of a microfluidic channel. It demonstrates that this accumulation is not due to standard active Brownian motion but is instead driven by an effective cross-channel diffusion caused by persistent stochastic turning. The findings show that the accumulation follows a predictable exponential growth pattern governed by a single relaxation timescale.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study explores non-equilibrium dynamics and effective transport coefficients emerging from stochastic angular fluctuations, which is highly relevant to understanding driven-dissipative systems and coarse-grained dynamics in open quantum systems.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the mechanism behind the time-dependent wall accumulation of electrokinetically-driven Janus colloids in confined geometries and distinguishing it from standard Active Brownian or Run-and-Tumble particle models.

**Main result.** The study reveals that accumulation is driven by an effective cross-channel diffusivity and drift arising from persistent stochastic turning, leading to exponential wall population growth and a single confinement-controlled timescale.

**Method.** The researchers used 3D confocal imaging of density-matched suspensions and compared experimental results to an overdamped Ornstein-Uhlenbeck turning model using a Fokker-Planck approach.

**Model / system.** The system consists of 5 micrometer diameter gold-polystyrene Janus particles confined between parallel walls under an applied AC electric field, modeled via a 1D effective drift-diffusion equation derived from Langevin equations.

**Key observables.** Particle density distribution across z-layers, accumulation rate constant (k), propulsion velocity (Ux, Uz), and mean orientation angle (beta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electric field strength (E), voltage (V), angular relaxation rate (lambda), and effective diffusivity (Deff).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Small angle approximation (beta << 1), time-scale separation (t >> tau_or), and the overdamped limit (low Reynolds number).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental setup and particle orientation; Fig 2 displays progressive particle accumulation in different z-layers; Fig 4 compares experimental depletion time with theoretical model predictions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the experimental setup and system, describes the 3D imaging methodology, presents the theoretical derivation of the effective drift-diffusion model, compares experimental accumulation rates to the model, and discusses scaling laws and orientation dynamics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Electrokinetically-driven Janus colloids, e.g., with one metallic and one dielectric hemisphere, confined between parallel walls exhibit a boundary-accumulation mechanism enabled by an effective cross-channel diffusivity which is distinct from wall accumulation of active Brownian or run-andtumble particles. Using density-matched suspensions and three-dimensional confocal imaging, we directly measure the full time-dependent redistribution of particles across the channel under an applied AC electric field. The wall population grows exponentially while the bulk depletes, and data obtained over multiple field strengths collapse onto a single curve when rescaled by the measured relaxation rate, revealing one dominant, confinement-controlled timescale. Propulsion follows the expected induced-charge electrophoretic scaling, with a mean orientation angle lying between 2 degrees and 10 degrees above horizontal, leading to a top-biased accumulation. Comparison with an overdamped Ornstein-Uhlenbeck turning model suggests that persistent stochastic turning about a small out-ofplane angle results in a cross-channel effective drift and diffusion. The drift governs the dominant timescale and the diffusion is strong enough to provide significant accumulation on the bottom wall despite a mean upward orientational bias.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13234"></a>
### [A hyperbolic cell cycle law for early embryonic developmental timing](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13234v1)

**Authors:** Adrián Aguirre-Tamaral, Johanna Royer, Magdalena Schindler-Johnson, Jun-Ru Lee, Daniel R. Amor, Nicoletta I. Petridou, Bernat Corominas-Murtra  
**Type:** both · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13234v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

A) Schematic diagram of early embryogenesis depicting the rapid, reductive cell divisions (cleavages), followed by the Mid-Blastula transition (MBT) in which a marked elongation of the cell cycle length is observed. B) Plot of CCL across multiple species, with varying onset of MBT times (arrowheads) and overall durations. C) Normalized CCLs from the species shown in (B) to the fastest cell cycle (y-axis) and generation at gastrulation (x-axis) revealing a coinciding pattern across species. D) Model assumptions: (top) depletion of finite maternal resources, acting as substrate for the chemical reactions underlying...</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13234_figures/2605.13234_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2: Early development timing across several metazoa exhibits a hyperbolic elongation of the CCL. A) Metazoan phylogenetic tree showing the phyla for which experimental data were available for quantification of CCL growth. B) CCL progression during the cleavage period (cell generations) across several eumetazoan species developing ex-utero. All species display hyperbolic elongation patterns (dashed curves). Red dashed lines indicate the estimated position of the singularity s∗</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13234_figures/2605.13234_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3: Resource concentration and consumption rates capture developmental timing even in heterochronic species A) Schematic illustrations of the experimental set-up used to reduce resource concentration via aspiration of maternal material (top) and to inhibit the activation of the zygotic genome and thus production of new resources (bottom). B) Plot of CCL over generations in WT, α-amanitin-treated (inhibition of transcription and synthesis of new resources - grey data points) and resource-depleted embryos (aspiration - yellow data points) C) Representative experimental images showing the developmental progression of D. rerio (top) and N. furzeri (bottom) across different stages, from...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper proposes that the universal slowing of cell division in early embryos is a physical consequence of depleting maternal resources. By applying Michaelis-Menten kinetics to resource consumption, the authors show that a single hyperbolic law can predict developmental milestones like gastrulation across widely different species.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the universal mechanism behind the slowing down of cell duplication speed (cell cycle length elongation) across diverse metazoan species and determining if this is driven by fundamental biochemical constraints.

**Main result.** The study identifies a universal hyperbolic law for cell cycle length driven by the depletion of finite maternal resources, which also predicts the timing of gastrulation across various phyla.

**Method.** The authors combine mathematical modeling using Michaelis-Menten kinetics with comparative analysis of existing cell cycle data and live imaging of zebrafish embryos to track histone distribution.

**Model / system.** The system consists of early metazoan embryos undergoing reductive cleavage. The model uses a biochemical framework where cell duplication rates depend on the concentration of a limiting maternal substrate following Michaelis-Menten-like kinetics.

**Key observables.** Cell Cycle Length (CCL), cell number evolution, cell volume, and the cytoplasmic-to-nuclear (C/N) ratio of histone intensity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Maximum reaction rate (uM), Michaelis-Menten constant (Km), initial resource concentration (c0), and the singularity generation (s*) where resources are exhausted.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes cell duplication rates follow Michaelis-Menten kinetics and that resource depletion can be approximated as a smooth, monotonic function of the generation number.

**Figures summary.** Figures show the schematic of embryogenesis, the collapse of multi-species CCL data onto a single universal curve, zebrafish histone distribution and decay, and the prediction of gastrulation timing and cell volume changes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of cell cycle slowing, presents a mathematical model of resource-limited kinetics, demonstrates the universal scaling across species, validates the model via zebrafish experiments (resource reduction and ZGA inhibition), and discusses the implications for developmental timing and heterochrony.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Across metazoans, early embryos exhibit a strikingly conserved slowing down of their cell duplication speed, despite widely varying developmental paces and underlying molecular mechanisms. Here we show that this common behavior arises because early development unfolds along a biochemical rather than a chronological timescale, resulting from the coupling of finite maternal resource consumption to the Michaelis-Menten-like kinetics governing the rates of the biochemical reactions involved in cell duplication. This leads to a hyperbolic growth of the Cell Cycle Length (CCL), approaching a mathematical singularity, which would correspond to developmental arrest. Data from a wide range of organisms -- cnidarians, nematodes, arthropods, molluscs, echinoderms, tunicates, amphibians, and fish -- collapse on a single curve, quantitatively capturing not only a universal CCL dynamical behaviour, but also key hallmarks of early metazoan development, including cell-number temporal evolution, the dependency of CCL on cell size, and, remarkably, gastrulation timing at the predicted singularity. Crucially, experimental modulation of resource availability and consumption rates validate the model and further demonstrate that a source of heterochrony in early development is an altered biochemical timescale of resource depletion. Overall, this work reveals resource consumption rates as a fundamental mechanism driving developmental timing in early embryogenesis across species.

</details>

### [Backdoor Threats in Variational Quantum Circuits: Taxonomy, Attacks, and Defenses](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13796v1)

**Authors:** Lei Jiang, Fan Chen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13796v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13796_figures/2605.13796_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Illustration of a backdoor attack on a variational quantum circuit (VQC). Under normal conditions, the objec- tive value is minimized. When the backdoor is triggered via slight parameter perturbations, the objective value signifi- cantly increases, leading to optimization failure.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13796_figures/2605.13796_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 3: The working mechanism of QDoor [3].</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13796_figures/2605.13796_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper surveys the evolving landscape of backdoor attacks in variational quantum computing, highlighting how new 'quantum-native' threats exploit hardware noise and compilers. It emphasizes that as quantum algorithms move toward deployment, developing robust, quantum-aware defenses is critical to prevent malicious manipulation of computational results.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the security vulnerabilities of Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) to backdoor attacks, where malicious behaviors are embedded into circuits and activated by specific triggers.

**Main result.** The authors provide a comprehensive taxonomy of attacks (data-poisoning, compiler-level, and quantum-native) and demonstrate that current defenses are largely ineffective against sophisticated, hardware-dependent threats.

**Method.** The paper performs a systematic survey and formalization of threat models, categorizing attacks by attacker capabilities and mechanisms, and reviewing existing detection and defense strategies.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on Variational Quantum Circuits (VQCs) used in NISQ-era algorithms, including Quantum Neural Networks (QNNs), Variational Quantum Eigensolvers (VQE), and the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (QAOA).

**Key observables.** Attack Success Rate (ASR), benign performance (accuracy/energy), measurement output distributions, and expectation values.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Poisoning rate, hyperparameter lambda (trade-off between benign and malicious tasks), hardware noise profiles, and qubit connectivity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes adversaries may have control over training data, compilation toolchains, or hardware-specific configurations like noise profiles.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows parameter perturbations triggering optimization failure; Figure 2 illustrates the workflow of a backdoor attack; Figure 3 depicts the QDoor compiler-level mechanism; Figure 4 demonstrates how ZNE-based attacks bias error mitigation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the security problem, provides a formal taxonomy of attack types (data, compiler, and quantum-native), reviews existing literature on attack strategies and metrics, analyzes the limitations of current defense/detection methods, and concludes with future research directions.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Variational quantum algorithms (VQAs) are a central paradigm for noisy intermediate-scale (NISQ) quantum computing, yet their reliance on predesigned and pretrained variational quantum circuits (VQCs) introduces critical security vulnerabilities, particularly backdoor attacks. These attacks embed hidden malicious behaviors that remain dormant under normal conditions but are activated by specific triggers, leading to adversarial outcomes such as incorrect predictions or manipulated objective values. This paper presents a survey of backdoor attacks in VQCs, covering data-poisoning, compiler-level, and quantum-native mechanisms. We formalize key terminology and threat models, and review existing attack strategies along with their empirical characteristics. We also analyze current detection and defense approaches, highlighting their limitations, especially against quantum-specific threats. By synthesizing recent advances, this survey outlines the evolving security landscape of VQCs and identifies key challenges and future directions for developing robust, quantum-aware defenses in hybrid quantum-classical systems.

</details>

### [Beyond Explained Variance: A Cautionary Tale of PCA](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13520v1)

**Authors:** Gionni Marchetti  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13520v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13520_figures/2605.13520_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1: All computations are performed on the standardized fossil teeth data. a) PCA two-dimensional optimal subspace. b) t-SNE two-dimensional embedding obtained using cosine distance and PCA initialization (τp = 29). c) Persistent homology diagram computed using Vietoris-Rips complex and Euclidean distance. d) Persistent homology diagram computed using Vietoris-Rips complex and cosine distance.</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13520_figures/2605.13520_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 2: a) Unit circle in R3, representing the geometric setup used for our generative probabilistic-geometric model. b) Histogram of 3828 pairwise cosine distances of points on a unit circle in R9, generated uniformly at random.
