**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that square SiC nanowires significantly enhance near-field thermal radiation compared to planar surfaces due to the presence of multiple corner and edge modes. The enhancement is most effective when the nanowire thickness matches the separation gap, offering a way to control heat transfer via geometry.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the reduction of planar surfaces into subwavelength square nanowires enhances near-field thermal radiation through localized corner and edge electromagnetic modes.

**Main result.** Square nanowires exhibit a four-fold enhancement of thermal conductance compared to planar surfaces, driven by multiple corner and edge resonances that decay more slowly across the gap than standard surface-phonon-polaritons.

**Method.** Numerical simulations using the Scuff-EM boundary-element method within the framework of fluctuational electrodynamics.

**Model / system.** Two parallel, subwavelength Silicon Carbide (SiC) nanowires with square cross-sections, characterized by thickness (t) and separation gap (d).

**Key observables.** Thermal conductance (G), spectral thermal conductance (G_omega), and the enhancement ratio relative to infinite planar surfaces (G/G_infinity).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nanowire thickness (t), separation distance (d), and the SiC Reststrahlen band frequency range.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is analyzed in the triple nanoscale regime where thickness, width, and gap are all subwavelength; room temperature (300 K) is assumed.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of heat transfer modes (propagating, evanescent, corner/edge); Figure 2 displays spectral and integrated thermal conductance vs. separation distance; Figure 3 shows frequency spectra and conductance vs. thickness; Figure 4 contains density maps.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of near-field heat transfer in nanowires, describes the physical system and numerical method, presents results regarding multi-mode resonance and frequency redshift, discusses the optimization of conductance via geometry, and concludes with implications for nanoscale heat management.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate that the near-field thermal radiation between subwavelength SiC nanowires with square cross sections is dominated by multiple corner and edge resonances rather than the single surface-phonon-polariton channel of planar surfaces. Fluctuational electrodynamics simulations reveal that these resonances lie within the SiC Reststrahlen band, redshift for thinner nanowires, and yield a four-fold enhancement of thermal conductance. This maximum enhancement occurs when the separation gap nearly matches the nanowire thickness, balancing dimensional confinement and interwire coupling. These findings establish square nanowires as a versatile platform for geometrycontrolled near-field heat transfer in nanoscale heat management and energy conversion.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses molecular dynamics to investigate how the chemical composition of liquid lead-lithium alloys influences the formation and stability of helium bubbles. It provides a detailed mechanical analysis of pressure inhomogeneities and interfacial tension at the bubble surface, which is critical for designing fusion reactor breeding blankets.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the formation, stability, and interfacial properties of helium bubbles in liquid lead-lithium (Pb-Li) alloys, specifically focusing on how alloy composition and curvature affect interfacial tension and pressure inhomogeneities.

**Main result.** The study establishes that helium bubble formation is a cavitation process driven by supersaturation and finds that interfacial tension is non-monotonic with alloy composition, peaking at intermediate concentrations. It also demonstrates that local pressure anisotropies at the interface are driven by the competition between kinetic and virial pressure components.

**Method.** Classical molecular dynamics simulations using the velocity-Verlet algorithm and the Irving-Kirkwood contour method to calculate local pressure tensor components and interfacial tension.

**Model / system.** Helium clusters (nanobubbles) embedded in liquid lead-lithium (Pb-Li) eutectic solutions, modeled using Embedded Atom Method (EAM) potentials (AEAM-2023 and BEAM-2019) for the metal phase and pairwise potentials for helium.

**Key observables.** Interfacial tension (gamma), pressure anisotropy (difference between normal and tangential pressure), bubble radius, density profiles, and interfacial free energy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (up to 1021 K), atomic composition (Pb:Li ratios), and interface geometry (planar vs. spherical/curved).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Helium is considered infinitely diluted; helium densities within the bubble and bulk are assumed uniform; helium-helium interactions are treated as purely pair-decomposable.

**Figures summary.** Snapshots of helium bubbles and bilayers; comparison of ideal vs. non-ideal pressure profiles; plots of interfacial tension and bubble radii as functions of lead fraction; convergence plots of tension with cluster size; and radial pressure tensor profiles for various alloys.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of helium solubility in liquid metals, describes the MD simulation setup and EAM potentials, details the mathematical framework for pressure tensor and tension calculations, presents results on interfacial structure and tension across different compositions, and discusses curvature and energetic implications.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The extremely low solubility of helium in liquid metals may lead to rapid supersaturation, promoting spontaneous formation of helium bubbles by nucleation. Once nucleated, the stability of these bubbles is governed by the properties of the helium liquid metal interface. In particular, interfacial tension between the immiscible phases controls bubble interactions and induces local pressure inhomogeneities. This work is motivated by the need of a better understanding of helium bubble formation in liquid Pb Li alloys, which are of particular relevance for the design of breeding blankets in the future nuclear fusion reactors. We employ classical molecular dynamics simulations to investigate helium segregation in a range of lead lithium systems, including the limiting cases of pure lead and pure lithium. Changes in local pressure are evaluated from direct mechanical calculations, enabling the characterization of interfacial properties. Interfacial tension and radius of the bubble are subsequently determined across multiple thermodynamic conditions, spanning temperatures starting near the melting points of the constituent metals up to 1021 K. The impact of curvature and composition of the alloy on the interfacial behaviour are also investigated.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13658"></a>
### [High-Pressure XRD Study of Ti-3Al-2.5V Titanium Alloy: Intermediate Transition Pressure and Composition Trends in Ti-Al-V Alloys](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13658v1)

**Authors:** D. Errandonea, R. Turnbull, P. Botella, R. Oliva, C. Popescu, S. MacLeod  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13658v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: XRD patterns of Ti3-2.5 embedded in an ME PTM, λ = 0.4246 Å. Black circles represent</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13658_figures/2605.13658_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This study demonstrates that increasing the aluminum and vanadium content in titanium alloys raises the pressure required for the alpha-to-omega phase transition without significantly altering the material's bulk modulus. This finding provides a way to tune the structural stability of titanium alloys under extreme pressure without changing their mechanical stiffness.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the addition of Al and V affects the structural stability and the pressure-induced alpha-to-omega phase transition in Ti-Al-V alloys.

**Main result.** The transition pressure increases systematically with higher Al and V content, while the bulk modulus remains nearly unchanged, suggesting a decoupling of phase stability and elastic properties.

**Method.** High-pressure X-ray diffraction (XRD) using a diamond-anvil cell at the ALBA synchrotron, with data analyzed via the Le Bail method and Birch-Murnaghan equation of state.

**Model / system.** Polycrystalline Ti-3Al-2.5V alloy, compared against pure Ti and Ti-6Al-4V, subjected to high pressures using various pressure-transient media (methanol-ethanol, silicone oil, and MgO).

**Key observables.** Transition pressure (P_onset), lattice parameters (a, c, c/a ratio), bulk modulus (K0), and volume collapse.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pressure range (up to ~30 GPa), Al and V concentration levels, and the degree of hydrostaticity of the pressure medium.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The transition mechanism is assumed to be fundamentally similar across the Ti-Al-V series; experimental scatter in omega-phase volume data required combined datasets for EOS fitting.

**Figures summary.** XRD patterns showing the emergence of the omega-phase (Fig 3-5), a plot of transition pressure vs. alloy composition (Fig 6), and the pressure dependence of lattice parameters and c/a ratios (Fig 7).

**Paper structure.** Introduction to Ti-Al-V importance, experimental setup (DAC and synchrotron), results of phase transition pressures across different compositions, analysis of elastic properties (EOS), and discussion of the decoupling of stability and elasticity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-pressure X-ray diffraction experiments were performed on Ti-Al-V alloys to investigate the effect of composition on structural stability, focusing on Ti-3Al-2.5V and comparing with pure Titanium and Ti-6Al-4V. Measurements using different pressure-transmitting media show a phase transition in Ti-3Al-2.5V at 17-19 GPa, intermediate between pure Ti (5-10 GPa) and Ti-6Al-4V (~30 GPa). Despite variations arising from the choice of pressure medium, the transition pressure shows a clear and systematic increase with higher Al and V content. Equation-of-state analysis indicates that the bulk modulus remains nearly unchanged across compositions. This suggests a decoupling between elastic properties and phase stability, with alloying primarily affecting the transition pressure rather than compressibility. These results highlight the role of composition in tuning high-pressure phase transformations in Ti-Al-V based alloys.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13528"></a>
### [High-temperature behavior of amorphous alumina coatings: Insights from in-situ nanoindentation and X-ray diffraction studies](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13528v1)

**Authors:** A. Zaborowska, L. Kurpaska, M. Zielinski, Q. Xu, E. Wyszkowska, J. OConnell, J. H. Neethling, F. Di Fonzo, M. Frelek-Kozak, S. Papanikolaou, R. Diduszko, J. Jagielski  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13528v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13528_figures/2605.13528_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Fig. 1. Schematic representation of high-temperature nanoindentation setup.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13528_figures/2605.13528_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Fig. 2. Schematic of the MD simulation model for the nanocontact between a spherical</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13528_figures/2605.13528_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Fig. 3. Representative load-displacement curves for a-Al2O3 coating measurements (a) in the</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13528_figures/2605.13528_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 4. Temperature dependence of the (a) nanohardness and (b) Young’s modulus of a-Al2O3</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13528_figures/2605.13528_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Fig. 5. Temperature dependence of the (a) work and (b) MDP of a-Al2O3 coating.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how high temperatures affect the mechanical and structural properties of amorphous alumina coatings used in nuclear reactors. It identifies a bond-switching mechanism that causes softening without changing the elastic modulus and tracks the transition from amorphous to crystalline phases up to 1050 C.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the high-temperature mechanical behavior and structural stability of amorphous alumina (Al2O3) coatings, specifically regarding hardness reduction and phase transitions.

**Main result.** Hardness decreases significantly with temperature due to an accelerating bond-switching mechanism, while Young's modulus remains constant; crystallization occurs between 650-700 C.

**Method.** In-situ high-temperature nanoindentation and X-ray diffraction (XRD) combined with Molecular Dynamics (MD) simulations using the Vashishta potential.

**Model / system.** Amorphous alumina (Al2O3) thin films deposited via pulsed laser deposition (PLD) onto 316L stainless steel substrates.

**Key observables.** Hardness (H), Young's modulus (E), Microhardness Dissipation Parameter (MDP), and phase composition (alpha, theta, etc.).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature range from 25 C to 1050 C; coating thickness of approximately 5 micrometers.

**Assumptions / limitations.** MD simulations use a high loading rate compared to experiments; thermal gradients exist due to bottom-up heating during indentation.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of experimental and MD setups; load-displacement curves; temperature dependence of plasticity (MDP); and TEM images of the coating cross-section.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to nuclear application needs, followed by experimental setup for nanoindentation and XRD, MD simulation methodology, results regarding mechanical properties and phase evolution, and discussion of the bond-switching mechanism.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Further development of nuclear power plant technology relies heavily on materials durability under operating conditions. Estimating the materials performance in the operando tests is crucial. In this paper, the mechanical behavior of thin amorphous nuclear-dedicated Al2O3 coatings deposited by pulsed laser deposition was investigated by nanoindentation over the temperature range of 25-650C. Experimental nanomechanical analysis was supported by MD simulations. The results indicate that the hardness of the amorphous coating experiences a gradual, constant decrease with temperature, while the Young modulus value remains constant in the whole temperature range. Observed phenomena confirm the increasing plasticity of the material and it is postulated to be related to the bond-switching mechanism that accelerates at high temperatures. The post-mortem transmission electron microscopy characterization confirmed that the loaded material was non-crystalline over the entire range of the indentation temperatures. The thermal stability of the structure was further studied in-situ up to 1050C by X-ray diffraction. The implemented methodology allowed us to follow the dynamic process of phase transitions occurring in the material above 650C. First, thermally activated crystallization was observed at 700C. Intermediate alumina phases were present up to 950C, while above this temperature, exclusively the thermodynamically stable alpha-Al2O3 was observed. The in-situ high-temperature characterization of the evolution of thin films boosts the understanding of the application limits of the coating systems at elevated temperatures. The added value is that the paper demonstrates the potential usefulness of combining high-temperature techniques to characterize the complete behavior of thin films at elevated temperatures.

</details>

### [Highly Efficient Exciton Modulation in MoSe$_2$/PdSe$_2$ Heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13211v1)

**Summary.** The researchers demonstrate that placing MoSe2 in contact with PdSe2 significantly boosts its light emission efficiency at room temperature. This is achieved through interlayer electronic coupling that redirects excitons into radiative pathways, offering a non-invasive way to engineer 2D optoelectronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** How to efficiently control exciton recombination and enhance photoluminescence quantum yield in 2D semiconductors without using invasive methods like chemical doping or strain.

**Main result.** A 6-fold enhancement of room-temperature A-exciton emission in MoSe2/PdSe2 heterostructures is achieved via interlayer electronic coupling that redistributes exciton populations toward radiative channels.

**Method.** The study combines experimental optical spectroscopy (PL, PLE, power/temperature-dependent PL) and structural characterization (AFM, Raman) with ab initio DFT simulations.

**Model / system.** A van der Waals heterostructure consisting of a monolayer of MoSe2 and a multilayer (approx. 6L) of PdSe2 on an hBN substrate.

**Key observables.** Photoluminescence quantum yield (PLQY), A-exciton and B-exciton emission intensities, B/A intensity ratio, and power-law exponent for exciton-exciton annihilation.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Room temperature, 6-fold enhancement of PLQY, broadband enhancement range of 450-725 nm, and ~200 meV splitting between A and B excitons.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The 4L PdSe2 model used in DFT is considered an efficient approximation of the 6L experimental system due to similar electronic structures.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the spatial arrangement of the heterostructure flakes and the electronic band structures/projected density of states. Supporting information includes Raman spectra for thickness verification.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of exciton control, describes the MoSe2/PdSe2 heterostructure fabrication and characterization, presents the observed PL enhancement and B-exciton quenching, provides mechanistic evidence via power/temperature dependence and PLE, and supports the findings with DFT simulations and control experiments.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Controlling exciton recombination in atomically thin semiconductors is central to their optoelectronic functionality, as the competition between radiative and non-radiative decay channels governs emission efficiency. Existing approaches, such as defect passivation, chemical doping, dielectric engineering, and strain tuning, primarily aim to suppress non-radiative losses. Here, we report a pronounced $\sim$6-fold enhancement of room-temperature A-exciton emission in a type-I MoSe$_2$/PdSe$_2$ van der Waals heterostructure, yielding a photoluminescence quantum yield of 6 %, compared to $\sim$1 % for as-exfoliated monolayer MoSe$_2$. This enhancement is accompanied by strong quenching of the B-exciton, consistent with interlayer electronic coupling that redistributes exciton populations toward the radiative A-exciton channel. Power- and temperature-dependent measurements reveal a suppression of exciton-exciton annihilation and a crossover to quenched emission at low temperature, indicating a redistribution of exciton relaxation pathways. Photoluminescence excitation spectroscopy further reveals a broadband enhancement spanning 450-725 nm, ruling out a resonance-specific mechanism. These results demonstrate that interlayer electronic coupling can be used as an efficient means to redirect exciton populations toward radiative channels, enhancing emission efficiency in two-dimensional semiconductors without chemical modification or strain.

</details>

### [Interface controlled spin filtering and nonreciprocal transport in Altermagnet/Ising superconductor junctions](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13317v1)

**Summary.** This paper explores how the unique spin textures of altermagnets and Ising superconductors can be harnessed at a spin-active interface to create highly efficient spin filters. It shows that by tuning the interface magnetization and the altermagnet orientation, one can achieve significant spin polarization and direction-dependent (nonreciprocal) transport, providing a platform for superconducting spintronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the interplay between momentum-dependent spin splitting in altermagnets and intrinsic spin-orbit coupling in Ising superconductors can be used to control spin-polarized transport and nonreciprocal effects via a spin-active interface.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that a spin-active interface enables tunable spin filtering (up to 86% efficiency) and nonreciprocal transport, with transport properties highly dependent on the relative orientation of the altermagnet spin texture and interface magnetization.

**Method.** A modified Bogoliubov-de Gennes (BdG) framework within the Blonder-Tinkham-Klapwijk (BTK) scattering formalism is used to calculate spin-resolved conductance and symmetry analysis.

**Model / system.** A heterostructure junction consisting of an Altermagnet (AM) with d-wave symmetry exchange field and an Ising Superconductor (ISC) with strong spin-orbit coupling, separated by a spin-active interface with spin-dependent potential.

**Key observables.** Spin-resolved charge and spin conductance, spin polarization (P), spin-filter efficiency (eta), and nonreciprocal transport signatures.

**Important parameters / regimes.** AM orientation angle (delta), interface magnetization angle (chi_m), spin-mixing strength (rho), and Ising spin-orbit coupling strength (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The altermagnet exchange field is assumed to lie in the xy-plane; the interface is modeled as a delta-function potential.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the heterostructure schematic; Fig 2 shows conductance spectra vs AM orientation; Fig 5 shows conductance density plots across different ISOC regimes; Fig 6 shows angular modulation of spin polarization and efficiency; Fig 7 shows variation of parameters.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the AM/ISC junction, defines the Hamiltonian and interface model, derives the transport formalism, analyzes transport in different ISOC regimes, and discusses spin-filtering efficiency and nonreciprocity.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate theoretically spin-resolved transport, spin filtering, and nonreciprocal effects in an Altermagnet/Ising superconductor (AM/ISC) junction with a spin-active interface. Using a modified Bogoliubov-de Gennes framework within the scattering formalism, we demonstrate that the interplay among intrinsic spin-orbit coupling (ISOC), anisotropic AM spin texture and spin-dependent interfacial scattering gives rise to strongly anisotropic charge and spin conductance. In the weak spin-mixing regime, transport remains predominantly helicity conserving and exhibits pronounced angular dependence governed by the relative orientation between the AM spin texture and interface magnetization. Increasing ISOC enhances spin conductance and leads to spin-selective Andreev reflection resulting in finite spin filtering. In contrast, the strong spin-mixing regime exhibits enhanced angular anisotropy and robust spin-polarized transport over a broad energy range. Conventional Andreev reflection becomes strongly suppressed, accompanied by substantial spectral redistribution. We further show that nonreciprocal transport persists throughout the single-band, intermediate and double-band ISC regime. The spin polarization and spin-filter efficiency exhibit nonmonotonic dependence on system parameters, reaching values up to $\sim 86\%$, with characteristic angular modulation determined by the AM spin texture. Finite-energy analysis reveals enhanced spin selectivity at low energies and suppression near the superconducting gap. Furthermore, strong spin mixing at the AM/ISC junction produces asymmetric conductance patterns, indicating nonreciprocal transport. Our results establish AM/ISC junctions as a versatile platform for tunable superconducting spintronics and directional spin transport.

</details>

### [Layer thickness dependent band gap of MBE grown single- to few-layer MoS$_{2}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13637v1)

**Authors:** Maciej Bazarnik, Thorsten Deilmann, Marta Przychodnia, Anika Schlenhoff  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13637v1>

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

**Summary.** The study tracks how the electronic band gap of MoS2 changes as it grows from a single layer to five layers. It reveals that the band gap drops below the known bulk value due to extra electronic screening caused by defects like grain boundaries and stacking faults inherent in the growth process.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the layer-thickness dependence of the electronic band gap in MoS2 from monolayer to 5 layers and determining why the 5-layer band gap is lower than the bulk value.

**Main result.** A drastic decrease in the band gap is observed as layer number increases, reaching values below the bulk limit, which is attributed to additional screening from growth-induced defects.

**Method.** Scanning Tunneling Spectroscopy (STS) was used for experimental measurement, while Density Functional Theory (DFT) and GW calculations were used for theoretical comparison.

**Model / system.** MoS2 layers (1 to 5 atomic layers) grown via Molecular Beam Epitally on a graphene/Ir(111) substrate using a Stranski-Krastanov growth mode.

**Key observables.** Band gap (Eg), conduction band and valence band edge positions relative to the Fermi level, and dI/dU differential conductance.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Layer thickness (1-5L), temperature (5.5 K), and screening/doping weights.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theoretical model assumes a defect-free experimental structure and uses empirically estimated doping weights to match experimental data.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows STM topographies of different coverage regimes (1-2L, 2-4L, and 3-5L); Figure 2 shows normalized dI/dU curves and extracted band gap data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the importance of band engineering in TMDs, describes the MBE growth and STM/STS experimental setup, presents the thickness-dependent band gap measurements, compares results with DFT/GW calculations, and concludes with a discussion on the role of growth-induced screening.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

In light of the rise of transition metal dichalcogenides as 2D semiconductors for device applications, band engineering becomes very important from an application point of view. In many of these materials, such as the canonical example of MoS$_{2}$, the semiconductor band gap depends on the layer number. It changes from indirect to direct as it evolves from a bulk semiconductor to a monolayer. Interestingly, it was predicted and experimentally confirmed that, by thinning the material from bulk to a bilayer, the indirect transition shows a strong blue-shift. Here, we present the results of scanning tunnelling spectroscopy measurements on MoS$_{2}$ that has been grown \textit{in situ} via molecular beam epitaxy on graphene on Ir(111) at thicknesses ranging from 1 to 5 layers. We find a drastic decrease of the band gap with increasing layer number, to values even below the band gap in bulk. We also observe that the pinning of the conduction band vanishes above 4 layers. Comparing our experimental data with density functional theory and \textit{GW} calculations indicates that an additional screening is introduced by the sample growth conditions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13184"></a>
### [Magnesium-graphene interphase boundaries created by high-pressure torsion enhance hydrogen storage kinetics:Mechanisms and significance of activation energy and frequency factor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13184v1)

**Authors:** Runchen Zhou, Payam Edalati, Anthony Alhayek, Shivam Dangwal, Marc Novelli, Md. Amirul Islam, Baran Bidyut Saha, Thierry Grosdidier, Kaveh Edalati  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13184v1>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a method to speed up hydrogen storage in magnesium by using high-pressure torsion to create magnesium-graphene interfaces. These interfaces act as high-speed pathways for hydrogen diffusion, significantly increasing the reaction frequency without changing the energy barrier.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The slow hydrogen absorption and desorption kinetics of magnesium, which is a promising but kinetically limited hydrogen storage material.

**Main result.** Creating magnesium-graphene interphase boundaries via high-pressure torsion enhances hydrogen storage kinetics by significantly increasing the frequency factor, without altering the activation energy.

**Method.** High-pressure torsion (HPT) is used for severe plastic deformation and grain refinement, followed by Sieverts-type measurements for kinetics and Kissinger analysis for thermal parameters.

**Model / system.** Magnesium (Mg) and magnesium-graphene composites (0, 2, 5, and 10 wt% graphene) processed via high-pressure torsion to create nanostructured bimodal morphologies.

**Key observables.** Hydrogen absorption/desorption rates, hydrogen storage capacity (wt%), activation energy (Ea), frequency factor (A), and grain size.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (623 K), hydrogen pressure (4.03 MPa absorption, 0.01 MPa desorption), and graphene weight percentage.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that the kinetic improvement is driven by the density of active sites rather than changes in the fundamental energy barrier.

**Figures summary.** XRD profiles for phase identification; SEM/EBSD/TEM images showing grain refinement and graphene distribution; DSC/TGA data for thermal analysis; and Kissinger plots showing changes in the frequency factor.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the kinetic bottleneck; experimental synthesis via HPT; microstructural characterization (XRD, SEM, TEM); hydrogen storage kinetic measurements; kinetic modeling and Kissinger analysis; and discussion of the mechanism shift from 3D reaction to 2D diffusion.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

A strategy to overcome sluggish hydrogenation/dehydrogenation of magnesium is demonstrated by creating magnesium-graphene interphase boundaries via high-pressure torsion (HPT). HPT reduces the grain size of pure magnesium from 1 mm to 850 nm, with 70% of grain boundaries having high misorientation angles. Graphene addition leads to even finer grain sizes of 10-500 nm with a bimodal morphology. The magnesium-graphene composites exhibit superior kinetics at 623 K while maintaining high air resistance. Kinetic modeling reveals that the rate-controlling mechanism transits from interfacial reaction in coarse-grained magnesium to atomic diffusion in magnesium-graphene nanocomposites. Kissinger analysis shows that the activation energy for hydrogen desorption remains unchanged at 145 +/- 2 kJ/mol, regardless of the presence of grain or interphase boundaries. However, the frequency factor (number of successful attempts to overcome the activation energy) increases with the generation of interfaces, which serve as sites for hydrogen diffusion and heterogeneous metal/hydride nucleation. These findings highlight the impact of interphase boundary engineering via severe plastic deformation for enhancing the kinetics and air resistance of hydrogen storage materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13763"></a>
### [Magnetization-dependent and stacking-tunable Edelstein effect in two-dimensional magnet 2H-VTe2](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13763v1)

**Authors:** Weiyi Pan, Jaroslav Fabian  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13763v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13763_figures/2605.13763_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. (a) The atomic structure of monolayer 2H-VTe2. (b) The band structure of monolayer 2H-VTe2.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">

**Summary.** This paper explores how the Edelstein effect in the 2D magnet 2H-VTe2 can be tuned by rotating magnetization or changing the stacking configuration of bilayers. It identifies a mechanism for nonvolatile control of spin-orbit torque through interlayer sliding. These findings suggest 2H-MX2 materials are promising for high-efficiency, multifunctional spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the Edelstein effect (current-induced spin accumulation) in 2D magnetic systems can be controlled via magnetization orientation and crystal symmetry (stacking configuration).

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that in 2H-VTe2, the Edelstein effect is magnetization-dependent and that additional spin components can be reversibly switched by changing the stacking from AB to BA via interlayer sliding.

**Method.** First-principles calculations using Quantum Espresso, Wannier90, and WannierTools, with the Edelstein response tensor evaluated using the Kubo formula via the Linres code.

**Model / system.** The system is the 2D in-plane ferromagnetic semiconductor 2H-VTe2, analyzed in both monolayer (D3h symmetry) and bilayer (C3v symmetry) configurations with AB and BA stacking.

**Key observables.** Edelstein response tensor (chi), spin accumulation components (delta S_x, delta S_y, delta S_z), and time-reversal-even/odd contributions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fermi energy (Ef), magnetization angle (phi), electric field (E), and Hubbard U = 1 eV.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reference electric field of 1 V/A used in calculations is noted as difficult to realize experimentally; calculations assume a specific thickness for monolayer and bilayer.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows atomic structure and band structure; Figures 2-3 show symmetry-allowed spin components for monolayers; Figures 4-6 show bilayer structures and stacking-dependent spin accumulation; Figures 7-11 show k-resolved spin accumulation and angular dependence.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of spintronic efficiency, presents the structural and electronic properties of 2H-VTe2, analyzes the monolayer symmetry and spin response, extends the analysis to bilayer stacking and its tunability, and concludes with the generalizability of the findings to other 2H-MX2 materials.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Edelstein effect in magnetic systems enables magnetization switching via the coupling between current-induced spin accumulation and intrinsic magnetic order, and is therefore highly promising for next-generation spintronic devices. Realizing and manipulating the Edelstein effect in two-dimensional (2D) magnetic systems is particularly desirable for achieving high-efficiency and multifunctional spintronic applications. In this work, based on first-principles calculations and symmetry analysis, we demonstrate that the Edelstein effect can intrinsically arise in the 2D in-plane ferromagnetic semiconductor 2H-VTe2, with its behavior strongly dependent on the magnetization orientation. For monolayer 2H-VTe2 with D3h crystal symmetry, under an applied current along the +x direction, only the time-reversal-even z component and the time-reversal-odd y(x) component of the spin accumulation are allowed when the magnetization is aligned along +x (+y). For ferromagnetic bilayer 2H-VTe2 in AB or BA stacking, where the crystal symmetry is reduced to C3v, additional spin components emerge with the presence of in-plane magnetization. Specifically, for magnetization along +x (+y), besides dSz_even and dSy_odd (dSz_even and dSx_odd), extra components such as dSy_even and dSz_odd (dSy_even) become allowed. Notably, these additional components can be reversibly switched by changing the stacking configuration from AB to BA via interlayer sliding. Our results not only deepen the understanding of current-induced spin accumulation in 2D magnetic systems from both symmetry and first-principles perspectives, but also identify 2H-MX2 materials as a promising platform for realizing intrinsic and tunable Edelstein effects in high-efficiency spin-orbit torque devices.

</details>

### [Magnetocaloric Effect in Nanostructured $La_{0.6}Sr_{0.4}Fe_{1-x}Co_{x}O_3$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13611v1)

**Authors:** Fabiana N. Morales Alvarez, Mariano Quintero, Joaquín Sacanell

**Summary.** The study demonstrates how tuning the cobalt content and the physical shape of perovskite nanostructures can enhance the magnetocaloric effect. By using template-assisted synthesis, the researchers achieved higher magnetic entropy changes suitable for intermediate-temperature refrigeration applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Optimizing the magnetocaloric effect (MCE) in nanostructured perovskites by controlling both chemical substitution (Co-doping) and morphological features (nanotube/nanowire diameter).

**Main result.** Increasing Co content enhances ferromagnetic coupling, increasing saturation magnetization and Curie temperature, with the x=1, 800nm sample achieving a maximum entropy change of 1.13 J/(kg K).

**Method.** Synthesis via a pore-wetting method using polymeric membranes, followed by structural characterization (XRD, SEM) and magnetic measurements (VSM) using Maxwell relations for MCE analysis.

**Model / system.** A series of nanostructured perovskite oxides, La0.6Sr0.4Fe1-xCoxO3, featuring rhombohedral symmetry and morphologies including nanotubes and nanowires with diameters of 200 nm and 800 nm.

**Key observables.** Magnetic entropy change (-delta S), saturation magnetization (MS), Curie temperature (TC), Relative Cooling Power (RCP), and coercive field (HC).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cobalt substitution level (x = 0 to 1), pore diameter (200 nm vs 800 nm), and applied magnetic field (up to 3 T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of Maxwell relations assumes the magnetic transitions are second-order, which was confirmed via Arrott plots.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows XRD patterns; Figure 2 displays SEM micrographs of morphology evolution; Figure 3 shows magnetic properties; Figure 7 plots magnetic entropy change vs. temperature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the research problem, describes the synthesis and characterization methods, presents structural and morphological results, details the magnetic and magnetocaloric findings, and concludes with the optimization strategy.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work presents a systematic study of the magnetocaloric effect in the nanostructured perovskite series $La_{0.6}Sr_{0.4}Fe_{1-x}Co_{x}O_3$ (x = 0, 0.2, 0.5, 0.8, and 1.0), synthesized by a pore-wetting method using polymeric membranes with pore diameters of 200 nm and 800 nm. All samples were calcined at 1000°C. Structural characterization was made by X-ray diffraction and confirmed the formation of a single-phase perovskite with distorted rhombohedral symmetry, without detectable secondary phases. We observed significant influence of substitution of Fe by Co on the morphology, as the analysis by scanning electron microscopy revealed a clear evolution from smaller to larger particles and from thin to thicker nanotubes, as the Co content increased. Magnetic measurements showed that the cationic substitution enhances ferromagnetic coupling, increasing both the saturation magnetization (MS) and the Curie temperature (TC). The magnetocaloric properties, determined through the Maxwell relations, exhibit a maximum entropy change of 1.13 J/(kg K) under an applied field of 3 T for the sample with x = 1. These results demonstrate that the combination of Co doping and controlled nanostructuring effectively optimizes the magnetocaloric response.

</details>

### [Multiple Softening Q-vectors Driving a Cascade of CDW Phases in $\mathrm{1T-VSe}_{2}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13458v1)

**Authors:** Zheng-Hong Li, Yung-Ting Lee, Yu-Chan Tai, Cheng-Tien Chiang, Chien-Cheng Kuo, Meng-Kai Lin, Chun-Liang Lin, Hung-Chung Hsueh, Ming-Chiang Chung, Po-Tuan Chen, Chi-Cheng Lee  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13458v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<img src="2605.13458_figures/2605.13458_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">

**Summary.** This paper reveals that the complex variety of charge density wave phases in monolayer VSe2 is not a collection of isolated states, but a continuous cascade of structural transitions driven by phonon instabilities. By tracking how different unstable modes lead to the same stable ground state, the authors provide a systematic framework for understanding hierarchical phase formation in 2D materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding the complex, hierarchical structural evolution and the causal relationships between competing Charge Density Wave (CDW) phases in monolayer 1T-VSe2.

**Main result.** The study identifies a phonon-driven cascade mechanism where multiple unstable wave vectors drive sequential symmetry-lowering distortions that eventually converge toward a common, low-energy stable configuration.

**Method.** First-principles Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations using the OpenMX package combined with an iterative phonon-driven relaxation algorithm to identify subsequent instabilities.

**Model / system.** Monolayer 1T-VSe2, a two-dimensional transition-metal dichalcogenide (TMD) undergoing structural phase transitions.

**Key observables.** Phonon dispersion/spectra, imaginary-frequency phonon modes, CDW ordering vectors (Q-vectors), and total energy changes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Wave vectors QA, QB, and QC; supercell sizes; energy reduction per atom (meV/atom).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study relies on the accuracy of the GGA-PBE exchange-correlation functional and the finite-displacement method for phonon calculations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the phonon-driven cascade including phonon dispersions and lattice distortions; Figure 2 displays the phonon dispersion of the pristine phase along high-symmetry paths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of CDW hierarchy, describes the DFT and iterative phonon analysis methodology, presents the identification of first-generation and second-generation CDW phases, demonstrates the convergence of different pathways to a common ground state, and concludes with a unified framework for CDW formation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Charge density wave (CDW) formation in two-dimensional materials is governed by complex competing lattice instabilities that remain incompletely understood. Here, we investigate the structural evolution of monolayer $\mathrm{1T-VSe}_{2}$ using first-principles electronic and phonon calculations. The pristine phase exhibits several imaginary-frequency phonon modes associated with dominant instability wave vectors $\mathrm{Q}_{CDW}$, which generate the first-generation CDW phases. Subsequent phonon analyses reveal that several of these intermediate structures remain dynamically unstable and undergo further symmetry-lowering distortions into larger superstructures. Through iterative phonon-driven relaxations, we identify multiple transformation pathways that converge toward the same low-energy $2\sqrt{3}\times4$ CDW configuration. Although these pathways originate from distinct intermediate CDW states, they ultimately reach nearly degenerate energetically stable phases, demonstrating that different phonon-driven routes can lead to the same ground-state configuration. The results establish a unified phonon-driven cascade mechanism for hierarchical CDW formation in monolayer $\mathrm{1T-VSe}_{2}$ and provide a systematic framework for understanding competing ordered phases in low-dimensional quantum materials.

</details>

### [Nodal Topological Superconductivity Driven by Crystalline Antiunitary Symmetry in Altermagnets](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13656v1)

**Authors:** Xiao Xiao, Arun Bansil

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates how the unique symmetry of altermagnets can naturally drive nodal topological superconductivity. It identifies specific phases hosting Majorana flat bands and edge states that are robust even when certain symmetries are spontaneously broken. The study provides specific tunneling signatures to help experimentalists identify these phases in candidate materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of realizing intrinsic topological superconductivity without relying on engineered platforms, strong spin-orbit coupling, or fine-tuned symmetry breaking.

**Main result.** The discovery of a symmetry-driven mechanism in altermagnets where crystalline antiunitary symmetry forces spin-singlet and spin-triplet mixing, leading to robust nodal-point and nodal-loop topological superconducting phases.

**Method.** Group theory (projection operators and Wigner test) for pairing classification, solving the linearized gap equation using Matsubara Green's functions, and topological analysis via winding numbers and Z2 invariants.

**Model / system.** A two-sublattice square lattice model of fourfold rotational collinear altermagnets featuring momentum-dependent spin splitting and crystalline antiunitary symmetry (T C4z).

**Key observables.** Superconducting transition temperature (Tc), Majorana flat bands (MFBs), chiral Majorana edge states, and tunneling conductance signatures in altermagnet-half-metal junctions.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hopping parameters (t1-t5), exchange coupling (J), chemical potential (mu), interaction strengths (V0, V1), and the gap scale (D0 = 0.1t1).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the regime of fourfold rotational collinear altermagnets and assumes the validity of the linearized gap equation approach.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the lattice and spin-split band structure; Fig 2 presents the phase diagram of superconducting instabilities; Fig 3 illustrates edge states and phase winding; Fig 4 shows gap distributions and nodal loop spectra.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the altermagnetic system, applies group theory to classify pairing channels, solves the gap equation to find instabilities, analyzes the resulting topological phases (nodal points and loops), and concludes with proposed experimental tunneling signatures.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Topological superconductivity hosts protected quasiparticles and is central to topological quantum computation, yet its realization in intrinsic materials remains challenging and often relies on engineered platforms. Here we uncover a symmetry-constrained mechanism for nodal topological superconductivity in altermagnets. Focusing on fourfold rotational collinear altermagnets, we show that the native crystalline antiunitary symmetry $\mathcal{T}C_{4z}$ generically forbids pure spin-singlet pairing and selects pairing structures that admit Bogoliubov-de Gennes (BdG) Hamiltonians with emergent chiral symmetries. These symmetries further give rise to robust nodal topological phases over broad parameter regimes, including a nodal-point phase hosting Majorana flat bands (MFBs) and two distinct nodal-loop phases with chiral Majorana edge states. Notably, the nodal structure persists even after spontaneous breaking of the antiunitary symmetry, indicating that the topology originates from symmetry-constrained pairing rather than direct symmetry protection. Finally, we propose tunneling signatures that can distinguish these nodal phases and probe symmetry breaking experimentally.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.13618"></a>
### [OpenAaaS: An Open Agent-as-a-Service Framework for Distributed Materials-Informatics Research](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13618v1)

**Authors:** Peng Kang, Bixuan Li, Xiaoya Huang, Shuo Shi, Weiqiao Zhou, Zhen Li, Yu Liu, Lei Zheng  
**Type:** both · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13618v1>

Master Agents (Kimi CLI, Codex, Pi-mono, or custom systems) interact with the OpenAaaS Network Hub (Server + Agent Core + SQLite Module), which routes tasks to specialized Sub-agents deployed as near-data execution nodes. Sub-agents expose local resources—HEA databases, self-driving labs, materials design platforms, knowledge bases—as composable network services without migrating raw data.</figcaption>

During intent rewriting, it preserves materials-specific entities, including alloy designations, phase names, heat-treatment conditions, property terms, and characterization methods. This preservation is important because small terminology changes can substantially shift the literature scope, especially...</figcaption>
</figure>

Each stage preserves intermediate states for failure-mode isolation and end-to-end...</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper introduces OpenAaaS, a framework that allows researchers to use AI agents to query and analyze materials data located at different institutions without moving the raw data. This approach preserves data privacy and solves the bandwidth bottleneck of transferring massive datasets, enabling a secure, interconnected network for automated materials discovery.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The 'last mile' problem in materials informatics: the lack of organizational infrastructure to securely integrate specialized, proprietary, or large-scale materials data across institutional boundaries without compromising data sovereignty.

**Main result.** The development of OpenAaaS, a distributed Agent-as-a-Service framework that enables multi-agent collaboration using a 'code flows, data stays still' principle, successfully demonstrated via high-performance literature analysis (AlphaAgent) and secure processing of a 17.4 TB high-entropy alloy database.

**Method.** A three-tier hierarchical architecture (Master Agent, Network Hub, and Network Node) utilizing the Model Context Protocol (MCP) for near-data execution within Docker sandboxes to prevent raw data migration.

**Model / system.** An open-source distributed framework for materials informatics, featuring a Master Agent layer for task orchestration, a Rust-based Network Hub for routing, and localized Agent Cores for executing tasks near proprietary datasets.

**Key observables.** AlphaAgent performance scores (4.66/5.0 on analytical questions), data transfer reduction (7 orders of magnitude smaller than raw database size), and alloy ductility improvements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 17.4 TB database scale, 194-dimensional descriptor vectors, 5.4 x 10^10 candidate compositions, and 50 MB file relay limit.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the integrity of API-key-based authentication and relies on Docker sandboxing for security; assumes that nodes can initiate outbound connections via reverse polling to bypass firewalls.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the hierarchical architecture of the framework; Figure 2 illustrates the AlphaAgent workflow/skill composition; Figure 3 compares centralized vs. near-data computing models; Figure 4 shows the HEA-Executor sub-agent workflow; Figure 5 demonstrates the task submission interface and results.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the materials informatics 'last mile' problem, description of the OpenAaaS architecture and design principles, detailed implementation of the three-tier system, case study 1 (AlphaAgent for literature analysis), case study 2 (HEA-Executor for large-scale alloy database), and discussion of future directions and limitations.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Materials Genome Initiative catalyzed the proliferation of centralized platforms--SaaS, PaaS, and IaaS--that aggregate computational and experimental resources for accelerated materials discovery. In parallel, breakthroughs in large language models (LLMs) and autonomous agents have created powerful new reasoning capabilities for scientific research. Yet a critical "last mile" problem remains: while we possess world-class models and vast repositories of materials data, we lack the organizational infrastructure to compose these capabilities securely across institutional boundaries. The development of structural and functional materials for harsh service environments--high-temperature alloys, radiation resistant steels, corrosion-resistant coatings--remains characterized by long-term iteration, mechanistic complexity, and high domain expertise--demands that exceed both monolithic agent systems and traditional centralized platforms. To address this gap we propose OpenAaaS, an open-source hierarchical and distributed Agent-as-a-Service framework that enables organized multi-agent collaboration for intelligent materials design. OpenAaaS is built on a single foundational principle: code flows, data stays still. A Master Agent plans and decomposes complex research tasks without requiring direct access to subordinate agents' managed data and computational resources. Sub-agents, deployed as near-data execution nodes, retain full sovereignty over local datasets, proprietary algorithms, and specialized hardware. This architecture guarantees that raw data never leaves its domain of origin while enabling cross-scale, cross-domain secure integration of previously isolated materials intelligence silos. We validate the framework through two representative case studies: (i) AlphaAgent, an evidence-grounded materials literature analysis executor that achieves 4.66/5.0 on deep analytical questions against single-pass RAG baselines; and (ii) an ultra-large-scale hexa-high-entropy alloy descriptor database service that demonstrates secure near-data execution and domain-specific scientific workflows under strict data-sovereignty constraints. OpenAaaS establishes a principled pathway toward "organized research" via agent collectives, offering a scalable foundation for next-generation materials intelligent design platforms. All source code is available at https://github.com/Wolido/OpenAaaS.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper provides direct magnetic evidence for chiral superconductivity in rhombohedral graphene and shows that the superconducting state inherits its domain structure from a parent isospin-polarized phase. It demonstrates that these chiral domains can be switched reversibly with extremely low currents, suggesting potential for ultra-low-power electronic functionality.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the origin of chiral superconductivity (CSC) in rhombohedral multilayer graphene and whether its time-reversal symmetry breaking is inherited from the underlying isospin-polarized parent state.

**Main result.** The study establishes that CSC in rhombohedral pentalayer graphene inherits its chiral domain structure from the isospin-polarized 1/4M phase and demonstrates that these domains can be deterministically reconfigured using ultra-low currents.

**Method.** Nanoscale SQUID-on-tip (SOT) magnetometry is used for spatial imaging of magnetic textures, combined with transport measurements and current-driven manipulation of isospin domains.

**Model / system.** Rhombohedral pentalayer graphene (R5G) at high displacement fields, characterized by a spin-valley-polarized quarter-metal (1/4M) phase and a superconducting phase.

**Key observables.** Out-of-plane magnetization (Mz), differential magnetization (mz), longitudinal resistance (Rxx), Hall resistance (Rxy), and stray field (Bz,ac).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (down to 20 mK), carrier density (n), displacement field (D), and current-driven switching at the nanoampere scale.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The switching mechanism is assumed to be driven by linear momentum transfer from reflected electrons rather than spin-transfer torque.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows device geometry and gate control; Figure 2 illustrates transport hysteresis and the identification of domain walls; Figure 3 details the transition from bubble to system-spanning domain walls; Figure 4 and 5 demonstrate current-driven domain expansion, contraction, and resistance switching.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the R5G system, uses SOT imaging to prove TRS breaking in the CSC phase, demonstrates the inheritance of order from the 1/4M phase, analyzes the transport regimes (LR vs HR) caused by domain walls, and concludes with the demonstration of reversible, current-controlled domain reconfiguration.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Rhombohedral multilayer graphene at high displacement fields hosts superconductivity emerging from a spin valley polarized quarter metal, with transport signatures suggestive of time reversal symmetry (TRS) breaking and chiral superconductivity (CSC). These observations have motivated proposals of topological superconductivity and non-Abelian quasiparticles, yet direct magnetic evidence and microscopic insight into the superconducting state remain lacking, limiting understanding of this unique state. Here we use nanoscale SQUID on tip magnetometry to image isospin-polarized domains in rhombohedral pentalayer graphene and establish CSC via spatially resolved thermodynamic detection of TRS breaking. We find that the density at which domain walls proliferate at elevated temperatures coincides with the onset of CSC, indicating an underlying transition in the parent state that both induces superconductivity and reduces domain wall energy. We further show that the chiral domain structure in the superconducting phase is inherited from the isospin-polarized parent state. Strikingly, the CSC phase exhibits multiple transport regimes governed by configurations of chiral domains separated by highly resistive domain walls. We demonstrate deterministic, ultra low current control of these domains, enabling reversible switching between states of opposite chirality a defining CSC property absent in other superconductors. These results establish rhombohedral graphene as a unique platform for reconfigurable CSC and ultra low power electronic functionality based on controllable isospin textures.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13826"></a>
### [Reducing cross-sample prediction churn in scientific machine learning](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13826v1)

**Authors:** Gordan Prastalo, Kevin Maik Jablonka  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13826v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">

<img src="2605.13826_figures/2605.13826_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> Figure 1: Twin-bootstrap eliminates most of ERM’s retraining-induced prediction flips on BACE. Each row is one of the 80 test molecules with the largest cross-sample contrast; each column is one of ten retrainings on an independent bootstrap of the BACE training pool; cells are coloured by predicted class. Visible vertical stripes in the left (ERM) panel are predictions that flip class across retrainings; under twin-bootstrap (right; λ=300, matched compute against bagging-K=2) the same molecules become near-uniform. The mean class-flip rate over the full test set drops from 16.1% (ERM) to 5.7% (twin-bootstrap).</figcaption>

Left: paired ∆id-churn vs. ERM for six methods, one row per dataset (smallest N at top), 95% paired-bootstrap CIs across the 45 seed pairs. Vertical reference lines mark each method’s across-dataset mean (twin-bootstrap solid, others dashed; colours match markers); the solid black line is parity with ERM. Right: ∆vs. matched-compute bagging-K=2, twin-bootstrap only; CI excludes zero on 7/8 datasets and ties on DILI. Frozen λ=300 is selected on the BACE development dataset alone; no held-out tuning.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13826_figures/2605.13826_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> Figure 3: Routing the top 30% of test predictions (ranked by per-example churn from one extra retraining) to a human reviewer captures 58–100% of all retraining-induced class flips on the 9 chemistry datasets. Each curve is one dataset (BACE in red, held-out in grey); x-axis is the fraction of test predictions reviewed in churn-rank order, y-axis is the cumulative fraction of total flip-mass captured. The diagonal marks random ranking; the shaded band marks the practical review fraction. The triage workflow is a one-bootstrap operation on top of an existing pipeline.</figcaption>

Twin-bootstrap at λ ∈{1, 3, 10, 30, 100, 300} traces an accuracy-vs.-churn trajectory; the pre-registered selection rule (largest λ with id-acc ≥ERM-id-acc −0.02) picks λ=300 (filled red). Bagging-K=5 (blue circle) achieves a similar accuracy at higher churn; ERM (open square, upper-right) at the highest churn. Error bars are bootstrap 95% CIs.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13826_figures/2605.13826_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> Figure 5: Twin-bootstrap eliminates per-molecule retraining flips on BACE. Six id-test molecules where ERM flips class on ≥36% of seed pairs and twin-bootstrap flips on 0%. Below each structure: ten ERM and ten twin-bootstrap seed predictions, each cell is the predicted class at one retraining; the gap separates the two methods. ERM stripes flip; twin-bootstrap collapses to a single colour.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper identifies a hidden instability in scientific machine learning where individual predictions flip between training iterations even when accuracy remains stable. It proposes a 'twin-bootstrap' training method that uses a consistency loss to stabilize these predictions, making downstream experimental decisions more reproducible.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The phenomenon of 'cross-sample prediction churn,' where machine learning model predictions (e.g., molecular class labels) change significantly when the model is retrained on different random draws of the same training data, despite stable aggregate accuracy.

**Main result.** The proposed 'twin-bootstrap' method significantly reduces prediction churn (median 65% reduction) and distributional disagreement (sym-KL) with minimal accuracy loss, outperforming standard parameter-side methods like Deep Ensembles and MC Dropout.

**Method.** Twin-bootstrap, which involves training two networks jointly on independent bootstraps of the training set using a symmetric KL (sym-KL) divergence consistency loss.

**Model / system.** The study evaluates scientific machine learning models (MLPs, GIN, ChemBERTa, ResNet) applied to chemistry and materials science datasets, including MoleculeNet, TDC ADME/Tox, and various molecular property benchmarks.

**Key observables.** Argmax churn (class-flip rate), symmetric KL divergence (sym-KL), Jaccard overlap of top-K predictions, and in-distribution/out-of-distribution accuracy.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The regularization strength (lambda) of the consistency loss and the 0.02-tolerance rule for hyperparameter selection.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes that bootstrapping from a single observed training set is a valid proxy for true population-level churn and that the models are trained on iid draws.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares prediction stability between ERM and Twin-bootstrap; Figure 2 shows the relationship between churn and ERM across datasets; Figure 4 illustrates the accuracy-vs-churn Pareto frontier; Figure 5 provides a case study of class flips in BACE molecules.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of prediction churn, quantifies its magnitude in chemistry benchmarks, demonstrates the failure of existing uncertainty methods, proposes the twin-bootstrap method, validates it across various architectures and tasks, and provides a rule for hyperparameter selection.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Scientific machine learning reports predictive performance. It does not report whether the same prediction would survive a different draw of training data. Across $9$ chemistry benchmarks, two classifiers trained on independent bootstraps of the same training set agree on aggregate accuracy to within $1.3\text{--}4.2$ percentage points but disagree on the class label of $8.0\text{--}21.8\%$ of test molecules. We call this gap \emph{cross-sample prediction churn}. The standard parameter-side techniques (deep ensembles, MC dropout, stochastic weight averaging) do not reduce this gap; two data-side methods do. The first is $K$-bootstrap bagging, which cuts the rate $40\text{--}54\%$ on every dataset at no accuracy cost ($K{\times}$-ERM compute). The second is \emph{twin-bootstrap}, our proposal: two networks trained jointly on independent bootstraps with a sym-KL consistency loss between their predictions, which at matched $2{\times}$-ERM compute reduces churn a further median $45\%$ beyond bagging-$K{=}2$. Cross-sample prediction churn deserves a column alongside predictive performance in scientific-ML benchmark reports, because without it the parameter-side and data-side methods are indistinguishable on the metric they actually differ on.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12986"></a>
### [Selective Octahedral Accommodation of Cr$^{3+}$ and Weak Magnetic Connectivity in the Sugilite Analogue KNa$_2$Cr$_2$Li$_3$Si$_{12}$O$_{30}$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12986v1)

**Authors:** Yuya Haraguchi, Taishu Aoki, Daisuke Nishio-Hamane, Hiroko Aruga Katori  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12986v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>

</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that Cr3+ ions in a milarite-type structure occupy octahedral sites with high selectivity, preventing significant antisite exchange. It reveals that while the structure forms a honeycomb lattice, the magnetic interactions are weak because the intervening Li+ ions act as electronic insulators in the exchange path.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether Cr3+ ions selectively occupy the octahedral A-site or undergo antisite exchange with the tetrahedral T2 site in a milarite-type framework, and examines the resulting magnetic connectivity.

**Main result.** The researchers proved that Cr3+ is selectively accommodated in the octahedral A-site with negligible T2 occupancy, and found that the magnetic connectivity is weak due to the inert Li+ ions in the exchange pathway.

**Method.** The study uses solid-state synthesis, Rietveld refinement of powder X-ray diffraction data, Maximum Entropy Method (MEM) analysis, and magnetic susceptibility measurements.

**Model / system.** The system is KNa2Cr2Li3Si12O30, a chromium analogue of the mineral sugilite, featuring a milarite-type framework with a honeycomb-like Cr sublattice and an extended Cr-O-Li-O-Cr exchange path.

**Key observables.** Antisite parameter (x), lattice parameters, magnetic susceptibility, Curie-Weiss temperature (theta_W), and effective magnetic moment (mu_eff).

**Important parameters / regimes.** theta_W = -4.78(7) K, mu_eff = 3.763(6) uB, and Cr-O distances of approximately 1.948 A.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The structural analysis assumes a composition-conserving antisite model for Cr/Li exchange.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the projected honeycomb arrangement of the Cr sublattice and the Cr-O-Li-O-Cr magnetic coupling pathway; Figure 4 displays temperature-dependent magnetic susceptibility and its Curie-Weiss fit.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Cr-analogue of sugilite, details the synthesis and structural characterization (XRD, MEM), presents magnetic susceptibility data, and concludes with design rules for creating honeycomb and kagome magnets.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report the synthesis of the Cr analogue of sugilite, KNa$_2$Cr$_2$Li$_3$Si$_{12}$O$_{30}$, in the milarite-type framework. Rietveld refinement of a composition-conserving antisite model gives $x = 0.0024(18)$ in KNa$_2$[Cr$_{2-x}$Li$_x$][Li$_{3-x}$Cr$_x$]Si$_{12}$O$_{30}$, corresponding to a T2-site Cr occupancy of $0.0008(6)$. X-ray MEM analysis shows no detectable Cr-like density at T2. Magnetic susceptibility indicates weak antiferromagnetic interactions with $θ_{\mathrm{W}} = -4.78(7)$ K and no ordering above 1.8 K.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13722"></a>
### [Shubnikov-de Haas Characterization of Superconductor-Semiconductor Heterostructures](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13722v1)

**Authors:** A. M. Zimmerman, Saeed Fallahi, Sergei Gronin, Tyler Lindemann, Patrick Sohr, Ray Kallaher, Alejandro Alcaraz Ramirez, Georg W. Winkler, Samuel M. L. Teicher, William Cole, Sebastian Heedt, Eoin O'Farrell, Gijs de Lange, Roman Lutchyn, Michael J. Manfra, John Watson  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13722v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13722_figures/2605.13722_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. Raw magnetoresistance R(B) showing SdH oscillations on a magnetoresistance background from the combined semi- conductor metal system. Data is from a sample presented in Fig. 4. Inset: schematic of the heterostructure, Al/top barrier (TB)/QW/back barrier (BB), with cyclotron orbits in the QW. Arrows indicate disorder scattering (τ) and escape into the metal (Γ).</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13722_figures/2605.13722_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. SdH analysis steps for the data shown in Fig. 1. (a) Background-subtracted oscillations ∆R vs. 1/B showing beating from spin-orbit coupling. (b) Inset of panel c: FFT of ∆R(1/B) for B &lt; 1 T, showing two spin-split peaks and Lorentzian fit (green). (c) Raw R(B) with two-channel magnetoresistance fit (red dashed). The fit is performed over the entire data range (d) ∆R vs. 1/B with full SdH model fit (red, dashed) used to extract τq. Fit is performed for B &lt; 0.7 T.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13722_figures/2605.13722_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. SdH-extracted parameters vs. Sb fraction in the QW for InAs1−xSbx quantum wells. (a) Rashba SOC α. (b) Car- rier density n. (c) Transport (τt, circles) and quantum (τq, squares) scattering times. (d) Ratio τt/τq.</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13722_figures/2605.13722_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> FIG. 4. Induced superconducting gap ∆ind vs. transport scat- tering time τt. Data points (circles) are independent QPC and SdH measurements on the same stacks. Curves show the model [Eqs. (1)–(6)] for τt semi = ∞(clean limit), 1.5, and 2.5 ps with ∆0 = 305 µeV.</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new way to quickly characterize the material properties of superconductor-semiconductor heterostructures using simple magnetoresistance measurements. By analyzing SdH oscillations, researchers can extract spin-orbit coupling and scattering times, even estimating the superconducting gap without needing ultra-low temperatures. This provides a much faster feedback loop for optimizing materials used in topological quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for a rapid, non-destructive method to characterize essential material properties (spin-orbit coupling, carrier density, and scattering times) in hybrid superconductor-semiconductor heterostructures without complex nanofabrication or mK measurements.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that Shubnikov-de Haas (SdH) oscillations can extract fundamental parameters and even estimate the proximity-induced superconducting gap by analyzing changes in scattering lifetimes.

**Method.** Analysis of SdH oscillations in unpatterned 2D films using high-pass filtering, FFT for subband density, and two-band conductivity model fitting.

**Model / system.** A hybrid nanostructure consisting of an Aluminum (Al) thin film coupled to a two-dimensional electron gas (2DEG) in an InAs or InAsSb quantum well.

**Key observables.** Carrier density (n), Rashba spin-orbit coupling strength (alpha), transport scattering time (tau_t), quantum scattering time (tau_q), and the ratio tau_t/tau_q.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Metal-semiconductor coupling strength (Gamma), proximity-induced superconducting gap (Delta_ind), and scattering regimes (long-range vs. short-range).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system can be modeled as two parallel coupled electronic bands where the metal has extremely high density and low resistivity.

**Figures summary.** Figure 3 shows extracted parameters (SOC, density, lifetimes, and ratio) vs. Sb fraction in InAsSb; Figure 4 plots the induced gap against the transport lifetime.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the characterization problem, describes the hybrid S-Sm system and the SdH measurement methodology, validates the method using InAsSb alloying, and demonstrates the predictive power for the induced superconducting gap.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hybrid superconductor-semiconductor nanostructures are a central component for research spanning condensed matter physics and quantum information processing. Continued progress relies critically on the ability to characterize, control, and optimize several intrinsic material properties including spin-orbit coupling, band offsets, and disorder in a device-relevant stack that necessarily couples the electronic states of a superconducting metal film and a semiconductor. Here we report a new method to extract fundamental material parameters utilizing simple Shubnikov-de Haas (SdH) oscillation measurements in heterostructures in which metallic electronic states are coupled to a two-dimensional electron gas (2DEG) residing in an InAs quantum well beneath an aluminum thin film. Proper analysis of the full magnetoresistance data facilitates extraction of the quantum well carrier density, spin-orbit coupling strength, and both transport and quantum scattering times. Most importantly, the extracted scattering times in the 2DEG are impacted by the metal-semiconductor coupling strength allowing us to quickly gain information on proximity-induced superconducting gap without any fabrication or mK measurements. The wealth of information that is accessed with these simple measurements positions this methodology as an important tool for hybrid materials optimization.

</details>
