<a id="paper-2605.13477"></a>
### [Space-Charge Effects in Silicon Reconfigurable Nonlinear-Processing Units](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13477v1)

**Authors:** Jonas Kareem, Lorenzo Cassola, Reinier J. C. Cool, Janiek I. van Slooten, Peter A. Bobbert, Wilfred G. van der Wiel  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.13477v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<div class="figure-strip-hint">↔ Scroll figures horizontally</div>
<div class="paper-figures-scroll">
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13477_figures/2605.13477_fig1.jpg" alt="Figure 1">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 1.</b> FIG. 1. RNPU characteristics and logic functionality. (a) Two-terminal I–V characteristics of boron-</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13477_figures/2605.13477_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> FIG. 2. Trap-induced reduction of the free carrier concentration. (a) Arrhenius plots of low-bias</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13477_figures/2605.13477_fig3.jpg" alt="Figure 3">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 3.</b> FIG. 3. Experimental I–V characteristics measured on a boron-implanted region for (a)–(d) transmission</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13477_figures/2605.13477_fig4.jpg" alt="Figure 4">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 4.</b> Fig. 23). In particular, increasing 𝑉c increases the availability of equilibrium free carriers,</figcaption>
</figure>
<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.13477_figures/2605.13477_fig5.jpg" alt="Figure 5">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 5.</b> FIG. 4. TCAD device simulations of p-type TLM structure, highlighting the role of background doping</figcaption>
</figure>
</div>

**Summary.** This paper identifies that the nonlinearity in silicon-based computing primitives arises from space-charge-limited conduction rather than disorder-induced hopping. By demonstrating that the effect is driven by the competition between injected carriers and fixed dopants, the authors provide a framework for creating reproducible, CMOS-compatible nonlinear hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The physical origin of room-temperature nonlinearity in silicon-based Reconfigurable Nonlinear-Processing Units (RNPUs) was poorly understood, with previous models incorrectly suggesting 2D variable-range hopping.

**Main result.** The nonlinearity is driven by space-charge effects, specifically the competition between injected carriers and fixed ionized background dopants, which creates a nonlinear electric-field profile.

**Method.** The study combines temperature- and length-dependent current-voltage measurements with drift-diffusion and TCAD simulations to model carrier transport and trap distributions.

**Model / system.** The system consists of silicon-based RNPUs with implanted boron or arsenic, featuring a silicon/native oxide interface with high surface trap density and a doping gradient created via reactive-ion etching.

**Key observables.** Current-voltage (I-V) characteristics, activation energy (Va), power-law exponent (alpha), crossover voltage (Vcross), and effective injected charge (Neff).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Ohmic, quasi-exponential, and space-charge-limited current (SCLC) regimes; interface trap density; background doping polarity; and electrode geometry.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes Maxwell-Boltzmann statistics for carrier concentration and assumes that high activation energy is due to interface trap-induced Fermi level pinning.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows I-V curves and logic gate demonstrations; Figure 2 displays Arrhenius plots and band diagrams; Figure 3 presents I-V characteristics for TLM structures and SCLC schematics; Figure 4/5/6/7 show TCAD simulations of voltage scaling, dopant profiles, and device architecture.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by characterizing I-V nonlinearity, identifies the role of surface traps in suppressing carriers, proposes a space-charge-limited model, validates it via TCAD simulations, and concludes with design guidelines for scalable hardware.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Reconfigurable nonlinear-processing units (RNPUs) are multi-terminal electronic devices that act as computational primitives, exploiting intrinsic nonlinear charge transport combined with electrostatic tunability. Silicon-based realizations provide a scalable and technologically relevant platform, yet the physical origin of their room-temperature nonlinearity has remained insufficiently understood. Here, we investigate charge transport using temperature- and length-dependent current-voltage measurements on physical devices, complemented by drift-diffusion simulations, and show that transport is governed by space charge. Interface trap states strongly suppress the equilibrium carrier density, while the functional nonlinearity arises from the voltage-dependent competition between injected mobile carriers and fixed ionized background dopants. The resulting non-equilibrium transport exhibits a transition from an Ohmic regime to a strongly nonlinear regime, and ultimately to a velocity-saturation space-charge-limited current regime, as evidenced by the observed voltage and length scaling. We further show that background doping of opposite polarity to the injected carriers controls the onset and strength of the nonlinearity, leading to behavior exceeding the quadratic dependence of the classical Mott-Gurney law. Agreement between experiment and simulation supports that the spatial distribution of injected carriers and fixed charge governs the internal electric-field profile and device response. These results establish a physical framework for silicon-based RNPUs without requiring disorder or hopping transport, and provide design guidelines for reproducible, scalable, and CMOS-compatible implementations of nonlinear computing hardware.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.12605"></a>
### [Stability of localized solutions to lattice dynamical systems](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12605v1)

**Authors:** Bocheng Ruan, Jack M. Hughes, Jason J. Bramburger  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12605v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

Solid lines indicate stability; dashed/dotted lines indicate instability. </figure>

**Summary.** This paper provides a rigorous theoretical framework to predict the stability of complex localized patterns in lattices by analyzing the simpler components that form them. It demonstrates that the number of unstable eigenvalues in multi-pulse or multi-dimensional structures can be calculated from the properties of individual fronts and backs. This is significant for understanding the formation and persistence of localized structures in various physical and biological media.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to characterize the spectral stability of localized steady-state solutions (single-pulse and multi-pulse patterns) in 1D and multi-dimensional rectangular lattice dynamical systems.

**Main result.** The authors develop a framework showing that the stability of complex localized structures can be determined by the stability of their constituent 'front' and 'back' components, specifically proving that the Evans function asymptotically factorizes for well-separated localization regions.

**Method.** The study utilizes a discrete Evans function formulation, exponential dichotomies, and Rouché's theorem to analyze the point spectrum and characterize the essential spectrum.

**Model / system.** The model consists of lattice dynamical systems, specifically a real-valued cubic-quintic Ginzburg-Landau lattice and Nagumo-type systems, representing phenomena in physics, biology, and materials science.

**Key observables.** The number and multiplicity of unstable eigenvalues (point spectrum) and the boundaries of the essential spectrum.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength (theta), bistability parameter (mu), localization width (N), and lattice dimension (M).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on steady-state solutions, assumes sufficiently large separation between localized regions, and is primarily restricted to rectangular lattice geometries.

**Figures summary.** Figures show steady-state solutions (fronts, backs, pulses), bifurcation diagrams illustrating 'snaking' structures, and numerical computations of the largest real-part eigenvalues for 1D and 2D patterns.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of spectral stability, develops a mathematical framework using the discrete Evans function and exponential dichotomies, proves theorems regarding eigenvalue inheritance for single and multi-pulse solutions, and provides numerical demonstrations on Ginzburg-Landau and Nagumo-type lattices in 1D and 2D.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Localized patterns are spatially confined structures that arise in lattice dynamical systems and play an important role in physics, biology, and materials science. While their existence and bifurcation structure are well-understood, the stability of these solutions remains largely unexplored, particularly in discrete and high-dimensional settings. In this work, we develop a general theoretical framework to analyze the spectral stability of localized steady states in one-dimensional and multi-dimensional rectangular lattices. Our approach leverages the properties of front and back solutions, combined with a discrete Evans function formulation, to characterize the spectrum of localized solutions. We prove that, for well-separated regions of localization, the Evans function asymptotically factorizes into contributions from the underlying fronts and backs, allowing explicit counting of unstable eigenvalues. This framework applies to solutions with single or multiple plateaus, including oscillatory and multi-pulse configurations. We illustrate the results on a real-valued cubic-quintic Ginzburg-Landau lattice, a prototypical Nagumo-type system, and provide numerical demonstrations of bifurcation structures and eigenvalue spectra.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.13166"></a>
### [Structural, electronic, and optical properties of hexagonal GeSn from density functional theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.13166v1)

**Authors:** Yetkin Pulcu, János Koltai, Andor Kormányos, Guido Burkard

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that hexagonal GeSn alloys can achieve a direct bandgap at much lower tin concentrations than the standard cubic phase. This allows for efficient tuning of the material's absorption edge into the mid-infrared, making it a promising candidate for new infrared optoelectronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to overcome the indirect bandgap and high Sn-concentration limitations of cubic GeSn by investigating the structural, electronic, and optical properties of the hexagonal (lonsdaleite) GeSn alloy phase for mid-infrared optoelectronics.

**Main result.** The 2H-Ge1-xSnx alloy maintains a direct bandgap at the Gamma point even at low Sn concentrations, exhibiting strong bandgap bowing into the mid-infrared and significant optical polarization anisotropy.

**Method.** First-principles Density Functional Theory (DFT) using the mBJ-LDA functional and spin-orbit coupling, employing Special Quasirandom Structure (SQS) supercells and band unfolding techniques.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the hexagonal (2H/lonsdaleite) germanium-tin (Ge1-xSnx) random alloy in the dilute Sn regime (x <= 0.10), characterized by D6h crystal symmetry.

**Key observables.** Bandgap energy (Eg), lattice parameters (a, c), effective masses (in-plane vs. out-of-plane), absorption coefficient, and optical transition matrix elements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Sn concentration (x <= 0.10), c/a ratio, and the mid-infrared wavelength range.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study focuses on the dilute Sn regime; optical properties are calculated in the independent-particle approximation without GW or BSE corrections; the alloy is modeled using finite-sized SQS supercells.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows atomic configurations and SQS supercells; Figure 2 plots lattice parameter evolution; Figure 3 compares electronic structures; Figure 4 shows effective band structure and spectral weight; Figure 5 shows bandgap evolution; Figure 6 illustrates polarization anisotropy of matrix elements; Figure 7 displays calculated optical absorption spectra.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for hexagonal GeSn, describes the DFT/SQS methodology, analyzes structural evolution, details electronic band structure and effective masses, evaluates optical properties and polarization, and concludes with a comparison to the cubic phase.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Unlike cubic GeSn, which requires a high Sn concentration to undergo an indirect-to-direct bandgap transition, lonsdaleite (2H) germanium is an intrinsic direct-gap semiconductor. We employ first-principles density functional theory to investigate the structural, electronic, and optical properties of 2H-Ge$_{1-x}$Sn$_{x}$ random alloys in the dilute Sn regime ($x \le 0.10$). The extended alloy disorder is modeled using 48-atom special quasirandom structure (SQS) supercells, and the coherent effective band structure is recovered via spectral band unfolding. We show that 2H-Ge$_{1-x}$Sn$_{x}$ maintains a direct bandgap at the $Γ$ point across the studied composition range, exhibiting a strong bandgap bowing that shifts the fundamental absorption edge into the mid-infrared. Evaluation of the optical transition matrix elements reveals a giant polarization anisotropy dictated by spin-orbit coupling. The fundamental transition is strongly dipole-allowed for light polarized perpendicular to the crystal $c$-axis, an optical selection rule that is robustly preserved despite the random alloy disorder breaking the symmetry. These results demonstrate that hexagonal GeSn bypasses the compositional threshold limitations of the cubic phase, providing a highly tunable direct-gap system for infrared optoelectronics.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper uses 3D electron microscopy to reveal that the 'random sponge' morphology in block copolymers is topologically distinct from the double-gyroid because its network loops are unlinked. It identifies the sponge as a single-network, kinetically-trapped precursor to the ordered, interlinked double-gyroid phase.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The structural relationship between ordered polycontinuous networks (like the double-gyroid) and disordered 'random sponge' morphologies remains poorly understood, specifically regarding whether the sponge is a stable phase or a kinetic precursor to order.

**Main result.** The researchers found that the amorphous sponge is a single-network morphology characterized by unlinked loops, whereas the ordered double-gyroid consists of intercatenated loops; the transition between them is a narrow boundary marked by topological changes and 'short-circuiting' bridges.

**Method.** 3D reconstruction using slice-and-view scanning electron microscopy (SVSEM) combined with skeletal graph extraction, topological analysis (Gauss linking integral), and medial geometry analysis of the interface.

**Model / system.** Self-assembled polystyrene-b-polydimethylsiloxane (PS-PDMS) diblock copolymers forming both ordered double-gyroid and amorphous sponge network morphologies.

**Key observables.** Node valence, loop catenation (linking number), node angles, strut length, inter-domain curvature, and mesoatom volume/shape.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Voxel resolution of 3 nm, PDMS volume fraction of 0.41, and the transition zone width of approximately 50-60 nm.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The 50% isocontour of the smoothed intensity is assumed to represent the physical interface, and the skeletal graph assumes that removing dangling branches does not lose essential topological information.

**Figures summary.** SEM images of coexisting phases; 3D renderings of network structures; FFT analysis showing Bragg peaks vs. halos; comparison of mesoatom volumes and curvatures; and visualization of the boundary transition and loop linking.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the problem of ordered vs. disordered networks; Experimental setup and SVSEM methodology; Structural analysis of the ordered phase; Characterization of the amorphous sponge; Comparative analysis of topology and geometry; Boundary analysis between phases; and Discussion of kinetic implications.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many soft matter systems exhibit ordered, polycontinuous network morphologies, such as the cubic (double) gyroid or diamond, as well as disordered network morphologies known generically as ``random sponges". While presumed to share similar local packing geometry, the structural relationship between these ordered and disordered network morphologies has remained obscure. We use slice and view scanning electron microscopy to analyze and compare multi-scale morphological features of an ordered double-gyroid morphology to the amorphous sponge morphology formed in the same block copolymer sample. We find that node valence of the minority component network of the sponge is mostly gyroidal (trivalent), with a small fraction of diamond-like (tetravalent) connections. We analyze mesoatoms -- space-filling volumes occupied by chains around each network node -- finding significant differences in shape and size between ordered and amorphous regions. Local block thickness and inter-domain curvature within mesoatomic units of the disordered sponge exhibits a surprisingly similar degree of dispersity to the ordered double-gyroid. The mean differences in local packing geometry derive from topological distinction: loops of the minority networks of the ordered double-gyroid are intercatenated, while loops of the disordered sponge are not. In this way, the sponge may be viewed as disordered variant of a single-gyroidal morphology. We exploit these topological differences to demarcate the boundary region between ordered and disordered networks and highlight modulations of the mesoatom motifs at the boundary. These observations point to new questions about potential metastability of disordered networks and their possible role as kinetic precursors to long-range ordered network morphologies.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.12861"></a>
### [Ultraviolet plastic scintillators based on naphthalene-doped polystyrene and polyvinyltoluene](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12861v1)

**Authors:** Nicolás Agustín Molina, Victoria Alejandra Gómez Andrade, Javier Martín Abbas, Alan Fuster, Luciano Ferreyro, Martín Alurralde, Martin Mirenda, María Dolores Pérez, Paula Guidici

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that doping polystyrene and polyvinyltoluene with naphthalene enhances their ultraviolet light emission and improves their resistance to radiation damage. The resulting Naph-doped PVT scintillator is a promising candidate for use in compact, radiation-resistant detectors for space and high-energy physics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for radiation-hardened, UV-sensitive plastic scintillators that can match the response window of GaN-based HEMTs for use in extreme environments like space.

**Main result.** Naphthalene (Naph) doping significantly enhances the light yield and UV emission of polystyrene and polyvinyltoluene matrices, with Naph-doped PVT showing superior radiation tolerance to proton irradiation.

**Method.** Fabrication of polymer films via toluene dissolution, followed by photoluminescence (PL), time-resolved PL (TRPL), and scintillation light yield measurements, alongside 10 MeV proton irradiation tests.

**Model / system.** Plastic scintillator films composed of Polystyrene (Ps) and Polyvinyltoluene (PVT) matrices doped with varying concentrations (0-20% w/w) of naphthalene.

**Key observables.** Emission wavelength/spectrum, decay lifetimes, scintillation light yield (photons per MeV), and radiation-induced light yield reduction.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dopant concentration (0-20% w/w), proton irradiation dose (approx. 3 Mrad), and emission peaks (e.g., 335 and 350 nm for PVT).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of 10 MeV protons is assumed to be a valid simulation of space-related radiation damage; light yield measurements were performed on partially opaque samples to avoid detector saturation.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows photographs of the scintillator films; Fig 3 displays absorption factors and PL spectra showing red shifts and bimodal emission; Fig 4 shows TRPL decay curves; Table I summarizes lifetimes, emission maxima, and light yields.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the radiation-hardness problem, description of material fabrication, characterization of optical properties (PL, TRPL, absorption), evaluation of scintillation performance, radiation damage testing via proton irradiation, and concluding remarks on PVT-based detectors.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

This work reports the fabrication and optical characterization of ultraviolet-emitting plastic scintillators based on polystyrene [-(CH2-CH(C6H5))n-] and polyvinyltoluene [-(CH2-CH(C6H4CH3))n-] doped with different concentrations of naphthalene (Naph). Photoluminescence (PL) measurements show that Naph incorporation enhances the emission intensity, inducing a red shift in the emission wavelength of the polystyrene (PS), while Naph-doped polyvinyltoluene (PVT) exhibits a bimodal emission with peaks around 335 and 350 nm. Time-resolved photoluminescence (TRPL) reveals fast decay components for the undoped matrices and a dominant slow component of approximately 87 ns in the doped samples. Scintillation light yield measurements indicate moderate performance for the undoped polymers and a significant enhancement upon Naph doping. Proton irradiation experiments reveal a reduction in light yield for all samples, with Naph-doped PVT retaining a larger fraction of its initial light yield compared to PS-based scintillators, indicating improved radiation tolerance. Overall, these results demonstrate the effectiveness of Naph as a UV emission enhancer and highlight Naph-doped PVT as a promising candidate for compact and radiation-resistant scintillation detectors.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.12687"></a>
### [Using a spin-triplet encoding to enhance shuttling fidelities in Si/SiGe quantum wells](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12687v1)

**Authors:** Merritt P. R. Losert, S. N. Coppersmith, Mark Friesen  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.12687v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

When the dot travels through a region with small valley splitting Ev, Landau-Zener (LZ) processes can excite the electron into an excited valley state. (d) En- ergies versus position x of...</figcaption>

<figure class="paper-figure-card">
<img src="2605.12687_figures/2605.12687_fig2.jpg" alt="Figure 2">
<figcaption class="paper-figure-caption"><b>Fig 2.</b> Figure 2. Infidelities obtained when shuttling spin triplets (Tr. Inf.) for different disorder and confinement strengths. </figure>
</div>

**Summary.** The paper proposes an unconventional two-electron spin-triplet encoding for Si/SiGe quantum dots to mitigate errors caused by valley-splitting variations during spin shuttling. This scheme significantly outperforms conventional single-electron shuttling by suppressing Landau-Zener leakage. The study demonstrates that high fidelities are achievable even in the presence of significant alloy disorder.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a novel encoding strategy to protect quantum information against environmental-induced leakage, which is a fundamental problem in open quantum systems and hardware-efficient quantum computing.

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Spatial variations in valley splitting in Si/SiGe quantum wells cause Landau-Zener-like excitations during spin shuttling, leading to leakage from the qubit subspace.

**Main result.** A two-electron spin-triplet encoding is proposed that is largely immune to valley-induced leakage, achieving fidelities 2-3 orders of magnitude higher than single-electron schemes.

**Method.** Numerical simulations using Full Configuration Interaction (FCI) with effective-mass and tight-binding models, combined with Schrieffer-Wolff transformations and time-evolution propagators.

**Model / system.** Two-electron spin-triplet qubits in Si/SiGe heterostructure quantum wells using a conveyor-mode shuttling architecture with oscillating sinusoidal potentials.

**Key observables.** Trace infidelity, leakage error rates, valley splitting, and orbital excitation energies.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Shuttling velocity (1-100 m/s), valley splitting (1.5-300 ueV), orbital confinement (2-6 meV), and disorder strength (10-50 ueV).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes orbital splitting is much larger than valley splitting; excited orbital states are treated perturbatively; interface steps are treated as a weak perturbation.

**Figures summary.** The figures illustrate two-electron orbital energies, trace infidelities across different disorder and confinement levels, and the scaling of errors with shuttling velocity.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of valley-induced leakage, proposes a two-electron encoding, details the Hamiltonian and leakage mechanisms (SOC and alloy disorder), presents numerical results comparing single vs. two-electron fidelity, and discusses error sources like charge noise and phonon relaxation.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Spatial variations of the valley splitting in a quantum well present a key challenge for conveyor-mode shuttling of electron spins in Si/SiGe, giving rise to Landau-Zener-like excitations that cause leakage outside the qubit subspace. Here, we propose an unconventional two-electron qubit encoding, based on valley-singlet states, that is largely immune to Landau-Zener leakage processes. In contrast to single-electron spins, the shuttling fidelity actually improves for small valley splittings, in this case. We show that high fidelities can be achieved without applying any special procedures, such as fine-tuning of the shuttling path.

</details>

### [Visible-NIR-Frequency Hyperbolic Response in Nodal-Line Semimetal PbTaSe$_2$](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.12611v1)

**Authors:** Vivian J. Santamaria-Garcia, Morgan G. Blevins, Simo Pajovic, Carolina Orona-Navar, Svetlana V. Boriskina

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the nodal-line semimetal PbTaSe2 acts as a natural hyperbolic material in the visible and near-infrared spectrum. Unlike many known hyperbolic materials that operate in the mid-IR, this material provides a platform for high-anisotropy light-matter interaction at higher frequencies. This discovery is significant for developing unpatterned, electronic-based hyperbolic nanophotonics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details markdown="1"><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize the intrinsic hyperbolic optical response of the nodal-line semimetal PbTaSe2 in the visible to near-infrared spectral range and identify the spectral windows for Type-II hyperbolic dispersion.

**Main result.** The researchers experimentally demonstrated that PbTaSe2 exhibits robust Type-II hyperbolic behavior in the visible-NIR range, characterized by a large anisotropy parameter (|R| approx 231) and a competitive quality factor (Qmax approx 2.8).

**Method.** The work combines experimental spectroscopic ellipsometry, AFM, and reflection spectroscopy with first-principles DFT calculations and Drude-Lorentz modeling of the dielectric tensor.

**Model / system.** The physical system is PbTaSe2, a layered, non-centrosymmetric, nodal-line topological semimetal with a hexagonal crystal structure. The optical model uses a uniaxial dielectric tensor (epsilon_ab and epsilon_c) and a multilayer stack of PbTaSe2 on SiO2/Si.

**Key observables.** In-plane and out-of-plane dielectric functions (epsilon_ab, epsilon_c), optical conductivity, anisotropy parameter (|R|), and hyperbolic quality factor (Q).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Type-II hyperbolic regimes between 0.8-1.4 eV and 1.9-2.6 eV; anisotropy parameter |R| approx 231 at 0.78 eV; quality factor Qmax approx 2.8.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The crystallographic c-axis is assumed to be parallel to the substrate normal; an isotropic Drude-Lorentz model is used as an initial baseline for the optical response.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Crystal structure, SOC-induced Fermi surface, and orbital-projected band structure. Fig 2: Experimental ellipsometry data, DL model fits, and extracted dielectric functions/conductivity. Fig 3: Identification of Type-II hyperbolic regimes in experimental and DFT data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of natural hyperbolic materials, describes the material properties and experimental setup, presents the spectroscopic ellipsometry and DFT results, identifies the hyperbolic spectral windows, and concludes with the significance of the findings for nanophotonics.

</details>

<details markdown="1"><summary>Abstract</summary>

Natural hyperbolic materials offer a powerful platform for light-matter interactions by supporting highly anisotropic electromagnetic modes without the need for artificial patterning. In this work, we experimentally demonstrate that the nodal-line semimetal PbTaSe$_2$ exhibits robust hyperbolic optical behavior in the visible to near-infrared spectral range, which arises intrinsically from its anisotropic electronic structure and layered crystal symmetry. By combining first-principles calculations, ellipsometry, Drude-Lorentz modeling, and reflectance measurements, we establish a consistent experimental and theoretical picture of bulk hyperbolicity in this material. This hyperbolicity is of plasmonic origin and is characterized by a competitive quality factor ($Q_\mathrm{max} \approx 2.8$) and a very large anisotropy parameter ($|R| \approx 231$) at 0.78 eV.

</details>
